# The Receiving Line

# Chapter 1

By 5:12 the white cars were already sweating through their seams. Heat worked fast at Puerta Seco. It lifted the old urine out of floor bolts, turned restraint foam tacky, and swelled the blackwater tanks until every gauge lied a little high. Marisol Anaya stood on the ladder under Car 6 with her left knee complaining at the third rung and listened to the pump cough through the service line. After twenty years on the concession she trusted sounds before numbers. A clean pull gave a hard vacuum whine and then steadied. This one shuddered, spat back, and took a wet breath like something drowning.

"Shut it. Open the inspection cap first," she said.

The youngest yard hand on the line crew twisted the valve closed and looked at her for approval before touching the cap. He had taped his wedding ring to the inside of his glove because he was still not used to the tan line on his finger without it. Marisol did not know if the marriage had ended or if he was only keeping metal away from the machinery. She did not ask. She held the hatch light low and waited while he loosened the cap one quarter turn. Pressure sighed out, rank and warm. A clot of paper, shit, and pink vomit threads pressed against the mesh.

"There it is," Marisol said. "Don't jab it deeper. Hook it and bring it clean."

The yard hand nodded, swallowing through his mouth. He had made that mistake once, on a family car in August, and spent three hours elbow-deep in a backflow nobody was paid enough to touch. He used the bent steel hook this time and worked slow, pulling out the blockage in a slick rope that slapped the tray. The smell came up thick enough to taste. Marisol did not step back. She checked the level float herself, knuckle against the casing, then reset the sensor and watched the gauge settle from red to amber.

Amber would pass if the other two tanks held. Amber would pass if the trauma room was turned over and the dispatch-safe checklist stayed dry enough for a signature. Amber would pass because the train had a departure slot at 7:40 and RailPassage had already stacked too many bodies into the morning for anything gentler than passable.

She climbed down, wiped the underside of her wrist across her forehead, and caught the sting where old chemical damage crossed the skin inside her right arm. Her hands smelled like bleach through the gloves. They always did. The gloves only changed the temperature of it.

The yard around her was coming awake at full irritation. Forklifts beeped between stacked supply crates. Intake officers shouted lane counts over the synthetic station voice that kept repeating welcome language in two languages no one on the ground believed. Beyond the chain fence, buses were unloading the first surge from the overnight holds: men with wrists zip-linked for the walk, women carrying plastic property sacks, two children already crying hard enough to vomit. The corrugated shade over intake did not touch the air. It only striped it.

Marisol took the stairs into the center service car two at a time despite her knee and keyed the trauma room open with her passcard. Cold air should have met her there. Instead she got tired fluorescence, the sweet-metal smell of cauterized skin, and Paloma Rivera standing at the sink with one gloved hand braced flat while the other shook around a bottle of enzymatic cleaner.

Paloma had twisted her silvering braid up with a green tubing tie. A paper cup of basil sat beside the sharps bin under the vent, leaves blown half-sideways by the weak fan. She kept trying to grow things in rooms that were built to forget bodies. Most of them died. She kept bringing more.

"You're late to my funeral," Paloma said without looking up.

"I was inside a blackwater lie."

"Then you were in management."

Marisol crossed to the restraint chair. They had wiped the straps, but a dark crescent still sat in the seam where a shoulder blade had ground against vinyl. There were singed hairs in the floor drain. Someone on the night shift had used the cautery wand too close to skin again or too long. The room always held that smell after, not exactly meat and not exactly plastic, something in between that stuck to the back of the tongue until coffee turned bitter.

"Who was it?" Marisol asked.

"A man who kept sawing at his cuff until he cut himself to the tendon." Paloma capped the cleaner and flexed her right hand once, hiding the tremor by turning it into a reach for gauze. "They wanted him transportable. I made him transportable."

The line's real clinical term for mercy was transportable. He did not have to be healed, safe, or even stable. He only had to keep moving.

Marisol bent, pried the strap seam apart with a gloved thumb, and worked the cloth edge through until the blood smear came up. "You need a new chair pad."

"I need six more nurses and a room with a window."

"You can put both in the requisition."

"I did. I'm sure the requisition is being sung to sleep somewhere."

Paloma said it deadpan, which made it funnier than it deserved. She squared the gauze stack with the side of her wrist, turned the cleaner label to face front, and then hid the shaking hand by tucking it under her opposite elbow. Even the basil cup had been nudged back into the strip of air from the vent.

Marisol stripped the paper from the tray, checked the suction canister, and cleared the cautery tip from the instrument table. She had done this job herself long before she carried supervisor authority. She knew where blood hid after a rushed wipe, where foam held vomit, where the company inspectors ran fingers when they wanted to prove the line had failed. She signed the surface check on her slate only after she had leaned close enough to make sure the room smelled mainly of hospital citrus and not of burned person. It still did not pass for clean. It passed for certified.

The intake alarm went off before she pocketed the slate. Lane Three went first. Lane Two followed. A child began retching in the corridor with the hollow barking sound that meant there was nothing much left to bring up.

Paloma closed her eyes once. "They started early."

"Heat buses always do."

Marisol was already moving. She grabbed a sleeve of emesis bags, a pack of absorbent powder, and the red-seal restraint key set from the hook by the door. The key set rode against her hip when she walked, heavy and familiar. In theory it was for emergency releases only. In practice it was a reminder that every lock on the train could be opened by someone who drew a salary.

Lane Three was backed from scanner to fence by the time she got there. Intake had split into three lines under the canopy and all three were failing in their own ways. In Lane One an old man had pissed straight through the leg of his paper suit and was standing in the runoff because nobody had moved him yet. In Lane Two a little girl with a melted cartoon rabbit on her T-shirt had vomited orange electrolyte across her mother's property sack and the mother's shoes. The mother was trying to clean both with the hem of her own transport shirt while a custody officer told her to keep the line moving. In Lane Three the wristband reader kept chirping amber and spitting out duplicate binds.

"Stop sending them through wet," Marisol said to the scanner clerk. "You're smearing the chips."

"They came wet."

"Then dry the bands before you scan them."

The clerk shoved a towel dispenser toward the lane without taking his eyes off the screen. Sweat had already pasted his bangs to his forehead. Marisol stepped around him, wiped the scanner bed herself with a square of gauze, and started doing what she always did when volume got stupid: triage the process harder than the bodies.

"You." She pointed at the officer nearest the vomiting child. "Bag the clothes now or you'll be carrying the smell onto the car. If she throws again inside, Paloma will want the whole bench stripped. Do it once."

The officer opened his mouth to argue, saw her badge, and shut it.

"You two, separate the dry sacks from the wet ones. Anything wet rides outside the bedding net. If you pack damp clothes into heat, you get rot by Caldera."

Hands started moving. They usually did when Marisol spoke in that voice. She did not raise it. She only made everything sound like the step before a larger mess.

Another amber chirp sounded from the reader.

The man under the scanner was lean enough that the paper suit floated off him at the ribs. One sleeve had ridden up, showing finger-shaped bruises yellowing over newer purple along the upper arm. His hands were bound in front with soft restraints for intake, and despite the dust and the holding-cell soap his fingers were still stained blue-black at the nails as if ink had lived there longer than dirt. He tipped his face away from the scanner's light and the old white split through his left eyebrow flashed.

Marisol's body knew him before her mind allowed it. The scar was small. It had always been small. She had held a kitchen towel over that brow while he swore at fourteen and told her he did not need stitches if she would just stop pressing. She had salted pasta water that same night because there had been no money for urgent care after rent. She had forgotten the date of it and not the shape.

The scanner chirped amber again.

The man lifted his head against the custody officer's flattening palm. He did that too the way he had met everything he resented since childhood: neck gone rigid, mouth shut, refusal running through him so plain he would not offer one extra inch of himself to anyone asking badly.

Marisol looked full into Gabriel's face.

His cheekbones were sharper. He had lost weight from the jaw and neck first. There was a healing split at the corner of his mouth and the flat, mean look of somebody who had slept under strip lights and then been handled by men paid to hurry him. The scanner light changed nothing. Gabriel stood there with his father's surname nowhere visible and his own name nowhere in the room.

"Supervisor?" the scanner clerk said.

The wristband printer spat half a label and jammed.

Every intake failure demanded a decision. She could rescan him, pull him, flag a hold, make paperwork, or slow the lane. Marisol felt the entire line turn toward the point where she had stopped moving.

She picked the ruined band off the tray. "Manual bind later," she said. "Push him through on visual. Lane can't hold here."

The clerk frowned. "Procedure says-"

"Procedure says we don't stack a hundred people under broken shade for one bad chip. Mark him manual and move the line."

The custody officer shoved Gabriel forward. For one second Gabriel's eyes met hers. Recognition hit clean and ugly. Gratitude never crossed his face. Surprise never made it that far. A door slammed hard behind his eyes, one she had not known was still there.

Then he was through the gate, into the holding pen for Car 8, and the line surged to fill the space he left.

Marisol realized the ruined wristband was in her gloved hand. The band still held the chip pellet, warm from his skin. She closed her fist around it hard enough to feel the edges through latex.

The little girl in Lane Two vomited again, more liquid this time, down the front of her mother and onto the concrete. The mother made a sound Marisol did not let herself hear as anything but operational.

"Powder first," she said to the nearest attendant. "Then scrape. Don't mop puke into the drain."

She kept the lane moving. She got the child onto a cooler bench and the mother a clean shirt from stores. She rerouted two family groups away from the jammed scanner, called for extra water at the canopy, and sent one custody officer to fetch fresh foam pads before the wet ones went sweet in the heat. Her hands did exactly what they had been trained to do by repetition and necessity.

All the while the ruined band sat in her pocket like a live battery.

By 6:03 the queue had shortened from impossible to merely dangerous. The blackwater gauge on Car 6 held amber without climbing. The trauma room was cleared. Intake was recovering the shape of a system. Marisol signed three partials, denied a request for a cosmetic delay on Car 4, and stepped into the dispatch office with the excuse already built in her mouth.

The manual bind had to be corrected before seal.

That much, at least, was true.

* * *

The dispatch office had one fan that only stirred paper and one window painted over on the outside to keep detainees from reading screen glow at night. By dawn the room already smelled like dust warming under electronics. Marisol shut the door, stripped one glove off with her teeth, and laid the ruined wristband chip on the desk beside the keyboard.

The manifest system took her passcard and then asked for reason code. She selected `SCAN FAILURE / MANUAL BIND` and entered the band number. The file came back greyed out with a compliance lock across the receiving-state field.

Of course it did.

Ordinary intake cases could be corrected from route supervision if the printer swallowed a number or a chip lost signal. Receiving-state assignments were sealed higher up. There were too many treaty hooks, too much liability, and too many ways for the wrong person on the floor to notice what country had just been invented for someone.

Marisol keyed in the yard override she was not supposed to know and had learned because managers liked practical miracles while pretending miracles had no mechanics. The lock trembled, shifted, and opened one layer deeper. Her slate flashed with an incoming compliance ping before the screen fully loaded.

Teresa Ordonez appeared in the upper corner from some brighter office farther north, face already made for the day, fatigue tucked under foundation that never quite covered it. She was looking past the camera with irritation sharpened by speed, one hand sliding files aside until she found the pair of reading glasses she was forever misplacing. When she set them on, Marisol could see the bitten skin around her cuticles.

"Supervisor Anaya," Teresa said. "Why are you inside a receiving-state lock from intake?"

Marisol kept her eyes on the loading field below Teresa's image. "Manual bind failure on Lane Three. The band won't hold. I need the destination code before seal."

"You need the chip rewritten."

"The chip pellet is damaged."

"Then replace the band and move him. Caldera can reconcile."

Marisol clicked deeper while Teresa spoke, using the pause between Teresa's clauses as cover. "If the replacement throws in-car, it becomes a restraint inventory exception instead of a yard correction. I'm not sending a rolling exception over one bad bind."

Marisol heard herself keep going, adding restraint inventories and rolling exceptions, each clause fitted tight against the next until Teresa had no clean place to interrupt. Teresa stopped riffling files. She slid the reading glasses higher on her nose and listened the way management listened when procedure started protecting them from exposure. Competence bought Marisol thirty seconds.

"Thirty seconds," Teresa said. "Do not alter the file. Confirm the code, rewrite the band, and close it."

The full manifest opened.

The photograph was not the intake camera still warm from the lane. It was the arrest image from first processing, harsher light, more bruising. Gabriel looked straight into it with blood drying at one nostril and contempt already organized. The line below read:

`AVELAR, RAFAEL`

`CASE 4471-R`

`RECEIVING STATE: SANTA AVELINA`

`BASIS: ANCESTRAL ADMISSIBILITY`

`SUPPORTING PACKET: TOM AVELAR / DOCK LABOR CERTIFICATE / DECEASED`

For a second the room narrowed to the letters alone. Tom Avelar had been dead eleven years and was useful again. RailPassage had found one old dockworker certificate in the archive, found one son who had never set foot on that island, and made paperwork do the rest.

Teresa was still talking. "Is there a sanitation relevance here, Supervisor, or did intake send me a clerical vanity project before breakfast?"

Marisol clicked into the packet and saw the embedded certificate. Thomas Avelar, badge photo in his twenties, before the cough took hold in him, before the union blacklist, before he spent his last year on a couch with an oxygen bottle stuttering at his feet. Gabriel had his mouth there around the corners when he was trying not to show pain. Everything else came from her side.

"There's a relevance," Marisol said. "The printer jammed the country line."

"Then print it again."

Marisol closed the packet one layer and entered the bind rewrite field. Her thumb hovered over `CONFIRM`. If she rewrote the band cleanly and let the case disappear back into compliance lock, she would have nothing in her reach except a name she was not supposed to know. The train would seal, the file would travel, and by the time the next correction point opened Gabriel would be a moving problem owned by somebody else's schedule.

She hit `MIRROR TO YARD EXCEPTION CACHE`.

The system asked for authorization. She entered her passcard again and chose the lowest-priority sanitation code she could find, the one used when a manifest needed to follow a contaminated blanket or bloodied restraint into local chain-of-custody review. It was not meant for people. It accepted him anyway. A second access line flashed green in the corner of the screen before settling into the audit log.

There it was: one duplicate trace, one stupid, visible breadcrumb telling anybody who checked closely that Marisol Anaya had touched a case twice when once should have been enough.

"Did you hear me?" Teresa asked.

"I heard you."

"Then close the file."

Marisol rewrote the band from the chip fragment, printed the fresh strip, and let the thermal paper spit out `RAFAEL AVELAR` in clean black letters that made her want to tear the machine off the desk. She did not. She pressed the band flat against the table instead, smoothing the adhesive with two fingers until the letters became just another task requiring pressure.

"The bind is corrected," she said.

Teresa studied her through the screen a beat longer than comfort allowed. Teresa was good at that. She could make silence feel like documentation.

"Dispatch is tight this morning," Teresa said. "Do not bring me preventable holds."

"I don't."

One of Teresa's eyebrows moved. She logged the answer the way she logged every small risk, with a silent mark beside it. Then the call cut. The corner of the screen went dark.

Marisol sat with the fresh band in her hand and the mirrored file still cooling in local cache. She opened the case once more from there, quicker now, without permission, just to make sure the details had not changed because part of her still wanted the machine to admit an absurd error and hand back somebody else's son.

It did not.

Gabriel Anaya had become Rafael Avelar inside the system. Santa Avelina had accepted him by treaty hook. The dead father had been attached to the living son like cargo paperwork. If the train rolled clean and on time, the lie would cross state lines with her signature under the sanitation check.

She printed the replacement band, pocketed it, and stood.

Outside, the yard horn gave the six-thirty warning for load finalization. She had less than an hour before seal and less than that before custody officers stopped tolerating supervisor errands inside the cars.

She keyed the door open and went back into the heat.

* * *

Car 8 sat on the east side of the consist, nearest the loading cages and furthest from the yard office, which was one reason intake liked it for overflow. Another reason was that nobody with authority visited Car 8 unless they had to. It was where the line stuffed the mistakes that volume made: families split and recombined wrong at the fence, single adults moved beside minors because the manifest had outrun the bodies, people too sick for general restraint and too healthy for Paloma to requisition. The white paint around the door seam had bubbled from old heat and old bleach. Rust showed through like dried tea.

Marisol crossed the ballast with the replacement band in one pocket and the red-seal keys hitting her hip. The platform was busy enough to cover purpose. Officers were counting heads against slates. Two attendants hauled wet bedding into a mesh bin. Someone farther down the consist was hosing vomit off a stepwell and sending diluted orange back onto the gravel.

At the foot of Car 8, a young custody officer with a dinosaur bandage wrapped around his thumb put out an arm when he saw her coming. "Seals in eight."

"Then I'm early," Marisol said. "Manual bind correction on Lane Three."

"Do it fast."

He stepped aside. He could not have been more than twenty-three. There was sunscreen missed at both ears, bright red against his neck. He smelled of mint gum and stress sweat. Marisol had supervised men like him for years, boys paid to become hard before their faces settled.

Inside, the air sat on the skin like a damp cloth left in an engine room. The overhead fans turned and only redistributed breath. Families had been assigned to the near bunks, single men to the center benches, overflow women and older children toward the rear partitions where sight lines were worst. People had already begun the small irreversible work of transport: tucking property under thighs, rationing water by the sip, deciding who would get the window slit. A baby cried at the pitch of actual pain. Somebody nearer the back was coughing up something thick.

Marisol could read a car in one glance the way other people read weather. Too much moisture near the floor meant a leak or a child who had not said he needed a bucket. Too much silence in one section meant somebody hurt worse than the guards had logged. She took all of it in while moving straight for the middle bench where the manual-bind list had put `4471-R`.

Gabriel sat with his wrists looped to a waist chain and his shoulders held carefully away from the seatback. That meant rib pain, or bruises worse than she had seen at intake. The paper suit had been traded for a transport shirt that did not fit his chest and made him look even leaner. The fresh split at his mouth had opened again. Someone had wiped the blood and missed the corner.

He saw her before she stopped in front of him.

Recognition moved through his face so briefly that anyone else would have missed it. His eyes sharpened. His jaw set. Something older and more private crossed there, and then it was gone behind a stranger's regard so flat it felt practiced.

Marisol kept her own voice low. "I had to rebind the chip."

He did not look at the band in her hand. He looked at the badge clipped to her shirt, then at the keys on her belt, then back to her face. He took inventory first. He had always been his father's child in that.

"Your ribs?" she asked.

"You checking cargo condition?"

The line hit clean under her breastbone. Gabriel had always known where to put the blade.

The dinosaur-bandage officer leaned in from the aisle. "Problem?"

"No problem," Marisol said before Gabriel could answer. She bent as if only to inspect the old band, close enough to smell dried sweat and the bitter holding-cell soap still trapped in his collar. Under it there was another smell, metallic and stale, the smell of a night in restraints. She saw the deepening shadow over his left side where fingers or a baton had landed earlier.

"Listen to me," she said, barely moving her mouth. "When med rounds come through, tell them it hurts to draw breath. Don't be proud today."

His eyes flicked once to hers, and for a second she thought the old reflex might survive in him somewhere, the part that had once handed her an inhaler without argument because air was air and pride could wait.

Then he straightened against the chain and spoke loud enough for the officer and half the bench to hear.

"My name's Rafael Avelar," he said. "If you need the plastic to tell you that, read it slower."

Several heads turned.

Marisol felt the room tilt around a still point. He had not called her mother. He had not even called her liar. He had taken the false name and used it as a wall.

"Keep your voice down," she said.

"Why." His mouth moved at the cut and split it wider. "You don't know me."

The officer snorted as if detainee attitude belonged to weather and not to history. "Band on and move, Supervisor."

Marisol took Gabriel's wrist. His skin was hotter than it should have been. The bruising around the restraint line was fresh enough to feel slightly raised under her thumb. When she wrapped the new band over the old one, he did not pull away. He let her touch him exactly long enough to know the permission meant nothing.

She fastened the adhesive and kept hold of his arm one second longer than procedure required. "When they ask destination, don't sign anything extra."

That one sentence was all she had to offer: bad advice, thin advice, the kind a woman gave when she controlled seals and tanks and had no control at all over what mattered.

Gabriel looked at her hand on his wrist until she let go.

"You should know that already," he said.

He kept this one low, pitched for her alone.

Her hand closed again before she told it to stop. Bruised heat and the raised welt from the restraint bit into her palm; the waist chain gave one hard click. She let go so fast her knuckles struck the bench rail. The sting flashed up her arm and brought him back in one ugly shard: Gabriel in her apartment doorway with a duffel bag on one shoulder, twisting free while something burned on the stove behind her.

The seal timer chirped from the door panel.

"Out," the officer said. "We're closing."

Marisol stepped back. In the next bunk a mother was wetting a rag from her own drinking bottle to wipe sour milk off a toddler's chin. Across the aisle an old man kept one hand over a paper bag as if it were money. A teenage boy at the rear partition was mouthing the station announcements half a beat behind the speaker, either mocking it or teaching himself the words.

This was what Car 8 always held: overflow, misfit categories, bodies the system had sorted fast and badly and then declared organized because the doors could still shut.

Gabriel had turned his face away by the time she reached the threshold.

The officer pulled the door in. Gasket met frame. The latch dogs bit one by one with the same sequence Marisol had heard for years and never once mistaken for anything human. She waited for the seal light to go from red to green. It did.

On the platform outside, load-final horns were sounding up and down the consist. She checked them automatically: Car 3 green, Car 4 green, Car 6 still amber on blackwater but holding, Car 8 green, trauma room clear, dispatch window intact. The train was becoming a fact, and it was her fact, built this morning by signatures, wipes, thresholds, bags changed in time, straps dried enough to pass inspection, one manual bind corrected instead of challenged.

Her slate buzzed with the departure matrix: Southbound Meridian West, Puerta Seco release 7:40, status `ON SCHEDULE`.

She stood with the ballast crunching under her shoes and looked through the narrow reinforced slit in Car 8's wall. She could not see Gabriel from there, only moving shadows, the blur of a child's hand, one face turning and gone.

The duplicate case sat in local cache under a false sanitation code. The fresh band on Gabriel's wrist said Rafael Avelar. Tom Avelar's dead certificate was doing live work in the system. Teresa had warned against preventable holds. The blackwater alarm might still climb if the heat kept up. The trauma room might fill again before noon. The entire line was sliding toward departure on the rails Marisol knew better than any room she had ever called home.

The worst part was that none of the train's numbers cared.

Another horn sounded. The board dropped to final two-minute.

Marisol touched the passcard clipped to her breast pocket as if the plastic might tell her which part of the morning still belonged to her. The plastic came away slick under her thumb. She had spent half her life learning how to keep this corridor dispatchable, how to make blood into paperwork and waste into thresholds and panic into a schedule nobody above her had to smell.

Now her son was inside the cleaned car under another man's country, and the train was still on time.

Medical could still interrupt a clean departure. It could not do it for the lie in his file or for the dead man's certificate. It could do it for pain, for breath, for a body that refused to stay transportable. Marisol turned toward the service car and started walking. By the time she hit the stairs, heat complaints were already backing up into the trauma-room hall.


# Chapter 2

The trauma room had no room in it.

Bay One held a boy no older than ten with restraint foam dried white at the corners of his mouth and one shoe missing. Bay Two held an old man on oxygen, his chest lifting shallow and fast under a paper gown that had gone damp clear through. In Bay Three a woman from lane six kept pressure on her scalp with a wad of towels that had once been white and now looked like weak tea. The floor drain under the sink burped pink water every time somebody rinsed an instrument tray. Summer heat had made the plastic mattress covers tacky. The overhead lights buzzed hard enough to feel in the back teeth.

Marisol stopped in the doorway and took stock the way she always did: what still moved, what could be cleared, what would become a report if it got any worse. A trauma-room runner named Celia Velez was counting sterile packs under her breath, tapping each one with a fingernail striped blue from cheap polish she had not had time to remove. Celia had wrapped pink kinesiology tape around her left wrist, and the tape had gone gray at the edges from powder and sweat.

"Not now," Celia said without looking up. "If this is about another heat complaint, they can sweat where they're standing."

"Case 4471-R," Marisol said. "I need him in here."

Celia looked up then. "You and everybody else."

Marisol stepped farther into the room. The air hit her face cold from the wall unit nearest the oxygen bay, but the cold never reached the hall. It only sat in this room with the disinfectant and the old iron smell, pretending there was a line somewhere between treatment and transport. She could hear the intake speakers outside repeating lane assignments in two languages, bright synthetic politeness over the drag of boots and the wet coughs from holding.

"Upper-arm restraint bruising, right rib side, guarding when he twists," she said. "If they cracked a lower rib into his liver and he decompensates after seal, it lands in your log and mine."

Celia gave her a long look. "You saw guarding or you want guarding."

Marisol did not answer that. She did not have time to make it sound less personal than it was. Gabriel was already in Car Eight under somebody else's country code. Every minute he stayed in that steel box the hatch got closer to sealed, the file got closer to hard. Quiet corrections happened before steel doors shut and a train became timetable instead of yard.

"Clear me a table," she said.

"I don't have a table."

"Then clear me a wall and a tray."

Celia's mouth tightened into the look she got when the math turned ugly: who got seen, who waited, which body might still be walking when the southbound cars started to move. She jerked her chin at the rolling stainless cart by the sink. "Three minutes, maybe four. If he vomits blood I need to know whether it was before or after you got sentimental."

Marisol let that pass. The word sentimental had no business in the room, but she had already spent the authority that would have let her challenge it cleanly. She swiped her supervisor passcard over the trauma-room reader and felt the lock click. The system would show she had opened a medical bay against intake load. It was another trace in the system, another reason somebody could later ask why.

At Car Eight's service spur the air was ten degrees hotter. White paint on the car wall had blistered along the seam beside the fold-down transfer bench, and the rust underneath showed through in a line as fine as a split lip. Officer Baca stood by the open hatch with a short-link restraint line hooked to one fist and a clear property bag looped over the other wrist. He kept worrying the pale groove beneath his wedding ring with his thumb, turning the band half around and back every time the service spur backed up. A nicotine patch had started to peel from the side of his neck.

Gabriel sat on the transfer bench where Baca had parked him after hauling him back out of Car Eight for medical. His shoulders were rounded forward against the chain clipped at his waist, but his head was up. He had always done that when he was trying not to give somebody the satisfaction of seeing pain land. The scar through his left eyebrow cut white through sweat and dirt. His fingers were still dark with ink at the nails as if he had been writing until the hands came down on him.

Marisol took in the rest in pieces because if she took it all at once she would lose the use of her own face. The shirt was twisted half out of his waistband. One sleeve had stretched where somebody had jerked him by it. Fresh bruising spread under the cloth from the side of his ribs toward his back, a low dark bloom already turning the color of bad plums. Thumb marks sat high on both upper arms, distinct enough to count.

Baca saw where she was looking. "He's ambulatory."

"He's going to trauma," Marisol said.

"For what."

"For not cracking open in my car."

Baca gave a short laugh that was not amusement. "You're pulling triage for a bruise now?"

Gabriel rolled one shoulder against the chain as if testing how much room Baca had left him. The movement pulled at his side, but he let the pain show just long enough to tell her he knew exactly what she was doing.

"I'm fine," he said. His voice was rough from heat and holding, but it still had that dry edge in it, the one that used to come out when he was thirteen and already knew which adult in a room was full of shit. "Load somebody who still wants your help."

Marisol stepped in close before Baca could take that as settled. She put two fingers under the hem of Gabriel's shirt and lifted. Heat came off his skin. The bruise on the right side was worse up close, the shape wide and ugly, with a yellowing edge where an earlier hit had already started to age under the new one. She set her hand against the lower ribs and pressed.

He sucked air hard through his teeth. It was enough.

"Tender," she said. "Right side. Possible fracture, possible liver bleed. He's not loading until med clears him."

Gabriel stared at her with something almost like contemptuous admiration, as if he could see every tool she was reaching for and hated that she still knew how to use them.

Baca shifted his grip on the chain. "You palpated him."

"That's what hands are for."

"You want Celia to sign that?"

"I want you to explain to compliance why you sent a possible internal upstairs because dispatch was making you nervous."

The property bag knocked against Baca's thigh when he moved. Inside it lay the usual intake subtraction: belt, shoelaces, folded papers, a cheap pen snapped in half at the clip. Beside them sat a small black recorder with a cracked screen and a mesh speaker grilled silver. Yellow evidence tape crossed it in an X. A sticker label read `UNDECLARED DIGITAL STORAGE` above the printed alias `RAFAEL AVELAR`.

For one second the room narrowed to that object. It looked old, worn smooth at the edges by a thumb, and somebody had wrapped one corner in clear tape to keep the casing from splitting before the seizure. The kind of repair people made to something they could not afford to lose because what lived inside mattered more than the machine.

Gabriel saw her see it. The line of his jaw changed.

"Property stays with custody," Baca said.

"Then custody comes with him," Marisol said.

Celia pushed through the lane gate with a pulse clip and a rolling tray, muttering multiplication tables under her breath as she counted syringes. When she saw Gabriel's side she stopped muttering.

"Well," she said. "That's more than a bruise."

"Thank you," Marisol said.

Celia clipped the pulse sensor to Gabriel's finger. "Don't thank me yet. If he starts dropping pressure in transit, I get to be right in writing."

Baca did not want writing. Nobody on the line wanted writing unless it could be pointed at somebody lower. He blew air through his nose, peeled the chain from the bench ring, and jerked his head toward the trauma corridor.

"Four minutes," he said. "Then he's back on Car Eight unless he opens up on your floor."

"Move," Marisol said.

Gabriel got to his feet slower than pride wanted and steadier than pain allowed. The short restraint chain ran from his waist to cuffed wrists, forcing a cramped half-balance that made every step look like obedience even when it was not. Marisol took the corridor side, Baca the wall side. Celia rolled the tray ahead of them. Intake noise thinned as they crossed the threshold into the medical wing and became smaller sounds: the squeak of the tray wheel with a flat spot, the air unit knocking once before it caught, a man behind one of the curtains begging somebody not to send his daughter back into the bright.

Gabriel's shoulder brushed Marisol's arm on the turn, quick and accidental. Pride kept his weight off her; he would have gone to the floor first.

In the trauma room Celia cleared the stainless cart with one sweep of a forearm, sending a stack of chart sleeves skidding to the floor. Baca hung the property bag from the IV pole hook and kept his hand on the chain while Gabriel sat. A custody radio clipped high on Baca's vest kept coughing lane codes into his left ear, and every few seconds he half-turned toward the corridor to catch the words over the wall unit and the old man's oxygen hiss. Up close, the ink on Gabriel's fingers had bled into the cuticles. There was dried blood in one ear. His lower lip had been split inside, not out; she could tell by the way he kept tonguing one side of his mouth.

Celia cut the shirt seam with blunt scissors and peeled the cloth back. The bruise along the ribs had the wide flat shape of baton work, not a stumble, and the skin above it was abraded raw where the restraint strap had ridden up and sawed during transport from the holding cage.

"Jesus," Celia said softly.

"Don't say that like he's unusual," Gabriel said.

Celia met his eyes. "You're not unusual. That's the problem."

She ran practiced fingers along the rib edge, gentler than Marisol had been. Gabriel went still in a different way then, the stillness of somebody bracing not against the pain itself but against giving strangers the sound of it.

"Nothing floating," Celia said. "Maybe a hairline. Maybe just deep bruising. He can breathe?"

"Can you?" Marisol asked before she could stop herself.

Gabriel turned his head toward her. "I could before you put on a show."

Celia straightened. Her gaze moved once between them and settled nowhere. "I need compression wrap and the portable scan from Bay Two. If either of you touches that property bag, I didn't see it and I'll deny I even know how hooks work."

"Celia," Baca said.

"Then keep staring at it," she snapped. "I need the machine."

She shoved the tray against the wall and disappeared through the curtain into the next bay. Baca stayed at the door with one boot planted against the threshold and his head angled toward the corridor, thumbing the radio when it spat back another custody count. The wall unit rattled, the oxygen mask hissed, and anything said low in the room had to fight its way through both. The recorder hung between Marisol and Gabriel at eye level, slowly turning on the IV hook from the draft of the wall unit.

* * *

He watched it instead of her.

"You should have let the shirt stay on," he said.

Marisol folded the cut edge of fabric over once, because her hands needed a task before they did something stupid like reach for his face. "I needed you in here."

"You needed me out of sight."

"Same thing for four minutes."

He laughed once, a dry scrape of sound that hurt him enough to make him stop.

Marisol forced herself into the voice she used when blackwater alarms tripped and three crews started shouting at once. "Listen to me. I can flag the intake injury, reopen the custody note, put you in observation. If I can get your file back into yard review before they lock the car list, I may be able to unwind the receiving-state assignment."

Gabriel finally looked at her full on. Heat had hollowed his face, sharpened it. Underfed, yes, but more than that: stripped down to the things he would not surrender. Tom's mouth when cornered. Her own eyes when she had been young enough to believe anger could feed a person.

"You may," he said. "That's a beautiful plan."

"Don't start."

"You started. Back there, with the ribs."

Marisol kept her voice down because Baca was six feet away with his radio to his ear, and because if she let it rise she would hear the plea inside it. "If I put you back on Car Eight, they seal the hatch. Once they seal it, I lose half the options."

"What options."

"Delay. Review. Recode. A hold long enough to put hands on the paperwork."

He tipped his chin toward the hanging property bag. "And that."

She followed the look though she did not need to. "I saw it."

"Did you."

"What's on it?"

For the first time something in him shifted beyond fury. His attention tightened on her, precise and unwilling, as if he were deciding whether she had earned even that answer.

"Voices," he said. "Forty-seven hours, if they did not wipe it already. Testimony, field notes, checkpoint sounds, berth calls, destination codes read wrong and then read again like saying them twice made them legal. A woman from Arroyo Norte listing everything they took out of her pockets because she knew nobody would log it honestly. Two brothers from the salt camps who could tell which car they were on by the pitch in the floor when the blackwater tank shifted. A man who got assigned to Santa Avelina because the officer liked the sound of his dead grandfather's surname. Things with dates on them. Things with names."

The recorder turned once on the hook, its yellow tape catching the fluorescent light.

Marisol heard herself ask, "Why were you carrying it here."

"Because that's where the trains are."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer you get."

She took one step closer. "Gabriel."

He flinched at the name the way people flinched from cold water, not because it hurt more than heat but because it made the skin honest. His eyes cut to Baca and back.

"Don't do that in here," he said quietly.

Marisol felt the chemical burn scar on the inside of her wrist go tight as she curled her hand. "Do what."

"Use my name like it buys you something."

The oxygen mask in Bay Two hissed. Somebody dropped a metal tray behind the curtain and swore. The room carried on being exactly what it was: a place where bodies were patched just enough to move on schedule.

"I'm trying to get you off that schedule," she said.

"Quietly."

"Yes. Quietly, if possible."

Gabriel lifted his cuffed hands a few inches and let the short chain strike the stainless cart. The clink cut through the oxygen hiss and the wall unit, a small deliberate sound that made the whole room feel like evidence.

"That's the whole religion here," he said at last. "Quietly. Quiet loading, quiet bruises, quiet mistakes. Quiet enough that the platform still smells like lemon and bleach by morning and everybody can call it transport."

Marisol swallowed whatever first answer came up because it would have been the wrong one, something defensive and small. "I keep people alive on that train."

"For transfer."

"For the night. For the next stop. Sometimes that matters."

"I know it matters. I'm not stupid." He nodded toward the drain in the floor. "That's why this works. They hit people in the lane, and then people like you know exactly how much blood counts as an incident and how much just goes down there with disinfectant."

The words landed harder because he said them without heat, each one set down like an entry in a ledger.

Marisol heard the old line rise in her head anyway, the one she had lived by so long it had worn a groove through her thinking: somebody would do this work. Somebody would keep the cars from turning into sealed rot. Somebody would clean the foam, clear the drains, get water into the right hands, keep a child with fever from riding three counties with nobody looking in. She could have said all of it. She had said versions of it to herself for twenty years while scrubbing other people's terror off surfaces designed to show no residue.

She did not say it, because in this room with his shirt cut open and his recorder hanging in a property bag between them it sounded like what it was. A worker's justification grown smooth from overuse.

"I know what the line is," she said.

"Do you."

"Don't mistake me for them."

That brought the heat back into him. It sharpened his mouth.

"Why shouldn't I." He jerked his cuffed hands as far as the short chain allowed. "They leave marks on people. You make the marks transportable. That's not the same job, maybe, but it's the same route."

She took the blow and kept standing because there was nowhere to set it down. "You think I taught them how to hurt you."

"No." His smile then was brief and mean and exhausted. "I think you taught them how not to see it as a problem once they had."

Baca shifted at the door. Marisol lowered her voice further.

"If I can get you moved into observation, I can buy time."

"For who."

"For you."

"No."

The word was immediate. Clean.

Marisol blinked. "You don't even know what I can do yet."

"I know enough."

"You don't know what I can move on this line."

"Then hear me clearer." He leaned forward despite the pull in his side, and now the pain showed because he no longer cared what it cost him to show it. "You don't pull me out by myself and call that fixing anything. You don't get to make me your private exception so you can go back upstairs and tell yourself the machine slipped once."

"That's not what this is."

"That's exactly what this is."

"You'd rather stay on the manifest."

"I'd rather the thing that happened to me have a name bigger than my file."

There it was, stripped of any request for gratitude or revenge. He was planting himself where she could not turn him into a private exception.

Marisol heard how the speakers outside kept repeating intake instructions in the same bright, clipped voice no matter who cried over them. She heard a gurney wheel click each turn because somebody had taped the hub instead of replacing it. Sound was how Gabriel had always sorted a room. As a child he had known her key in the apartment lock from the third-floor landing. He had known his father's cough from two blocks away. Now he was talking about destination codes and floor pitch and voices that would vanish if the recorder vanished, and the pattern was so old it made something cramped and ugly move under her ribs.

"Who are these people," she asked.

"People you transport."

"Don't be clever with me."

"I'm not being clever. That's the whole point." He tipped his head toward the recorder again. "They were on the line before me. Some got moved south. Some got deferred. Some just came apart in the paperwork after a medical hold and then nobody could find where the body went. I was carrying copies. Backups. Whatever word makes you feel less panicked."

His breathing had gone shallow with the talking. Celia had been right about the bruise. Deep enough to make speech expensive.

Marisol found herself looking at the strap abrasion crossing his side. The skin was torn in two places where the restraint had rubbed it raw. If she wet gauze and laid it there it would sting first, then cool. The knowledge rose in her with the old stupid force of motherhood, immediate and practical and useless at the scale of the room.

"Who picked you up," she said.

"Checkpoint team east of the yard."

"Why were the papers on you."

He gave her a look that said she was skipping over the actual problem on purpose. He was right. She was.

"Because I had them," he said.

"Gabriel."

"Because I had them."

She wanted to shake him then, not for insolence but for the terrible resemblance between his answer and the kind of answer she herself would have given at twenty-seven if somebody with authority asked for the wrong truth. She settled for gripping the edge of the stainless cart until the cold bit her palm.

"If I can't fix the paperwork in the yard," she said, "I can still alter where you sleep tonight. I can route you into medical observation, I can get a fresh scan, I can stop them from resealing Car Eight with you in it for this leg."

"And then what."

"Then I keep moving."

"By yourself."

"By whoever I need."

Baca swore under his breath when the radio cracked alive again, turned fully toward the corridor, and pressed two fingers to the earpiece while somebody barked for lane counts. Gabriel used the opening at once, leaning over the stainless cart until the short chain drew taut. For those few seconds Baca was all back and profile, listening away from them.

Gabriel leaned toward her over the stainless cart and kept his voice inside the machine hum. "If you move me alone, I tell the first officer who asks exactly why Supervisor Anaya suddenly cares about Case 4471-R. I tell them your real name and mine. I tell them you recognized me back in intake this morning and have been bending procedure since."

Marisol caught the edge of the stainless cart as the room lurched in her like a blackwater tank shifting too fast on a grade. By the time her stomach caught up, her mind was already counting the damage.

"You'd burn your own chance to get clear."

"Clear to where." He let out a short breath that might have become a laugh if his ribs had allowed it. "I'm already on the train."

She kept one hand locked on the cart and let the cold bite her palm while memory misfired anyway: the small boy who used to sleep with a cassette recorder under his pillow because he liked to tape the night trains two miles off and play them back slower in the morning. The boy was gone. Or rather he was present in the exact wrong way, grown into a man whose habits had sharpened instead of softened. The same attention to sound. The same refusal to let adults rename what he had plainly heard.

"What do you want from me," she asked.

"Not that."

"Say it plain."

He licked the split inside his lip and winced. "If there's anything left in you that isn't company, don't save me quiet. Get that recorder out. Keep the names from being scrubbed with the rest. If you can do more than that, prove it somewhere I can see."

There was no room in the sentence for mother. That was the point. He was setting terms the way he would have set them with any compromised adult asking for trust she had not earned.

Marisol opened her mouth and found nothing fit to put in it. Sorry was too small. I love you was impossible here, obscene almost, with Baca at the door and the alias tag on the recorder and the bruise opening purple under the room's bad light. The best she could summon was process.

"If I touch property chain-of-custody," she said, "they audit the room."

"They should."

"You don't know what that costs."

"I know what this costs."

He raised his cuffed hands again, a fraction, enough to indicate not just himself but the room, the line, the whole clean apparatus of damage control around them. Then he let them fall back against the short chain with a clink that sounded indecently small.

Celia came back through the curtain carrying a compression wrap and the portable scanner under one arm. Sweat had darkened her scrub top between the shoulder blades. She took in the silence first, then the distance between them, then Gabriel's face.

"Time is up," Baca said after he killed the radio, relieved now that the matter could return to ordinary violence and ordinary paperwork.

Celia set the scanner on the cart, thumbed it awake, and squirted a cold stripe of gel under Gabriel's right ribs before he could brace against anything but pain. The probe clicked once against bone. Gray shapes opened and closed across the small screen.

"Hold still," she said.

Gabriel bared his teeth and did.

Celia ran the probe along the lower rib edge, then higher, then back again until she trusted what she was seeing. "No free fluid that I can see. Nothing obvious around the liver. Maybe a hairline rib, maybe muscle torn deep. This rig won't rule a slow bleed, and it doesn't buy me an observation bed without a physician read."

Marisol did not move.

"Supervisor," Baca said.

Gabriel kept his eyes on her. Whatever else he had learned since she last had any right to ask, he had learned not to rescue the person with power from the consequences of using it.

"Do your note," Marisol said to Celia.

Celia wiped the gel away with a wad of gauze, then wrapped the ribs tight enough to support and loose enough to pass for humane. Gabriel shut his mouth on the pain this time. When Celia lifted the property bag to note the seized electronics in the medical addendum, the recorder swung close enough for Marisol to read every scratch and strip of tape on the casing. The alias label flashed under the fluorescents. Forty-seven hours, he had said, voices with dates on them. The bag spun once from Celia's grip and settled.

"Observation?" Baca asked.

The question hung there, simple and lethal. If she said yes, she would need more than what the room now held. The portable scan had shown no free fluid around the liver and nothing dramatic enough for Celia to own a hold. To force observation Marisol would need a doctor, a stronger chart, and a fight she could not hide. Baca would log the deviation. Celia would resent it. Gabriel would burn the secret on contact. If she said no, he went back onto Car Eight bruised, wrapped, and carrying the only thing in the room that might outlast both of them in a property bag marked for seizure.

Marisol looked at her son. He gave the smallest shake of his head.

"Cleared for monitored loading," she said.

The words came out in her public voice, clipped and exact. She hated how competent she sounded.

Baca nodded as if he had known all along this would settle into sense. He rehooked the short chain, took the property bag, and tugged Gabriel to his feet. Gabriel stood carefully, protecting the right side now that the wrap had made the pain more legible instead of less.

At the threshold he stopped and turned back just enough to make Marisol meet his eyes one last time.

"The guards leave bruises," he said. "You built the part that lets everybody else call them manageable."

Baca jerked the chain. Gabriel did not look at her again.

Celia stripped the paper backing from another wrap with her teeth. "Next time," she said without malice, tired almost to transparency, "bring me a body you actually mean to keep."

Then she was gone behind the curtain and the room reclaimed itself: oxygen hiss, cart wheel squeak, fluorescent buzz, the floor drain swallowing diluted blood one pink mouthful at a time.

Marisol stood alone beside the empty stainless cart until the intake speaker outside repeated the final southbound staging call. Her passcard trace was in the system now. Baca had seen too much urgency. Celia had seen more than that. Gabriel had refused the one version of mercy she knew how to offer and named the job beneath the job with a precision she could not scrub down into clerical language.

When she stepped back into the corridor, the heat hit her full in the face. At the far end Baca turned Gabriel back toward Car Eight's loading gate, the property bag with the recorder knocking lightly against his knee as if it held nothing heavier than laces and a broken pen. Marisol watched them disappear into the white-painted passage where rust kept showing through no matter how often the crews came at it with bleach. By the time the layover meal opened at Caldera Junction, she'd be expected upstairs with a tray in her hands and nothing visible on her face. The sentence she had been using to keep herself functional since dawn finally failed.

This was not a mistake inside the line.


# Chapter 3

The cafeteria at Caldera Junction smelled of fryer grease, wet denim, and the thin ammoniac sweetness that lifted off a crew after a full intake day. Southbound staging had already taken Gabriel out of Marisol's sight before the layover meal opened. She carried her tray with both hands and kept her face arranged for anyone who looked up. Her left knee had gone wooden on the metal stairs, and the stew trembled in its compartment like something barely set. The windows facing the commuter platform were filmed white from old detergent. Beyond them, on the maintenance track, the deportation cars sat in their patient row with the white paint failing at the seams. On the other side of the glass a commuter train slid in clean and silver, doors opening for people who still got to decide where they were going.

She took the corner table by the dead vending machine for the view of both doors. The route had trained that into her: clock the exits, sit where you could see who came through. Her hair was pinned so tight the skin at her temples ached. Bleach had dried the backs of her hands into a fine white nap, except over the old burn on her right wrist, where the skin never roughened evenly again. She set down the tray, sat carefully, and listened to the room: forks on plastic, boots dragging, a cook cursing in the steam line, the constant low vibration from the yard under all of it. The whole junction seemed built to remind a body that metal did not rest merely because people did.

Paloma arrived first, compact and square in her scrubs under an unbuttoned work shirt, silver braid darkened with sweat down her back. She carried her own tray in one hand and two little rinse cups in the other, each holding a basil cutting in water gone green at the stems. She set the cups in the strip of evening light on the table before she sat, more carefully than she set down the food.

"If I leave them in the dorm," she said, "those idiots will strip them for noodles."

Marisol looked at the cups. "You've brought the whole windowsill with you."

"I've got four survivors. That's not a windowsill. That's stubbornness."

Ivo dropped into the third chair with the rolling gait Marisol had known for years, his weight shifting half a beat ahead of every step, ready for the floor to betray him. He had grease under the nails no brush ever reached, and the small burn scar high on his neck had gone pink in the heat. A folded maintenance docket and an opened county envelope were crammed together in his coverall pocket. He slid a heel out of one boot under the table and made a face like a man removing a nail from lumber.

"What's dying?" he said.

"Everything on your tray," Paloma said. "Also those cuttings if the dorm boys find them."

Ivo looked into the stew. "No, those are safe. Nothing grows in that."

Paloma snorted. Marisol almost did. The laugh stayed shallow in her chest. Gabriel's face kept rising underneath every ordinary thing that tried to sit on top of it: the old scar through his eyebrow, the bruising under one eye already yellowing toward green, the clear property bag hanging from a hook in intake with the recorder inside it like an organ sealed for transport.

Paloma noticed what Marisol had not touched before Marisol did. "Eat while it's hot."

"It's not hot," Ivo said. "It's historically warm."

"Eat while it's historically warm, then."

Marisol broke the skin of the stew with her spoon. Steam rose carrying onion, powdered stock, and something that tried to be beef and never had the muscle for it.

"You stole those from the trauma room?" Marisol asked, nodding at the cups.

"I propagated them from the trauma room," Paloma said. "Stole is for narcotics and payroll."

"Same building," Ivo said.

He took the folded maintenance docket from his pocket and used it to level the bad leg of the table. His phone buzzed almost at once. He glanced at the screen, and the lines around his mouth eased before he could stop them.

"Lina sent me a picture from the school yard." He dug his phone from his breast pocket, thumbed to a blurred image, and set it between the cups. "Wanted to know if it's a kestrel."

Paloma leaned over. "That's a pigeon."

"A talented pigeon," Ivo said.

"A sick pigeon," Paloma said. "Look at the feathers."

He glanced at Marisol. "Tell her."

Marisol looked down at the screen because looking at a bird was easier than looking at anything with paperwork attached to it. The photo showed a lump of gray-brown feathers on a chain-link fence, breast puffed against the heat. In the corner a child had caught half her own thumb.

"Pigeon," she said. "Kestrels don't sit like that."

"Thank you," Paloma said.

Ivo took back the phone with exaggerated hurt. "My own table. Two women against me."

"Your daughter asked a question," Paloma said. "Now you can answer it without lying."

"She likes the hawk answer better."

"Children like candy better than soup too," Paloma said. "That doesn't make soup a conspiracy."

"This soup is absolutely a conspiracy."

The room around them kept up its own tired commerce. A group from the refit bay argued about coolant leaks. Someone at the next table was filing a torn nail with the edge of a keycard. A ceiling fan chopped hot air into slightly differently shaped hot air. Over the cafeteria speakers a synthetic voice announced the commuter departure for Platform C in English and Spanish, clipped and pleasant, a voice made for departure times and not for restraint foam slick with vomit, not for children in the white cars on the maintenance line.

Marisol ate under their eyes. In a room where worry was contagious, untouched food announced it too loudly. The spoon clicked once against her teeth. She kept seeing Gabriel in the trauma room, shirt lifted, ribs mapped under skin that had gone mean and lean from transport food. He had always been thin. Even as a boy he had looked built from long twine and grievance. It was the bruises that would not leave her alone. They had shape. She knew from one glance what buckle had made one mark and what grip had made the other. She knew too much about what left color on a body and how long each color took to clear.

Paloma had begun rubbing her right hand with the heel of the left, pressing the tremor down into the knuckles where it might pass for strain. The movement was small, practiced, almost private.

"You're favoring the knee again," she said without looking up.

"Everybody's favoring something," Marisol said.

"That wasn't the question."

Ivo pulled his boot back on and stamped his heel once. "My right foot feels like somebody hid a bolt head in the sole."

"That's because your fascia is shot and you buy boots like a martyr," Paloma said.

"I buy boots like a man with court arrears."

She lifted her eyes. "How much left?"

"Too much to be dignified, not enough to be dramatic."

"That's not a number."

"Six-eight, last time they mailed me something threatening."

Paloma made a face that mixed pity with diagnosis. "And you spent part of your layover at the pension office anyway."

"Had to. They lost a quarter again."

"A quarter of what?"

"A quarter year. On paper I apparently took three months off in 2037 to enjoy myself."

Paloma smiled. "Did you?"

"If I'd done that I wouldn't still be here eating a gravy crime under fluorescent light."

Marisol said, "It was the axle seizure in spring. They booked the shutdown wrong."

Both of them looked at her. The answer had come too fast, too exact. She could hear it herself, the way stress turned her into a woman who corrected labels while the room burned.

Ivo tore bread. "See, that's why I sit with you. I've got a memory problem and you were born mad at a ledger."

Paloma studied Marisol's face a beat longer than she needed to. "What did you come in here carrying?"

The question was soft. It went nowhere soft inside Marisol. She set down the spoon, wiped her mouth with the paper napkin, and bought herself the two seconds it took to lay a smaller lie over the larger one.

"A manifest mismatch," she said. "One of intake's scans bounced twice and came through with the wrong property count. If I don't fix it before southbound, it'll breed another six forms."

Paloma kept watching her. "Property count doesn't put that look on you."

"Blackwater alarm did," Marisol said. "Two of them. Car Nine burped back through the rear stack and the new kid on hose detail panicked and kinked the line. I spent an hour ankle-deep in somebody else's breakfast. There. Better story?"

It was too detailed, which made it more believable to anyone who did not know her stress well. Paloma knew it. Paloma also knew when not to pry where a body had already braced.

"Fine," she said. "Then after this you need heat on the knee and six hours horizontal."

"You prescribing horizontal now?"

"For workers? Constantly. Nobody listens."

Ivo pointed with his spoon. "See, that's why medics go mean. You keep giving orders to people who refuse repair."

"Mechanics don't refuse repair?"

"Mechanics postpone repair until catastrophic failure. It's a more honorable trade."

That got a real laugh out of Paloma, brief and low. It bent her over the tray for a second and showed the tiredness in her neck. Marisol held on to the sound. Keeping anything ordinary in place had become difficult work. Paloma smelling of camphor and disinfectant, Ivo grumbling about pension math, the three of them under a bad fan with a tray between them: it belonged to a world where the worst thing in front of Marisol was a swollen knee and not the fact that her son was sitting restrained two tracks over under a country's name that was attached to him by a dead man and a treaty formula.

Ivo ate two more spoons of stew, gave up, and pushed the tray away. "Storm's still sitting north."

"You can tell by your aching foot?" Paloma asked.

"By the coupler seals whining all afternoon. By the charge in the catwalk rails."

"Now the rails are gossiping to you."

"Everything gives warning before it quits."

Over the cafeteria speakers the synthetic voice cut in again, this time routing southbound exceptions: medical holds to trauma, property discrepancies to records, final reconciliation on Cars Seven and Eight before lock. Marisol's spoon stopped halfway down.

Paloma saw it at once. "Is it a body?"

Marisol set the spoon onto the tray. "It's a case."

"That wasn't the question."

The speaker rolled on to commuter boarding, bright and useless. Ivo was no longer watching the platform. He was watching Marisol.

"Sol," Paloma said, quieter now, "if this is one of mine, don't make that call on your handheld. Use the wall phone by pensions. Those route slabs rat on you cleaner than people do."

"It isn't medical."

Paloma's mouth tightened. "Bodies don't stop being medical because paperwork got there first."

Ivo wiped his fingers on the paper fork sleeve. "I've got an open coupler complaint on Car Eight and records still owes me the overflow sheet. If you need ten minutes off the map, you just remembered that for me."

"I didn't ask either of you for cover."

"You wore it in here," he said.

Paloma finished half her bread and wrapped the rest in a napkin for later. She wasted nothing edible and almost nothing green. "I've got to get back before intake decides another split lip is dehydration."

"You ever take a full break?" Ivo asked.

"Not since the company discovered understaffing."

"That's why your hand shakes."

"My hand shakes because I've been suturing idiots since sunrise," she said. "Different diagnosis."

She stood, gathering the cups. Her right hand shook once when she lifted the tray and she shifted the weight to the left without comment. Marisol stood too fast, as if the move had been waiting under her skin all meal, then regretted it when the knee caught and sent a hot grind up her thigh.

Paloma saw that and clicked her tongue. "Heat, Sol. Not stairs, not pride, not another shift of pretending your joints are made of policy."

"I've got one call to make."

"Then make it sitting down if you can."

"Look at you," Ivo said. "Medically bossed by a woman who can't finish a break."

"That's because I work with amateurs," Paloma said. "And if either of you touches my cups, I'll prescribe something ugly."

She left with her cups and wrapped bread, moving with that compact quickness she had even when exhausted, shoulders already turning toward the trauma-room corridor, already listening for the next body. Ivo watched her go.

"She's going to bolt herself to that trauma room one day," he said.

"She practically has now."

"Good. Somebody ought to keep one living thing on this route that isn't under inventory."

The sentence put a fine wire through Marisol's chest. She kept her face still. Ivo looked back at her then, really looked, the way a mechanic listened with his hands before he took a panel off.

"Car Eight?" he asked.

She folded the napkin once more, exact along the crease. "Don't do that."

He bent, pulled the folded maintenance docket out from under the bad table leg, and slid it across to her. "Then don't make me guess blind. Take this. If anybody asks, you're chasing my overflow sheet and signing the coupler note."

"You don't know what you're covering."

"I know it isn't a manifest mismatch."

There was the ghost of a smile at one corner of his mouth. It made him look younger and more tired at the same time. She had known that expression in a bunk once. In the junction light it was only an old tool left in the wrong drawer. He picked up his tray.

"I've got to check on that coupler complaint before night crew invents religion around it," he said. "If your call goes bad, find me on the platform."

"You should call Lina," Marisol said, because it was safer than answering what he had actually offered.

He grunted. "I will. I'll tell her what the experts said."

"Tell her pigeons survive everything," Marisol said.

"That's not a comfort."

"No," she said. "It isn't."

When he had gone, the folded docket was damp in Marisol's hand. The cafeteria grew louder for having fewer known faces in it. Marisol stacked her tray, left it at the return slot, and took the corridor toward the administrative wing where the crew phones hung. The hall was colder than the cafeteria because fewer bodies had breathed into it. Fluorescent tubes buzzed above her. A bulletin board outside the pension office held notices about hydration, a summer injury poster with a cartoon spine, and a payroll reminder about housing-deduction schedules. Someone had drawn a halo over the cartoon spine in blue pen.

She used the wall phone instead of her handheld. Route devices logged too cleanly, Paloma had named the risk aloud, and Marisol did not want this call attached to her name any more than it had to be. The handset smelled faintly of citrus sanitizer and older hands. She keyed in her supervisor number, then the case-services extension, then sat through the recorded menu in both languages while a synthetic woman explained that quality and compliance monitoring might occur. By the third prompt Marisol had dug the edge of her thumbnail under the paper label on the phone cradle and lifted half of it away.

A live voice came on at last. It was female, tired, neutral, mid-shift somewhere far from the heat in the yard.

"Corridor case services. Employee number, please."

Marisol gave it.

"How can I help you, Supervisor Anaya?"

"I've got a live southbound file out of Puerta Seco. Case four-four-seven-one-R. The receiving-state designation appears mismatched to subject history. I need a correction before release."

Keyboard clicks. Marisol could hear each one land hard and separate, little teeth on glass.

"Did intake validation clear the ancestry packet?" the voice asked.

"Yes. That's the problem."

"If the packet validated, the designation is not treated as a clerical error."

"It is an error."

"It falls under case-integrity review, Supervisor."

Marisol leaned her forehead briefly against the cool cinder-block wall. "Open one."

"This desk does not open review on a live route."

"Then who does?"

"Regional compliance can submit a case-integrity request. A court order can interrupt status. Receiving state refusal can interrupt at transfer."

Each sentence arrived smooth and properly spaced, no malice in it, which made it worse. A human voice had said the words, but the shape of them had already been decided somewhere else, in a template, in a training file, in a room full of people who never had to stand two feet from a restrained body and watch a bruise come in.

"I need somebody to look at the file now," Marisol said. "The train is at Caldera. We roll south tonight."

"I can log concern language and timestamp the contact."

"That won't hold dispatch."

"No, Supervisor."

"Then what good is it?"

There was the smallest pause, a worker checking which answer belonged to the slot.

"It preserves sequence," the woman said.

Marisol looked at the bulletin board across from her: hydration poster, payroll notice, cartoon spine with its stupid halo. She kept hearing the phrase preserve sequence. The line knew how to preserve sequence. It preserved sequence on restraint logs, on waste manifests, on the chain of custody for a child's inhaler taken at intake and never returned in the same country. Sequence was what systems said when they meant nobody was coming.

"Put it in, then," she said.

"I need concern language."

Marisol shut her eyes. What language could hold it? My son is not from the place you are sending him. A dead man's paper is driving a live body south. I saw the marks your intake team left on him and I need them not to become foreign soil. Instead she heard herself say, clipped and procedural because that was the only register the phone respected:

"Subject history appears inconsistent with designated receiving state. Request supervisory review of ancestry validation and attached source documents prior to transfer."

More keys.

"Logged," the woman said. "Ticket number K-nine-two-seven-one-six."

"Does regional compliance see it tonight?"

"I can't guarantee review time."

"Can you flag urgency?"

"It's already classified as active transport."

That was as far as the desk would bend. Marisol thanked her because years on the route had trained politeness into the muscles that took abuse and kept moving. She hung up before the woman could offer any scripted closing. The dial tone returned, level and indifferent. In the silence after it, the junction gave her back its real noises: a brake squeal from the commuter line, a cart rattling over a floor seam, someone laughing too hard two doors down.

She stood there with the useless ticket number in her head and understood the narrowness of what had just died. Hope had already taken hits. What failed here was her faith in a quiet clerical fix, the sort of back-channel adjustment that kept operations from embarrassing themselves. If she took this upward now it would not be a fix. It would be a file, a named challenge, a record with her own access badge all over it. Teresa would see it. Maybe she had to. Maybe that was next. But something in Marisol had already started moving sideways, away from permission. The folded docket crackled when she shifted her grip. Ivo had bought her ten minutes. Paloma had taken the cleanest audit trail out of her hand. Neither of them had asked for the truth in words, which made the debt feel heavier, not lighter.

The property room sat thirty feet down the hall behind a wire-glass door with a keypad most supervisors knew by feel. She knew which digits stuck in humid weather and which one needed the nail, not the pad. Inside, the air was stale with plastic, laundry powder, old pockets. Metal shelving ran to the back wall. Clear bags hung from hooks or lay in bins under car numbers: belts, phones with batteries taped to them, medicine bottles, cheap jewelry gone gray at the clasp, one pair of dentures in a cloudy cup, a canvas shoe with cartoon stars on the toe. Beside the door a movement ledger hung from a clipboard under a dead barcode wand. Anything pulled for manifest confirmation was supposed to be scanned out or signed by hand, because the wand missed half its uploads once the weather turned wet. Its violence was domestic: the shaved-off household matter of a life the state wanted portable.

Marisol let the door close softly behind her. She listened first. There were no steps in the corridor and no voices close, only the refrigeration unit kicking on in the adjoining records closet and the far murmur of the junction beyond the wall.

She found Car Eight's overflow bin where intake had shelved the unreturned electronics and paper items pending southbound manifest confirmation. The tags were in numerical order until they weren't. She moved them with the same hands she had used for twenty years to sort what could travel with a body and what had to be logged away from it. Her own breathing sounded too loud in the room.

Then she found 4471-R.

The bag was lighter than she had expected, a clear zip pouch with a barcode sticker curling at one corner. Inside sat the recorder she had seen hanging in intake, matte black, thumb-sized, with its batteries taped in a smaller inner sleeve and a strip of masking tape on the back carrying Gabriel's block print: NORTH / JUNE / WITNESS 3. The writing undid her more than the machine did. He had always labeled things as if disorder were a personal insult. As a boy he wrote his name on cassette cases, on school notebooks, once on the underside of a kitchen chair because he said everything in the apartment migrated when she was at work and somebody had to mark the survivors.

Marisol slid the bag onto the metal shelf and held still until the tremor left her fingertips. This was theft. The route had a thousand words for seizure and none for return.

She peeled the tape strip back from the battery sleeve with painful care, the adhesive making a tiny dry sound in the room. She took the batteries first and put them in the coin pocket of her work pants. Then she opened the larger zip, removed the recorder, and held it in her palm. It was warmer than the room from trapped heat in the plastic, absurdly light for something that had already pushed her out of the last safe excuse. It was a field tool, a witness box small enough to disappear in a closed fist.

The handle clicked before she could rehang the bag.

"Supervisor?"

The voice came through the wire glass, male, bored, close. "You in property? Records needs the Car Eight overflow sheet before they lock."

Marisol flattened herself against the shelving, recorder in hand. Beside the door the movement ledger hung open under the dead barcode wand, blank beside 4471-R. If he opened up, he would see the pouch gaping on the shelf and her with contraband in her fist.

"Battery sleeve split on me," she said. "Stay out unless you want caustic on your hands."

A muttered curse. "You need decon?"

"I need two minutes and a red drum."

His steps went off at once.

Marisol moved before the sound had cleared.
