She shoved the empty sleeve back into the pouch, looped an orange hazard tie through the zipper, and yanked open the drawer under the scanner cradle until she found a swollen handset battery wrapped in old evidence tape. In the movement ledger she wrote 4471-R / BATTERY LEAK / ISOLATED PENDING DISPOSAL and set her initials beside the time. Then she tore the Car Eight overflow sheet off the clip and folded it once. The lie would buy one glance, maybe two. It would also put her badge, her hand, and a false hazard call on the discrepancy when morning inventory caught up.

When the porter came back rolling the red drum, she had the pouch rehung and the scrap battery pinched inside the torn sleeve. She opened the door only wide enough to drop it in and pass him the overflow sheet through the gap.

"Tag that bin and leave the shelf alone till day crew," she said.

He looked at the sleeve, then at her face, and did what most workers did when given a chance to avoid extra contamination: he chose distance. "Copy, Supervisor."

She shut the door before he could ask anything else.

She tucked the recorder inside the waistband of her work pants, under her shirt, where the hard rectangle rested against the soft lower belly she still thought of as the part of her body Gabriel had once occupied without asking permission. The thought came and she nearly flinched from it. She shut the property-room light off, keyed the door behind her, and walked back into the corridor at the pace of a woman who had completed a boring errand.

On the platform side window, dusk had gone from yellow to a flat iron blue. A commuter train pulled out, taking its clean passengers home. Farther down the yard she could see Ivo near the edge of the maintenance lane, one shoulder against a post, phone to his ear at last. He was listening more than speaking, head bent, the posture Marisol had seen once before when Gabriel was ten and had split his chin on a sink and Ivo had stood outside the clinic calling his daughter to explain why he'd miss dinner. In the trauma-room corridor on the other side, Paloma was setting the little cups on a sill under a light, turning the leaves toward whatever weak brightness the building gave.

The recorder pressed against Marisol with each step, small and undeniable. The hotline had given her a ticket number and nothing else. The machine at her waist had weight. It could be carried. It could be hidden. It had survived somebody else's hands. By the time she started up to the records alcove, it had already warmed to her body.


# Chapter 4

Up in the records alcove above the sanitation bay, everything sweated by association. The floor was dry, the cabinets were dry, the forms were dry, but the heat from the wash line climbed through the metal anyway and turned the room into a place where paper curled at the corners before noon. Marisol let herself in with her passcard, felt the old catch in her left knee on the threshold, and locked the door behind her. The recorder hidden under her shirt pressed warm and rectangular against her lower belly when she turned the lock. Through the wall she could hear the blackwater pump on Car 6 taking its load in slow surges, a wet-choked thump followed by the cleaner whine of the recovery line. It was southbound prep. She had two hours, maybe less, before dispatch wanted every signature in place and every body counted as movable.

She set Gabriel's case number on the blotter. Numbers behaved better when you pinned them down. 4471-R. The terminal woke under her hand with a patch of dead pixels in one corner and the yellow RailPassage logo opening like bad milk. She logged into route review, then into treaty mirror, then into the secondary custody screen that still ran on old government architecture and always lagged when the corridor got busy. The train below her vibrated through the floor in a steady floorboard hum. Somebody dropped a wrench in the bay and swore. A synthetic announcement repeated boarding restrictions in English and Spanish, polite as a church volunteer and just as deaf.

She did not need hope. She needed a sentence. Somewhere in all the forms there had to be one sentence that let a living woman tell the machine it had the wrong country.

The file opened on the fourth try.

RAFAEL AVELAR.

CASE 4471-R.

RECEIVING STATE: SANTA AVELINA.

ROUTING BASIS: A-3 PARENTAL REGISTRY.

PRE-TRANSFER REVIEW: UNAVAILABLE WHILE ANCESTRAL CLAIM IS ACTIVE.

The yellow ancestry icon pulsed in the corner of the screen. Marisol hated that icon. It made a person look like weather, something already decided higher up than a body. She clicked into the supporting packet anyway. The system thought for a long moment, then gave her a stack of scans: a dockworker certificate from Santa Avelina, payroll fragments from a port authority she had only seen on manifests, a death record with Tom Avelar's name printed so cleanly it looked more official than he had ever been alive. The document edges showed salt rot and old fold lines. Someone in archives had flattened them, whitened the background, boosted the seals. The machine had polished the dead man for reuse.

She zoomed in until the letters blurred. Tom's surname was still the one Gabriel carried when he had been small enough to let other people decide who he belonged to. Marisol pressed two fingers against the bridge of her nose until she saw white.

She backed out and opened the challenge menu. Everything that mattered was gray. DESTINATION ERROR. LOCKED. IDENTITY CONTEST. ROUTE VIEW ONLY. FAMILY INTERVENTION. COMPLIANCE RESTRICTED. The only live box was ADD NOTE, and the warning below it made her stomach pull tight: ROUTE-LEVEL NOTES BECOME PART OF TRANSPORT CHAIN OF CUSTODY AND ARE AUDITABLE ACROSS MEMBER-STATE REVIEW.

If she wrote he is my son in that box, the sentence would never belong to her again.

She picked up the desk phone instead. The handset cord was so stiff with age it dragged the base an inch when she lifted it. She keyed through treaty review, listened to three levels of menu options that treated emergency, discrepancy, and appeal as if they were products she might prefer, and finally got a human voice.

"Pre-transfer desk."

The woman sounded as if she had a sunflower seed tucked in one cheek. Marisol could hear the shell click lightly against her teeth between words.

"Meridian West route supervision," Marisol said. "I need an immediate destination challenge opened on case 4471-R. The receiving state is incorrect."

Keys tapped. The woman swallowed. "That case is ancestry-verified."

"Yes. It is still wrong."

"That is not the same issue."

Marisol flattened the file number sheet with the heel of her hand. "The paternal record is a dead man's dock certificate. The detainee has never lived in Santa Avelina, never transited there, never worked there, and I need the route hold authority for reassignment before departure."

The woman clicked her tongue, not unkindly. "Supervisor, route hold is not available on an active ancestral hook. If the parentage packet has been accepted by the receiving state, corridor transport cannot suspend on disagreement alone."

"Then what can suspend it?"

"A court stay. Contradictory parentage documentation. Verified fraud in the packet. A receiving-state withdrawal, which you are not going to get before dispatch."

Marisol looked at Tom Avelar's death record on the second monitor. "The father is dead."

"Death does not dissolve parentage."

"The son has never been there."

"Presence is not required for ancestral admissibility."

The phrase landed with the weight of something recited a hundred times a week. Marisol could picture the woman at the other end without seeing her: low partitions, bad air, a plastic cup sweating rings into a desk, a line of cases waiting to be converted into the right phrases.

"What does required mean here?" Marisol asked. "Required by whom? The car is still on my line. The receiving state does not have him yet."

"The receiving state accepted the basis at 04:18. After that, your corridor is transport custody. You are not adjudication. If there is an appeal, it attaches after handoff."

After handoff meant after the southern port, after the ship or the bus or whatever uniformed stranger met the train and took the bodies farther out of reach. Marisol knew all the words. She had been working inside them for twenty years. Hearing them applied to Gabriel made them sound cheap and handmade.

"Open the fraud review anyway," she said. "I will send the contradictory note."

"What contradictory note?"

The truth crowded her mouth and would have ruined her if she let it out wrong. His father had not raised him. His father's country had never been home. His father's papers had spent more years in a drawer than the man had spent sober. None of that mattered to a field with locked drop-downs.

"There is a family discrepancy," she said.

Silence, and then the woman's voice sharpened by a degree. "If you are immediate family, you need to disclose conflict and recuse from case contact. I can transfer you to compliance."

Marisol kept her eyes on the screen. The departure column on the side panel had already turned from blue to green. Seal checks were ninety-two minutes out. Water load would start in seventy-six. Southbound release would follow after that if nothing failed.

"Transfer me," she said.

The hold music came on, a small bright tune with no business existing in a room like this. While it played, the system threw a new banner over Gabriel's file.

CASE ESCALATED TO REGIONAL COMPLIANCE REVIEW.

AUTHORIZATION: T. ORDONEZ.

Of course Teresa's name sat there before the call connected. Teresa had likely been watching the alerts already, maybe with one of her careful coffees going cold beside the keyboard, maybe with her expensive glasses somewhere she could not find them again. Marisol set the handset down before the transfer finished. She did not need Teresa through speaker static. She needed to see exactly how much door was left.

She pulled Tom Avelar's scans back up one more time. There was no hidden seam in them. The port seal had been verified. The death record had been linked. The receiving state had pressed Accept, and the machine had locked every route-level question behind that acceptance as cleanly as if it were closing a hatch. Gabriel's case had not slipped into the wrong country by clerical accident. Somebody had built a lane for cases like his and taught the system to call that lane lawful.

Marisol printed the packet cover sheet, then regretted it as soon as the page slid out warm and damp from the tray. Paper made things feel changeable. Paper could still be lost, stained, misfiled, crumpled in a fist. This page only gave the lock another body. She folded it once, put it in her pocket anyway, and went downstairs to Teresa.

* * *

Teresa Ordonez had taken over the old pension office beside dispatch, which still had a mural of smiling retirees fishing under a blue lake no one on the corridor had ever seen. The office glass was too clean for the building around it. Even the heat felt better behaved on Teresa's side of the door. A portable cooling unit hummed by the filing shelves, pushing cold air over stacks of compliance binders and sealed evidence envelopes. Teresa stood at the table in her immaculate gray uniform with her reading glasses nowhere visible, turning one folder over, then another, while her bitten cuticles flashed under clear polish when the light hit them.

"You are early," she said without looking up.

Marisol closed the door behind her. "You flagged the case."

"You touched it seven times in twenty-three minutes. I did not have to work very hard." Teresa finally found the glasses tucked into the front of her own jacket, put them on, and scanned Marisol over the rims. "Sit down."

Marisol stayed standing.

Years ago, when Marisol still spent most shifts with bleach on her sleeves and a mop handle digging a permanent callus into her palm, Teresa had been the one who noticed that her incident reports came in complete and on time. Teresa had signed the recommendation that moved her from sanitation attendant to lead, then from lead to route supervision. She liked workers who did not make her chase missing numbers. She liked people who knew how to turn chaos into paperwork that investors could survive. Marisol had mistaken that preference for faith exactly once and had not repeated the error.

"Is case 4471-R your son?" Teresa asked.

There was no point using the wrong noun now. "Yes."

Teresa set both hands flat on the table. Her nails were neat. One cuticle at the thumb had been worked raw enough to show a half-moon of pink underneath. "Then you have already made this worse than it needed to be."

"I asked for an exception."

"You asked for an exception after accessing a sealed file repeatedly without disclosing direct family conflict."

"I'm disclosing it now."

"You are pleading it now," Teresa said. "Those are not the same thing."

Marisol felt the paper in her pocket stick against the fabric with sweat. "The assignment is wrong."

"Wrong is not a route category."

"He has never set foot in Santa Avelina."

"That is irrelevant once the parental basis is accepted."

Teresa said it the way she might have said the train had already been fueled or the quarterly numbers had already gone upstairs. Not cruelly. Not with pleasure. With the calm of a person who had spent years surviving by naming the wall and then standing beside it.

Marisol put both hands on the back of the empty chair and leaned into the metal. "Then tell me what is relevant."

Teresa took off the glasses again, cleaned one lens with a square of cloth from her sleeve pocket, bought herself a breath. "At this stage, relevant means this. The receiving state has recognized his father's certificate. The Compact lets them claim provisional admissibility through parentage whether or not he has lived there. Once that recognition is entered, Meridian West is transport custody only. We move him. We log condition. We do not litigate belonging from a junction office over a cooling unit that leaks into the carpet."

Her voice never rose. The steadiness pressed on Marisol harder than anger would have. Anger could at least be pushed back. Teresa's control made every sentence sound pre-approved by somebody with a better desk.

"There has to be a discretionary review," Marisol said. "Medical vulnerability, misidentification, humanitarian hold, something."

"Medical holds require instability a clinician will sign and defend. Misidentification requires evidence that he is not who the file says he is. Humanitarian holds require external jurisdiction or a scandal no one in this building can contain. What you have is a blood tie and a bad outcome."

Marisol heard herself before she meant to speak. "He's not a bad outcome."

Teresa's eyes shifted, just once, and some older knowledge passed through them. She remembered Gabriel at sixteen, maybe, hanging in the employee lot with a backpack and a mouth that already looked hard to persuade. She remembered Marisol bringing leftovers to double shifts and never talking about the boy unless someone else forced the subject. Whatever Teresa knew, she did not use it tenderly.

"Do not do this in nouns you can love," she said. "It will not help him."

Marisol let go of the chair. Her chemical burn scar pulled pale on the inside of her wrist. "What helps him, then?"

"A quiet file. A transport that does not attract review for the wrong reason. A post-transfer petition from counsel who is not his mother and cannot be accused of contaminating chain of custody."

"Post-transfer." Marisol laughed once, with no humor in it. "You mean after they dump him somewhere he has never been and call it due process."

"I mean after he is in a jurisdiction that can hear the petition attached to the category you are objecting to." Teresa folded the lens cloth into a precise square. "If you force this into an undisclosed-family incident here, security will isolate him, the file will be frozen for internal review rather than reopened, and your own access will end today. They will not free him because you are emotional. They will simply move him under a harder set of hands."

Marisol thought of Gabriel's bruised ribs, the mark at the top of his arm where intake restraint had slipped and tightened on skin instead of sleeve. Harder hands were easy to picture. Harder hands were the line's answer to most delays.

"You're asking me to do nothing."

"I am asking you not to confuse motion with leverage." Teresa put the glasses back on. "There are times when the cleanest thing a mother can do is refrain from putting herself into the record."

When Teresa said mother, Marisol wanted to strike something. Teresa knew that. Teresa used family language the way she used concession language, as a pressure tool meant to guide behavior toward the least expensive outcome.

"He's my record," Marisol said.

"No. He is a detainee on a sealed route and your son on your own time." Teresa's voice flattened. "If you mix those categories inside the file, I cannot protect you."

Protect was the word Teresa chose. She always knew which words insulted less if she polished them. Marisol saw the shape of the threat under it anyway: her badge, her housing deduction, the health coverage that had paid for knee injections when the stairs got bad last winter, the years it had taken to climb out of mop water and into something with an office key. Teresa did not need to describe the whole fall. Both of them had watched enough people take it.

"You promoted me because I knew this line better than anyone," Marisol said. "You knew what I was when you signed those forms."

"Yes," Teresa said. "Which is why I am speaking to you here instead of letting internal security discover for itself why a route supervisor suddenly cannot stop touching one file."

Its partial truth held Marisol still for a beat. Teresa was containing her. Teresa was also tightening the walls.

Marisol took the folded cover sheet from her pocket and put it on the table between them. Tom Avelar's name sat there in block print, useful after death in a way he had never been alive.

"He hated the sea," she said before she could stop herself. "Tom got sick on ferries. He had one certificate from one season unloading crates at a port he wanted to leave. Now a dead man gets to drag his son across a border he never even crossed sober."

Teresa looked down at the page but did not touch it. "The file is not a biography."

"No. It's worse."

For the first time something like impatience broke Teresa's surface. She took off the glasses again and set them on the folder too hard. "What do you want me to say, Marisol? That the Compact is just when it is efficiently administered? It is a transport treaty built from categories. Categories are always cruder than lives. I do not have the authority to repair that from Caldera Junction, and neither do you."

The room cooled the backs of Marisol's damp arms and left the front of her body burning. The old pension mural behind Teresa showed a painted woman laughing with a fish on the end of a line. Marisol wanted to tear the mural straight down the middle.

"Then give me the route authority to delay pending review."

"No."

"Because you can't?"

"Because I will not create a discretionary lane that every lawyer on this corridor can later argue should have existed for every other file." Teresa's hand moved to the corner of the table where her tablet sat face-down. "And because if I open that door for your son because he is yours, I am no longer even pretending to be able to defend what comes after."

Teresa had come as close to honesty as Marisol had ever heard from her, and the admission opened a strange small space in the room, not sympathy exactly, but the outline of something a little rawer. She was not denying the ugliness. She was saying the ugliness had already been costed.

Marisol took a breath that tasted like machine-cooled dust. "So that is it."

"No," Teresa said. "This is it. I have placed the case under compliance watch until southbound release. Any further route access to 4471-R pings my terminal. That protects you as much as it limits you. You will finish your dispatch responsibilities. You will not add family language to chain-of-custody. You will not freelance counsel. If you need to speak to the detainee for operational reasons, you do it with a custody officer present and the contact remains brief."

"Operational reasons."

"Those are the only reasons that still exist on paper." Teresa picked up the glasses again, then frowned and searched the table for them before realizing they were already in her hand. She gave a short annoyed exhale at herself, put them back on, and continued as if Marisol had not seen. "After transfer, I can route a petition marker through receiving-state review. Quietly. That is what is available."

Marisol imagined the marker traveling south in some clean electronic lane beside the bodies, thin as thread and just as easy to snap.

"You think I should be grateful."

"I think gratitude is not the point." Teresa reached for the folder at last and slid Tom Avelar's cover sheet back across the table with one finger. "Do not contaminate the record with family feeling. If you want to help him, stay useful."

Marisol took the page, folded it smaller, and put it away. The conversation had done what Teresa wanted. It had turned rage into a space problem, too little room for it in the chest, too many consequences attached to where it might spill.

"You always did know how to say no like it was a favor," she said.

Teresa's mouth tightened, almost a smile and not one. "That is one of the reasons they pay me."

When Marisol opened the door, Teresa added, "And Marisol."

She turned back.

"Do not make me choose between reporting you properly and preserving what is left of your career."

Teresa had polished the threat by calling it preserving, but it still hit the room the hard way, sharp as bleach with the cap off. Marisol left before she had to answer it.

* * *

The custody car had the afternoon smell already, wet cloth beginning to sour, old urine worked up from seams by heat, the sweet plastic note of restraint foam warming after the morning wipe-down. The vents were running, but not well enough to flatten the human air. Bodies made their own weather in there. Every bunk carried a different arrangement of it: fever, fear, stale milk, cheap soap, anxious sweat, someone trying not to vomit again.

Marisol signed in at the car gate under the pretext of checking water allotment before seal. The custody officer on the latch side was young enough that his jaw still looked unfinished. A tiny purple crescent moon sticker clung to the back of his watch as if a child had pressed it there and he had forgotten to scrape it off. He saw her look and turned his wrist inward.

"Two minutes, Supervisor," he said. "Captain wants the aisle clear for count."

"Then don't spend forty of my seconds talking."

He almost smiled, then stopped short. "Two minutes."

Gabriel was on the lower bunk by the window slit, knees angled up because the space had been built for compliance, not comfort. Intake had left yellowing bruises along his upper arm and the dark spill under one rib where someone had knelt him too fast against the bench edge. His fingers were stained with old ink deep in the cuticles, as if the body still remembered the recorder and the notebooks even without them. He looked thinner in the car than he had in the trauma room. The line did that to people fast. It pressed them down into what could be transported.

He watched her come without moving to meet her. "You look terrible."

"I'm working."

"That's what I said."

The guard took two steps away for the sake of pretending not to hear. The aisle still carried noise enough to blur them, a child fussing farther down, somebody coughing up something wet, the automated station note repeating hydration guidance nobody could follow with any dignity.

Marisol kept her voice low. "I found the policy hook."

"Congratulations."

"It's your father."

Gabriel's face barely changed. Only one corner of his mouth twitched, not toward a smile. "He's been dead a long time and he still can't mind his business."

"His Santa Avelina certificate is what locked the route."

"Not the route. The country."

"Both." She looked down the aisle, then back at him. "They call it ancestral admissibility. Once the receiving state accepts the parentage packet, nobody on the corridor can hear a challenge before handoff. I tried review. I tried compliance. There's no lawful way to stop the transfer from here."

He let that sit between them for a second, maybe to see whether she would decorate it with apology. When she did not, he nodded once.

"So they ship first," he said, "and later some office decides whether the ship was a lie."

"Yes."

"That sounds efficient."

There it was, the humor that only showed up when he was close to rage. It was one of the last things about him that still felt like childhood to her, not because he had been funny then but because he had always learned the shape of a trap faster than other people in the room.

"Did you tell Teresa?" he asked.

"Enough that she's watching the file."

"That was generous of you."

"Don't start."

"What, with accuracy?"

The old fight wanted to open under everything else, mother and son falling back into the grooves they knew best, his contempt, her command voice, each one using what hurt because it was quick and already in reach. Marisol forced herself to stay with the fact.

"I'm telling you because you needed the truth before the door shuts," she said. "I don't have a petition. I don't have a judge. I have nothing clean to offer you."

The words pulled his attention harder than any promise would have. He looked at her properly then, at the sweat in her hairline, the paper cut on her thumb from Teresa's cover sheet, the way she was holding her shoulders too still.

"Nothing clean," he repeated.

"No."

"Good."

She blinked. "Good?"

"If you had come back with some neat little plan for me alone, I was going to tell you to go to hell again. This is at least the right size of ugly."

The guard shifted by the gate. One minute left, maybe less.

Gabriel slid his hand under the thin mattress edge and brought out a folded scrap no bigger than two postage stamps laid together. It had once been the silver backing from a meal-wrapper label. He had written on the white side in lines so fine Marisol could barely see them until he pressed it into her palm.

"What is this?"

"The start of a count."

The paper was soft with handling and stiff at one corner where dried soap had been used as glue. Names ran down it in cramped block letters with bunk numbers and destination codes beside them.

M. CARDENAS, 6-B, S.A.

H. FUNES, 4-A, B.M.

QUISPE, 8-C, P.A.

There were more on the reverse, half initials, half full surnames, all reduced the way frightened people reduced themselves when paper was scarce and guards liked to search.

Marisol looked up sharply. "What am I looking at?"

"People tagged to the wrong countries," Gabriel said. "People who know it because they know where they are from, which is still allowed for now. Some are ancestry hooks. Some are old labor-sponsor hooks. One man got assigned off a transit record from when he was nine and got put on the wrong bus for six hours. The system doesn't care what kind of wrong it uses if the receiving state says yes."

"How do you know these are wrong?"

He gave her a flat look. "I listen."

Then, because he saw she needed more than that, his voice changed. It lengthened, picked up detail. "Cardenas gets seasick from the fish slurry they serve on intake days and starts gagging before the tray gets near her. She's from dry inland cane country and has never seen a port bigger than a loading canal. Funes asked me three nights ago whether Belmora snow burns your lungs because he's only ever known heat. Quispe in 8-C knots wrapper strings around her bunk post every time somebody gets moved so she can remember who has vanished from where. None of them speak about the places on their tags like people who belong there. They speak about them like labels torn off crates."

The names on the paper blurred for a second before Marisol's eyes adjusted again. She realized Gabriel had not been making himself singular in the car. He had been keeping witness even here, in the smell and the heat and the constant counting. The instinct had survived intake, confiscation, bruises, all of it.

"Why give this to me?" she asked.

"Because now you know your case isn't your case."

"I knew that before."

"No." His voice stayed low, but the edge came back. "You knew the machine could hurt strangers and then it came for your son. That's not the same knowledge."

The line stung because it was exact. Marisol could have argued chronology, could have listed every body she had seen hauled through the trauma room and every time she had eased a threshold where she could. None of it would have touched what he meant. The system had been ugly for years. This was the first time she had let herself see design instead of damage.

She folded the scrap once along an existing crease. "How many?"

"More than I could write small enough before they searched the blankets. There are names on other cars too. If you get time, I can get you more. If you don't, start with those."

"Start how?"

He gave a short breath that might have been a laugh in another life. "You're the one with the keys."

"Keys don't open treaties."

"No," he said. "But they open doors and closets and maintenance panels and all the small stupid places a line depends on. You know what they respect. It's not us. It's stoppage."

Gabriel had reduced the whole day to its usable fact: the line did not respect blood or grief. It respected stoppage. The truth of that landed under her ribs with blunt physical force.

Down the aisle a baby started crying with the exhausted mechanical rhythm of a child who had gone past the point of wanting comfort and only had sound left. The guard at the gate lifted two fingers. Time was up.

Marisol put the scrap inside the cuff of her sleeve, against the burn scar where sweat would hold it flat. "If I do this," she said, keeping her voice so low it was almost breath, "I can't do it only for you."

Gabriel met her eyes. For the first time since intake, he did not look away first.

"Then don't," he said.

The guard came back down the aisle. "Supervisor."

Marisol straightened. Her knee bit hard when she turned. The car gave one long shudder underfoot as the coupling test started on the next line over. Everyone in the bunk row felt it. A dozen bodies changed posture at once, listening through the floor. They knew trains the way trapped animals knew weather. They could tell from vibration what might move them and what might break.

At the gate, the young officer with the moon sticker held out the sign-out tablet. He smelled faintly of medicated baby powder under the regulation soap, as if he had dressed in a hurry after leaving some domestic task half done. Marisol signed her name so hard the stylus skipped.

"Everything fine?" he asked, routine more than curiosity.

"No," she said.

He looked at her, uncertain whether that required action. She was already walking away before he could decide.

The dispatch corridor seemed narrower than it had an hour earlier. Freighted carts clogged one side, water drums the other. A deep-clean rig waited near sanitation stores with its tamper seals still bright red, the bleach canisters strapped tight, the clipboard fat with forms that could stop a route cold if any one box on them turned from preventative to active. Beyond it, through the glass, the departure board counted down green minutes toward southbound release, except for one car still sitting amber on the wall.

Marisol stood with the names damp against her wrist and listened to the building: pumps laboring in the walls, vents chopping hot air, wheel checks ringing metal to metal, the two-language politeness of the announcements smoothing over all of it. Teresa had been right about one thing. The file was not a biography. It would not open for grief, or blood, or the fact that Tom Avelar had become more useful dead than he had ever managed alive. Law had shut every door that used its own name.

But the rig was still there, sealed and waiting. The water drums had not been rolled to the car. The bleach canisters had not been signed out. On the clipboard, the stoppage categories sat in their neat boxes, the ones dispatch feared because they turned a late train into a liability: soft-surface exposure, ventilation contamination, full deep clean before release. The departure board could keep counting down in green; if one of those boxes went active before seal, southbound would stop itself.

The southbound clock kept moving.

So did she, turning toward sanitation stores.


# Chapter 5

Bleach had dried white in the creases of Marisol's knuckles by the time she looked up at the departure board. CAR 8 RELEASE in 00:18. SOUTHBOUND SLOT CONFIRMED. The red digits hung over Caldera's platform big enough to shame everybody. Under them the bay floor sweated old coolant, mop water, and a sweetness from the vending-machine syrup line that had been leaking for weeks. She stood at the wall terminal with her supervisor passcard clipped to her pocket. The clock lost a minute while she watched it. The line did most of its violence that way, with the digits stepping down while nobody touched the board.

She had spent twenty years learning which messes were considered real and which were only labor. Blood on restraint vinyl was labor. Urine pooled under a toilet flange was labor. Vomit in a family bunk was labor unless it got into the foam, and even then there were grades, there were codes, there were ways to write a body back into dispatch. But once waste was described as airborne, once contamination crossed from surface to system, no one in compliance wanted to own the release. The company could forgive filth. It could not forgive the words that made filth legible.

Car 8 was amber, not red. The aft-lavatory ammonia reading was high, and moisture sat under the lower bunks. The sensor history showed one brief backspit after intake, then a stable line. It was a tired car and a hot day. Nothing unusual. Marisol touched the incident field. The rubber of the glove caught on the screen.

`Cross-surface fecal aerosol following blackwater backspit during hold. Soft material exposure unresolved. Full deep clean required before dispatch.`

The system asked for source witness and authorizing supervisor. She entered her own number twice.

The first honest part of the lie was the filth itself. It had existed, as it always did. She invented the threshold, the point at which discretion ended and protocol became compulsory. She selected FULL COMPARTMENT SANITATION, acknowledged the warning that bedding, restraint foam, and temporary detainee displacement would be required, and pressed submit before she could start naming what the order would cost.

The red banner hit the board with a hard electronic chirp. CAR 8 RELEASE disappeared. HOLD - SANITATION REVIEW replaced it.

A custody sergeant came up the platform so fast the ring on his left hand kept striking the rail. He was a broad man with a peppermint swollen into one cheek and the damp collar of somebody who had quit cigarettes three times already this year and hated everyone for noticing. He looked at the board, then at Marisol.

"Tell me you hit the wrong box."

"I didn't. Aft backspit, soft surfaces, possible vent exposure. I need the compartment cleared."

"We sealed the manifest."

"Unseal it."

He laughed once, without humor. "You want me to pull thirty-one people back into holding because a toilet burped?"

"I want you to move them because if aerosol got into the vent track, you're about to spend six hours breathing shit for a schedule nobody's going to thank you for."

"You just found this now."

"I found it when I found it."

The sergeant stared at her long enough to show he heard the edges in that answer. The clock above them was already converting delay into money, incident emails, supervisors called away from better forms of contempt. Then he turned and shouted for the officers to crack the car back open.

Marisol moved before the echo finished. Authority, once spoken, had to keep moving or it turned into argument. She keyed the sanitation locker, issued two more Tyvek sleeves than the shift had budgeted, and told the cleaners to strip every soft surface in 8 from door gasket to rear lavatory. "Blankets first, then foam, then straps. Double-bag anything wet enough to drip. Don't leave me one dry-looking corner unless you're ready to sign your name to it."

A younger cleaner with a cracked phone screen glanced down at a text alert and swore softly in Spanish. The day-care app on the glass showed a late-pickup fee climbing by the quarter hour. He shoved the phone back into his pocket and took the bags without complaint. Another woman, older and already limping in one ankle boot, pulled her braid over her shoulder, tucked it into the neck of the Tyvek, and asked, "Whole compartment?"

"Whole compartment," Marisol said.

The woman blew air through her teeth. "Christ."

The shift had already run long enough that everybody's calculations were exposed. The cleaners did not talk about housing deductions in front of management, but Marisol knew them as well as she knew bleach ratios. Miss the crew shuttle and you paid for your own ride back to the dorm block. Miss enough scheduled turns and you lost the heat bonus that made June survivable. Every extra half hour on a hold climbed into somebody's groceries. The company loved sanitation work for that very reason. It made harm look like arithmetic.

Marisol did not apologize. The moment the order existed, apology was only vanity.

By the time the first officer got the side door rolled wide, the smell reached them in layers: old urine cooked into metal, bleach from the last turn, the sourness of milk spit, and beneath it all the wet-sewer heat of a blackwater system that had been asked to pretend it was fine too many days in a row. The car had family bunks on one side and standard restraints on the other, a compromise design that pleased nobody except the people who wrote procurement notes. The lower mattresses were dark in patches. A bucket sat under the aft sink because someone had given up trusting the drain.

There were thirty-one detainees in the car, and the hold made all of them harder to place. Officers unlatched chain points, cut two temporary ties, barked everybody upright. A toddler who had been half asleep started screaming the moment the door opened all the way and the platform light hit him. A man with diarrhea stains dried down one calf tried to ask for a toilet and got handed a specimen bag instead. A woman with a bandaged wristbone under a paper hospital strip kept saying she needed her inhaler from the property sack and nobody could tell her whether the sack had already gone to reseal.

Meal trays that should have come down the line in twenty minutes were delayed because nobody served into a red-tagged compartment. A blood-pressure round stopped halfway through another car when the officers got pulled to supervise displacement. Marisol heard a child asking in a flat stunned voice whether the blanket would come back with the cartoon dogs still on it and knew the honest answer was probably no. Deep clean translated every small possession into either evidence or trash.

Marisol signed the displacement sheet and hated how steady her handwriting looked.

While the lavatory seals stayed open, officers passed down specimen bags and told people to hold it. A boy tried squatting behind the yellow chain and got yanked upright by the elbow. At the service cart one of the cleaners shouted that the rinse drum was empty. Marisol signed for another without checking stores. Extra water meant another quarter hour before anybody could call the car habitable.

A cropped-haired girl from the middle bunks sat down on the concrete the moment an officer let her and began retying her paper wristband with the patient concentration of somebody repairing fishing line. Two fingernails were missing from her right hand. She used the torn edge of a meal-wrapper strip to reinforce the knot. Gabriel was three bodies beyond her, wrists linked, the old scar through his left eyebrow gone white while the chain between his cuffs pulled tight and his gaze caught on the stripped bunks. He had seen the red banner on the board and understood whose hand had done it.

Marisol kept moving. If she met his eyes too long in public, the whole lie would slant toward him.

The cleaners went in fast. They stripped bunks until the car looked flayed, foam slabs stacked by the door like pale organs, damp blankets slung into orange bio bags, restraint straps dropped hissing into bleach tubs. One of the men climbed onto the upper frame and wiped the vent track with a white pad; when he brought it down, a brown fan of spray bloomed across it, exactly the pattern Marisol had needed and had not actually found. That almost made her sick. Dispatch could have photographed that pad and called the whole hold obvious.

The custody sergeant stalked beside her reading updates off his tablet with the ring still tapping the rail. "Dispatch wants ETA. They want to know whether this is thirty minutes or ninety."

"Tell them I know when the vent swabs come back."

"They're going to ask for images."

"Then send them images."

He looked at the stripped bunks, the overflowing orange bags, the open lavatory panel. "You like this one too much, Anaya."

She turned on him before the thought on her face could become confession. "You want to send them south with aerosolized fecal mist in a closed car, do it under your badge. I won't write it under mine."

That shut him up because liability was a language he believed in.

It also copied the hold to compliance.

A small icon flashed at the top of her terminal: regional review queue notified. Teresa would not be sitting there staring at it yet. Teresa had bigger fires and cleaner shoes. But the notice existed now. If the hold ran long, if there was a discrepancy between the sanitation log and the blackwater history, if anyone got curious enough to pull the raw sensor trace, the order would harden into a real thing with her name on it.

Marisol climbed down to the service side and checked the aft panel herself, crouching with her stiff knee complaining against the metal stair. The line pressure reading was boringly stable. The valve seated clean. The backflow flap had left a smear, but not the kind that justified the words she had used. Deep clean would buy time. It would not buy enough. Once the soft surfaces were swapped and the vents re-swabbed, dispatch would pressure them to release on reduced comfort status. The company could tolerate pissed-off officers, sick children, lost meal windows. It could not tolerate a missed southbound corridor if there was any plausible way to keep steel moving.

A cleaner came out with a mattress skin rolled under his arms like a butchered hide. "You want these all incineration, boss?"

If she marked them salvage, the car would clear faster. If she marked incineration, replacement inventory would have to be counted, scanned, and hauled up from stores, which meant more delay, more cost, and more people sitting on hot concrete.

"Incineration," she said.

The cleaner swore, not at her exactly, but near her. "That was the last stack from Bay Two."

"I know."

He hefted the dripping bundle higher and went.

Each destroyed foam set would hit somebody's route budget before the week was out. Management loved to call that accountability when they wanted supervisors policing one another with shame. Marisol knew where the memo would land if Teresa started tracing expense spikes: first on sanitation, then on maintenance, then on whichever worker had signed the wrong minute in the chain. Sabotage did not remove anybody from the ledger. It only rearranged who got billed.

The hold turned mean fast. Twenty minutes in, the chemical mist from the portable sprayers mixed with rail dust and sweat until everybody tasted pennies and soap. An officer shouted at a boy for leaning against the yellow chain. The boy was maybe twelve and had only one shoe because the other had gone into a contamination bag with his blanket. He hopped back onto the hot sole of one foot and tried not to cry. A mother crouched over the screaming toddler and fanned him with a manifest copy somebody had dropped. The paper went soft in her hand.

One of the portable sprayers coughed dry. The cleaner with the cracked phone lifted the empty canister at her like an accusation. Marisol signed out another concentrate pack and told him to run a second pass on the lower frames and restraint rails. The bleach hit the air sharp enough to water everybody's eyes, and the new wet sheen gave dispatch one more reason not to put bodies back inside.

The board updated again: HOLD REVIEW 00:34. Dispatch had given her half an hour before escalation, more than she had expected and less than she needed. Southbound release after that would still make the next slot at reduced priority, still keep Gabriel moving, still keep the ancestry packet alive.

The sergeant's radio snapped. He listened, jaw working around peppermint.

"They want mechanical cross-check on the aft waste line," he said. "Procedure change after the Brownsville spill."

Marisol took the radio from him without asking and heard the dispatcher repeat it in that dry neutral voice people used when they were already saving messages for later blame. "Supervisor Anaya, confirm whether the blackwater return assembly has been cleared by rolling stock. Sanitation red alone does not justify corridor loss."

Of course it didn't. Somebody in compliance had tightened the rule since the last public embarrassment. Pure dirt never outranked schedule for long.

Marisol handed the radio back. "Get them a mechanic."

"From where?"

Caldera Junction was full of mechanics. Only one of them mattered.

She watched the cleaner on the upper bunk scrape along the vent seam, watched orange bags swell with the smell of other people's bodies, watched Gabriel standing in the holding line without looking at her, and felt the choice settle in her like a swallowed bolt. Under her badge, the lie was already dangerous. Once another badge sat under hers, it would start dragging other people onto the track beside her.

By the time the aft swab came back and told her exactly nothing she did not already know, Marisol had made the decision. She clipped the tablet to her belt, told the sergeant nobody reboarded until she said so, and cut through the service corridor toward the maintenance bay with bleach on her sleeves and dispatch chewing through the last of her clean options.

***

Ivo was halfway under a commuter carriage when she found him, boots sticking out from under the chassis and a socket wrench ticking against the railbed in patient irritation. The maintenance bay at Caldera always smelled different from the cars: hotter metal, axle grease, old rain caught in ballast, coffee burned into a hot plate somewhere nobody admitted owning. A fan turned overhead with the dignity of a dying bird. On Ivo's bench a pair of drugstore reading glasses lay folded beside a maintenance manual, one arm repaired with blue electrical tape. Next to them, held flat under a magnet shaped like a heron, was a small crayon drawing of three black birds on a wire and the crooked printed word `forage` in a child's hand.

Marisol stopped long enough to see that and nothing else. Then Ivo rolled out, wiping his forearm across his face and leaving a dark crescent of grease by his temple.

"If this is about Bay Four, tell them the compressor can wait till Jesus." He saw her expression and his own changed. "What happened?"

"I need a blackwater return valve on Car 8 to fail."

He stayed on the concrete another second, like the floor had become useful. Then he stood. Tall, heavy through the shoulders, gait still carrying that rolling mechanic's sway even when he had not been moving, he looked past her toward the departure-board glow at the end of the corridor.

"Need," he said. "That's a generous verb."

"It's the car under sanitation hold."

"I saw the red. Thought maybe you'd finally lost your patience with toilets."

"Ivo."

"Don't `Ivo` me like I'm a wrench." He reached for the blue-taped glasses, put them on, squinted toward the departure-board glow, then stripped them off and pinched the bridge of his nose hard enough to leave grease there. "What kind of fail?"

"One that buys time. One that looks bad enough not to clear fast."

"Those two aren't the same thing."

He knew, which was part of why she had come. Ivo did not talk in abstractions when machinery was involved. He believed in parts, wear, what load did to metal, what fraud looked like when it had to survive inspection. Marisol heard the dispatch clock even in here. She also heard herself choosing the next words and knew she could still lie smaller if she wanted.

"My son is in 8," she said.

The bay seemed to narrow around that. Somewhere further down, an impact gun barked twice and cut off. Ivo worried the taped arm of the glasses between thumb and forefinger. His mouth opened, closed, and when he looked up again the joke had burned off his face.

"Gabriel."

"Yes."

He rubbed the back of his neck at the little burn scar high on the left side, a habit she remembered from years ago, from nights when he was trying not to tell her he was afraid of money. "I saw a kid with his eyebrow, but I told myself I was making ghosts."

"I need the car held."

"You need the whole fucking treaty held."

"I need this first."

He leaned against the bench and looked past her again, toward the corridor, toward the train she had not named and he did not need named. When he got cornered, he usually joked harder. This time the humor came and went before it could settle.

"If I hang a red mechanical tag on a waste return, it sits under my badge until reconciliation," he said. "They read those logs in August when my pension clocks over. Family court reads my pay stubs at the end of that same month. You know what I've got left on that file."

She did. Six thousand eight hundred dollars was not an abstraction when you earned it with summer overtime and a back that had started answering every ladder in the morning. Ivo had once shown her a spreadsheet on a paper napkin because he did not trust the pension-office website. He had drawn columns for wages, child support, fuel, and rent, then one skinny column labeled only `L.` for his daughter, too superstitious or too scared to spell her name out inside the math. He had not been embarrassed to show her. That made the memory worse.

"I know," she said.

"Then say the number in your head before you ask me again."

She did not. She could not make his arrears into a test she passed by reciting them. Instead she said, "If they clear 8 on schedule, Gabriel's southbound by noon."

Ivo stared at the heron magnet on the bench, at the taped glasses, at the crayon birds. His daughter was twelve now and obsessed with urban raptors one month, drainage ditches the next, any living thing that made a home out of civic neglect. He had started carrying sunflower seeds in his pocket because she had once told him city birds recognized generosity faster than people. Sometimes Marisol found the striped shells on the dashboard of his service truck.

"Paper won't do it," he said finally. "Not anymore. After Brownsville they'll read pressure, seat time, sensor lag. Teresa sees a clean history and I'm dead."

"What will do it?"

He took a brass shaving off the bench with the edge of his thumb. "A dirty history."

She took that as the closest thing to yes she was going to get.

He moved fast once he chose, because mechanics had the decency to know indecision only made later work harder. He grabbed a service kit, a roll of red tags, and a replacement seal still in its plastic. "Come on."

They walked back through the service side under the car line where the blackwater pipes ran warm and low enough to take scalp if you forgot yourself. Ivo crouched at the aft assembly of Car 8, clipped his work light under the housing, and listened with the side of his screwdriver pressed to the valve body. He could have been diagnosing a real fault. He was diagnosing what kind of lie could live through scrutiny.

"Backspit's bullshit," he murmured.

"I know."

"Seal's worn but not failed. Sensor's fine. Return arm's sluggish because nobody flushed the line proper after intake." He glanced up at her. "You want a delay or you want an audit?"

"I need delay without a whistle today."

"Modest ambitions."

He opened the housing, drew the valve plate out half an inch, and let Marisol see how clean it was. It was not perfect, only ordinary. Then he took the brass shaving and lodged it against the seat so small it almost vanished. He reseated the plate, tightened it down, ran the test cycle once. The return motor whined, hesitated, clicked back. On the gauge the pressure dropped wrong.

"There," he said. "Now it's ugly."

"You can fix it later."

"If later comes before they impound the car, sure." He held up the untouched replacement seal in its plastic. "I'm not burning a good part unless I've got to. But I'm tagging this assembly for replacement anyway. Inventory will show I pulled one from stores and returned it unused. That means questions."

Marisol watched his hands move: grease black under the nails, motions economical even when he was committing a crime. He handled the valve with the same plain efficiency he brought to a bearing change, accepting load and consequence together. In his hands the red tag became one more part that had to be seated right the first time.

He keyed his mechanic ID into the tablet and began dictating. "Aft blackwater return valve. Intermittent seat obstruction under load. Incomplete closure on cycle. Release unsafe pending clean-out and manual verify." His voice on the log was flat, almost bored. That frightened her more than if he had whispered. Boredom was how durable lies got written.

He signed. The system stamped his number under hers.

The lie left her body then and became infrastructure. It lived in another worker's badge, another department's ledger, another future meeting where men with polished shoes would decide whether a red tag had been justified. Marisol felt that change with almost physical clarity. The first lie had been panic wearing procedure. This one was alliance, whether she deserved the word or not.

"That's it?" she asked.

"That's enough to hold them if nobody gets ambitious." He peeled a red tag, looped it through the housing, and pulled it tight with his teeth. "If Teresa gets ambitious, we're both fucked and I'll still have grease under my nails in the disciplinary photo."

He stood, joints cracking, and looked at her directly for the first time since she'd said Gabriel's name. "Does he know?"

"That I'm doing this? Yes."

"That you're asking me to?"

"No."

"Good. I'd rather he hate me for the right reason later."

Marisol almost smiled and could not make it happen. "I didn't ask you because of old times."

"I know. Old times never paid a pension anyway."

He shouldered past her toward the platform steps, then stopped and pulled the folded crayon bird drawing out from under the heron magnet. He slipped it into his breast pocket before anyone else could splash solvent on it. Only then did he head up to present the fault.

That small motion undid her more than the signature had.

By the time Marisol got back to the holding lane, the mechanical cross-check had already hit the board. HOLD - SANITATION / WASTE RETURN FAILURE. Dispatch would hate the pairing. Compliance would hate it more. The officers hated it immediately, because it converted waiting into procedure. Nobody reboarded while a waste return sat red-tagged.

The detainees had been shifted into a fenced service strip beside the car, shaded only by the train itself. Heat lifted off the concrete in visible curls. Officers had clipped a long waist chain through the adults on one end and seated the children against the wall on the other where a portable fan pushed chemical air over them. Somebody had passed out paper cups of water too small for anything except argument. The toddler who had been screaming before was quiet now, face slick and stunned with heat, asleep against his mother's thigh in a position that looked painful for both of them.

A little boy with only one shoe had gotten hold of a grease pencil and was coloring the exposed side of the wall until an officer noticed. Two women argued in low exhausted voices over whose property sack had come back missing a sweater. The cropped-haired girl with the repaired wristband sat cross-legged and counted knots into a bread-twist wire, flattening each turn with the blunt pad of her missing-nailed thumb before starting the count over.

Gabriel leaned against the fence with his wrists clipped in front now instead of behind, because somebody had made the reasonable decision that people waiting in heat got uglier when they could not swat flies. His shirt was damp between the shoulder blades. The old scar through his left eyebrow went pale when he looked at her.

She took a property-inventory board from the nearest officer, tucked it against her chest, and started down the line with her eyes on the grid, checking sacks, verifying contamination losses, and accounting for what the hold had turned from possession into waste. It gave her an excuse to stop in front of each person without seeming to stop for Gabriel alone. It also made her look directly at what she had done.

The board filled with the same blunt entries: blanket contaminated, pillow contaminated, one left shoe contaminated, inhaler pending, family photo pending, two cloth diapers contaminated, cheap paperback destroyed in bleach. A woman asked whether she would get her dead husband's letters back dry or wet and Marisol had no honest answer. The inventory box had only PRESENT, DESTROYED, or UNKNOWN.

When she reached Gabriel, he did not move off the fence. "You made everybody sit out here," he said.

His voice was low, almost conversational. That made the line harsher, not softer.

"You'd be moving already if I hadn't."

He looked past her to the toddler, to the old man hunched over a specimen bag behind his knees, to the woman fanning herself with the ruined manifest paper. Then back to her. "Don't sell me the discount version."

Marisol kept the board between them and ticked a false mark she could not later read. "I bought time."

"You bought them time too."

"For what?"

"For another manifest review. Another change of cuffs. More chances to lose a name." He tilted his head toward the stripped-open car. "And for that kid to piss himself because your deep clean shut the lavs."

The words landed without any old family theater in them. Gabriel was looking at the toddler, the specimen bag, the woman with the paper fan, and that made the point harder: the hold had a body count too, measured in smaller units than death.

"I know what it costs," she said.

"No. You know what it costs when the company does it. That's different. When you do it, you still want credit for meaning well."

She almost snapped back at him then, something ugly about keeping him off a port he had never seen, about how meaning well was not the phrase anybody on this line would use for her. But the officer two places down had turned away to answer a radio, and Gabriel's right hand came off the fence long enough to press a folded strip of waxed wrapper paper against the bottom edge of the inventory board.

"Take it," he said.

She pinned it with two fingers without looking down.

"What is it?"

"Berths that don't match the file labels. This car and one in 7. Maybe more after they reshuffle. Follow the bunk tags, not the names. The names get cleaned faster."

The wrapper strip sat warm against her palm, slick with his sweat, exactly his kind of evidence: memory cut down to something that could disappear into a fist. Marisol slid the wrapper into the cuff of her glove and moved her body half an inch to cover the exchange.

"How'd you get these?"

He gave her the smallest glance toward the holding lane, toward the heat-stunned cluster of people she had helped create. "You made them wait. Waiting is when people compare paperwork."

Two places down, the cropped-haired girl was still working the wire, tightening each twist before easing it flat against her palm. When Gabriel said "people," his eyes went there briefly and then away again. Marisol understood. Not all of this had come from him.

"I can't fix every file from a platform hold," she said.

"Then don't pretend the hold itself is the fix."

An officer started back toward them. Gabriel let his expression go blank so fast it was like a door shutting in her face.

She should have moved on. Instead she heard herself ask, very quietly, "What do you want from me?"

Even asking felt obscene. She had spent years translating care into competence because competence at least left a record. Want was another matter. Want had made her stupid before. Want had made her clean scenes she should have left dirty. But the question was out now, hanging between the inventory board and the fence.

The question narrowed him into precision.

"If you're going to do this again, do it for something bigger than me getting one more bad hour in a different place." His words stayed low and quick, the cadence of somebody used to making speech survive surveillance. "Get records. Get tags. Get word between cars. Use the time so it belongs to somebody besides them. And if you come back asking me to walk out alone, don't. I won't."

He glanced once more down the line. "Not while they keep the rest of the car on the bill."

The officer was close enough now to catch tone if not content. Marisol dropped her gaze to the inventory board and dragged one gloved finger down the column until it landed on a random sack number. "You think I can turn a sanitation hold into something bigger?"

"I think time with no use is just another room they built."

The officer stopped beside them. "Problem?"

Marisol flipped the board. "Missing sweater. Property discrepancy."

Gabriel said nothing. His silence could pass for hatred, which on this line was ordinary and therefore useful.

The officer grunted and moved on.

Marisol stepped to the next detainee in line, but the wrapper paper inside her glove had already changed weight. She could feel the recorder in her pocket on the other side, still stolen, still unheard. Together they felt like evidence against the fabricated assignment, names pressed up against the mechanism that erased them. Her hand smelled like bleach and hot paper.

Behind her, the service radio on the wall clicked and Ivo's voice came through with that same infuriating plainness: "Return assembly remains unstable on manual verify. Hold until repeat cycle and flush." There was no drama in it and no hint of favor, only a mechanic doing his job badly on purpose and well enough to make it stick.

One of the cleaners staggered past carrying another bagged mattress skin toward incineration, Tyvek pasted to his back with sweat. The day-care fee on his cracked phone would be climbing. The custody sergeant was already on a second peppermint, jaw working like a man grinding money between his teeth. The woman with the manifest fan had stopped fanning and was simply staring at the concrete. The old man with the bag between his knees did not look up at anyone.

Marisol finished the inventory sweep because that was what the role required and because stopping halfway made suspicion. She marked destroyed what had not yet been destroyed, present what she prayed might turn present later, unknown where the truth could not be reached in time. By the time she handed the board back, the car behind her was gutted to metal and the hold had swollen beyond anything she could call clean.

At the platform edge, she looked up at the departure board again. Ivo's number sat under the mechanical tag and hers under the sanitation order, red beside red. The system had room now for both lies and none for mercy.

She folded the wrapper strip smaller and slid it into the pocket with Gabriel's recorder. The two objects clicked softly against each other when she walked. Around her the train kept extracting payment for the hour she had stolen: from the workers in sweat and late fees, from the detainees in heat, wet clothes, and vanished property, from Ivo maybe in August when men in clean offices would decide whether his service record had remained acceptable enough to let him grow old less poor. When dispatch finally let the steel move again, Marisol rode south in the service alcove, and that debt rode with her in her pocket beside Gabriel's recorder.


# Chapter 6

South of Caldera, at Marsh Crescent, the outage took the fans first, then the strip lights, then the little courtesies that made custody look orderly.

The emergency bars along the corridor floor glowed a bad hospital green. Everything above them sweated in the dark. White wall panels that looked clean by daylight showed their seams at night, rust pushing through the paint in hairline blooms. The train had been standing still for almost an hour on the marshland siding, nose angled into black water and reeds, with the traction line dead and the backup system doing just enough to keep locks live and nothing else. There was no refrigeration in the galley, no proper air, and no false sense that movement itself was mercy.

Heat sat inside the cars like a second load. It pressed Marisol's shirt flat against her back and worked salt into the chemical burn scar on her wrist until the skin there prickled. Mosquitoes worried at the torn patch of screen over the crew vestibule window, whining so close to her ear that more than once she slapped at nothing and hit the metal wall. Beneath her boots the floor no longer carried the steady travel hum of the line. Instead it gave her the slow settling sounds of stalled tanks and strained couplings: a hollow shift in the blackwater below, a tinny pop from cooling bolts, somewhere farther down a latch knocking softly every time a body leaned against the wrong door.

She sat on an overturned waste drum in the service alcove with a flashlight, a dead comm tablet, and Gabriel's recorder laid across her thigh. The little machine looked cheap enough to have come out of a corner pharmacy, except Gabriel had wrapped a band of white labeling tape around the back and written a date block in his careful square hand. The adhesive had gone gray with pocket lint. There was dried something on one corner of the case, not blood, maybe coffee, maybe the syrup from canned fruit. She had been carrying it since Caldera, first in the inside pocket of her supervisor jacket, then in the locked drawer where she kept spare seals and incident blanks, then in her bra for the walk between cars when the audit trail on property storage started feeling too easy to read. She had stolen it because it was his. She had not listened because once voices entered a room, they changed the room's size.

A child cried twice in the family bunks and was hushed by somebody too tired to soothe for long. From one of the locked cars came the rubbery sound of a cough that would not clear. A guard swore under his breath when his flashlight clipped the jamb. Then the corridor went back to the marsh and the insects and the heat, those old patient things that needed nothing from policy.

Marisol should have been making another round. She knew the numbers already. Toilet load on Cars 6 through 8 was edging up because people kept drinking warm water and then losing it. Trauma room sink was low but usable. The portable bleach tank in sanitation was down to a quarter and the mop heads would sour by morning if the air stayed dead. The deep-clean hold at Caldera had bought time and cost all of them sweat, overtime, suspicion, and a harder push south once dispatch got the line moving again. That was the arithmetic she understood. The sequence in her head stayed operational: delay here, pressure there; vent this, seal that; carry the spill forward; do not call attention to the part of the machine that has started to smell like itself.

The recorder rested under her palm with a dull trapped warmth, as if it had its own body heat.

Paloma appeared in the doorway with two paper cups pinched between the fingers of her steady hand and a basil leaf stuck to the damp front of her scrub top as if it had ridden there all shift. Her braid had half come loose, silver threads escaping along her neck. The tremor in her right hand showed only when she transferred one of the cups to Marisol, a fine shiver at the wrist and then gone.

"Water with salt," Paloma said. "Don't ask me to call it cold."

Marisol took it. The cup was wet and nearly warm. "How bad?"

"For now?" Paloma leaned her shoulder against the frame and listened to the train the way other people listened to weather. "One toddler has heat rash over his whole back because his mother thought the synthetic blanket meant official and kept it on him too long. One old man is trying to faint with dignity. A girl in Car 7 bit a guard because he wouldn't let her mother hold the sick bag. I admire her, medically speaking."

"Any real emergencies?"

"You mean any emergencies that count on paper." Paloma sniffed once and pushed the basil leaf off her shirt with the back of her wrist. "Not yet. If the line doesn't come back before dawn, the family bunks are going to turn into a kennel and then into a clinic."

She tipped her chin toward the recorder. She did not ask where Marisol had got it. That was one of the things Marisol loved and resented in her oldest friend. Paloma could make room around a fact without pretending not to see it.

"You should at least sit by a doorway," Paloma said. "You're going to bake in here."

"I'm fine."

"You're not fine. You're just vertical." Paloma rubbed at the bridge of her nose with a knuckle that smelled faintly of camphor. "If dispatch wakes up and starts lying again, send someone for me before they decide sweat isn't a health issue."

Then she was gone, soft-soled and compact, carrying her tired body back toward other people's fevers.

Marisol drank half the salted water, which tasted like the rim of a cheap pot, and set the cup on the floor. The recorder waited.

Gabriel had handled evidence since before she understood he had built a life out of it. When he was fourteen he used to rescue labels from jars and stack them on the kitchen table to soak the glue off in a saucer, because he said paper deserved a second use if it had not lied yet. She had yelled at him then for making a mess in the only place they could eat. She could see the room now with the kind of accuracy that came only when she was trying not to think about it: the worker-housing kitchenette with one drawer that never shut flush, the fan in the window coughing hot air back at them, rice crusting in the cooker because she had picked up an extra wipe-down at the corridor clinic and got home too late, Gabriel bent over a thrift-store cassette player with a butter knife because he had lost the screwdriver again. She had come in still smelling of ammonia and old urine under the bleach, and he had looked up and asked whether dead batteries died all at once or got quieter first. She had not answered. She had gone to the sink and scrubbed her hands until the skin over her knuckles looked boiled.

Now she thumbed the play button.

For a second there was only hiss and the marsh's damp pressure around it. Then Gabriel's voice came through the tiny speaker, drier than it sounded when he had been standing in front of her with restraint marks on his ribs, but unmistakable. He tapped the casing once, a habit she remembered from all the cheap electronics he had tried to keep alive past their usefulness.

"Field file fourteen," he said. "South shelter overflow, loading week. First names only unless you want the rest on record. If you want me to stop, say stop. If you want water first, say that too."

There was a rustle, cloth or wrapper paper. Then a woman's breath, close enough to fuzz the mic.

The speaker's tin grain made Gabriel sound farther away than a few locked cars, already half translated into evidence. Even so, he started with water, with permission to stop. Marisol could not remember the last official question on this train that had offered a body that order.

"All right," Gabriel said. He spoke softer now. "Start wherever it sticks."

The woman laughed once, without humor. "It sticks in my pocket."

She gave her name as Amparo. Her voice had the grain of somebody who had spent years speaking over machinery and children both. Somewhere behind her on the recording a baby kept making the wet, irritated sound of a child too hot to cry hard. Amparo said they had let her keep the pocket things until intake because the first officer on the bus was lazy and the second one was flirting with a woman from records. She listed them slowly, as if inventory might make theft reversible.

"A bus token with the blue heron on it. One key with chipped green polish because it used to belong to my sister and I can never tell mine from hers by touch. Half a cinnamon stick in foil for my stomach. A receipt from Mercado Las Torres with soap, tomatoes, and powdered milk. The corner of a school picture where my girl is laughing before they tell her to stop showing all the gums in front. I had folded it small. Very small. Smaller than a finger joint. They gave me back the key because they said a key is harmless if the door is gone."

Marisol pressed her thumb into the raised seam of the burn scar on her wrist until the sting sharpened. Intake liked to call this stage property control, as if the right phrase could turn pocket-litter theft into procedure. She had watched officers shake receipts, prayer cards, antacid packets, and children's barrettes into trays with the bored care of people clearing drains.

She stopped talking. On the tape the baby started up again, then hiccupped itself quiet.

"What did the tag say?" Gabriel asked.

"Santa Avelina."

The name tightened the back of Marisol's throat. Outside, something brushed the torn screen and the mosquitoes changed pitch, a thin electric whine. Santa Avelina had started as salt-swollen paper in her closet; now the line used it like bleach, pouring it over whoever they wanted to rename clean.

"Do you have anyone there?"

"I've got a dead grandmother who once knew a man from there. That's how much I've got there."

Marisol sat so still that sweat gathered under her knees and ran down the backs of her calves. She knew the property bags Amparo meant. Clear plastic with a white strip for barcode and initials. She had signed for cartons of them. She knew the way intake clerks reduced a life into the categories on the preprint: jewelry, paper, medicine, miscellaneous. She had watched them mark family photos as paper and inhalers as plastic and wedding bands as personal metal as if the right noun made confiscation tidy.

At home, when Gabriel had been little, she had turned his pockets out over the bathtub before wash because if she fed screws or coins into the machine downstairs the super took the repairs out of the laundry deposit. She remembered marbles. Bread ties twisted into chains. A dead beetle in a matchbox with holes poked through the lid. Once, a whole tangerine gone soft and furry in the pocket of his school pants because he had decided to save it for later and forgotten. She had stood there after a double shift peeling moldy fruit from cloth with two fingers while he slept face-down on the foldout couch, his heel hanging over the edge, the room thick with the bleach smell she carried home no matter how long she showered. She had thought then that the difference between work and love was simple. At work you cleaned what strangers left in their bodies and went back the next day. At home you cleaned what your own people dropped without meaning to and called that ordinary life.

On the recorder Amparo said, quieter, "My daughter thinks Santa Avelina is made up because the name sounds painted on."

Gabriel did not fill the silence too fast. That was part of his skill, and hearing it made something bitter move in Marisol's chest. He had learned how to wait better than she had. On the line, silence was usually where lies went to breed. Gabriel had turned it into room.

"What's your daughter's name," he asked, "if you want it kept with yours."

"Lidia Mendez Ruiz," Amparo said immediately, like a hand slamming down to keep a paper from blowing away. "Write it properly."

The tape clicked again, brief and deliberate, while he marked something down.

Marisol looked at the blank back of an incident form on her lap, the one she had meant to use if the toilets overflowed before power returned. Without thinking too hard about it, she uncapped her grease pencil and wrote:

Amparo

Lidia Mendez Ruiz

The wax dragged on the paper fibers. Her handwriting looked too official for names.

She let the tape run.

The next voice belonged to a man who cleared his throat first as if about to enter a church argument. He called himself Leandro and then apologized for his breathing, which Gabriel told him not to do. You could hear from the edges of his words that he was smiling while angry, a trick Marisol knew from dockworkers and debtors and men who had learned not to hand fear over cleanly. He said he had fired brick for nineteen years inland and had never once stood close enough to salt water to smell it right.

"The officer spun the monitor and asked the room what they thought I looked like," Leandro said. "Like it was a fucking game. One of them says my hands are boat hands. These are kiln hands. Look."

There was a scrape, maybe him holding his palms up uselessly to a machine that could not see.

"Then the woman with the silver nails says Santa Avelina takes men with prior maritime family linkage, and I said I don't have prior maritime anything, I've got a cousin who drowned in an irrigation trench and an uncle who sold knockoff fans from the back of a truck, and she says, close enough, there's a surname packet attached."

"Did they show you the packet?" Gabriel asked.

"No. They showed me my own face on the screen like that settled it. Like if I looked tired enough I might have come from anywhere."

He laughed then, a short dry bark that turned into coughing. When he came back he sounded older.

"Write this part down. They chose a country because somebody liked the shape of the word in their mouth."

Marisol closed her eyes.

Tom Avelar had kept his papers in a round butter-cookie tin with a lid that no longer fit right. Dock license, vaccination card, union dues receipts from the months when there was union work to be had, Gabriel's birth certificate folded into quarters, and at the bottom the Santa Avelina parish record from a grandfather none of them had ever met. Salt had swollen the edges of all of it. After Tom died she could not bear to throw the tin away, partly because there were still practical uses for the documents and partly because grief, when you were broke, had to live in containers you already owned.
