Years later somebody on the line had scanned the parish seal and turned the old paper into a clean legal hook. A dead man's family folder had become a route code. She had kept that tin in the back of her closet beside extra bleach tablets and winter socks. She had never once imagined the train would reach inside it and come back with her son.

On the tape Gabriel asked, "What do you want beside your name?"

Leandro answered without hesitation. "That I make brick, not boats. That my daughter hates dust storms and chews the collars off her shirts. That I can't swim."

Marisol wrote his name too. Then the daughter: Ines, age nine, chews shirt collars. She did not know whether that detail belonged in a record that could save anyone. She wrote it anyway because it was the only truthful thing that had entered her hands all day.

The outage held. Outside, something big moved in the reeds and left a dragging sound through the shallows. The train answered with a shudder from the couplings, a complaint traveling through forty locked tons held still on bad temper and dead power. Sweat had dampened the collar of her uniform enough that the fabric rasped when she turned her head. Somewhere overhead a mosquito found the warm hollow behind her ear and fed until she slapped it, smearing a small crescent of blood she could not tell was hers.

She thought about the deep-clean order at Caldera, the way she had signed it with flat competent strokes and watched the crew wheel in the disinfectant drums. She had told herself delay was neutral, or at least neutral enough. Time was time. An extra six hours before departure. An extra six hours to call, to think, to improvise, to save Gabriel from the paperwork built around his father's name. But while she had been buying those hours, families had stayed longer in strapped vinyl seats. Children had slept longer in the heat. Guards had stayed on shift long enough to get mean in that unfocused tired way that left bruises for stupid reasons. There was no clean delay inside this machine. There was only where the weight landed.

The tape hissed, clicked, moved on.

"This one?" Gabriel said. "You can tell it or your mother can."

"I can tell it." The voice that came next was high and careful, not babyish, a child working hard to sound old enough to be believed. "You have to not say I cried a lot."

"I can leave the count off," Gabriel said, and Marisol could hear the smile in him even through the static.

"No, say I cried some, not a lot. My name is Luz Elena. I'm eight and a half."

There was a pause while someone in the background murmured that she was seven. Luz Elena snapped back, "I'm eight in November and that's halfway." Then, more composed: "They tied my mother first because she wouldn't let go of my brother's shoe. The foam smells bad after somebody throws up on it. It smells like orange cleaner and old spit and the pennies after you hold them in your hand all day. They turned the pad over but the smell stays in the middle."

Marisol saw the foam at once. Gray restraint blocks with wipeable covers, inventory code stenciled in black, always advertised in company training as hygienic because the outer skin did not absorb. That was only true if you cared more about surface than what lay warm underneath.

"Did they clean it before you had to lie there?" Gabriel asked.

"They wiped the top. Not the crack part." Luz Elena lowered her voice as if sharing a recipe. "The crack part keeps it."

Behind the child, a woman made a sound so tired it was almost a laugh.

"And the tag?" Gabriel asked after a moment.

"Mine says Santa Avelina too, but my mother says that's not where my grandmother came from and anyway my grandmother's dead, so she's not available to pick us up."

Gabriel breathed in through his nose, let it out slow. "Do you want your brother's name in too?"

"Mateo," Luz Elena said. "He throws one sock away every time he's mad, so if he gets there with only one sock, that's still him."

Marisol put the recorder on pause because the service alcove had tilted under her, just enough to make her reach for the wall.

The metal beside her shoulder was slick with condensed heat. It smelled faintly of wet dust and the cheap lemon compound they used on public-facing surfaces when investors rode along. She kept her hand there until the car settled. Her body still expected the old sway and had to correct when the floor stayed still.

Then somebody shouted from the family bunks, into the dark without reaching for a radio. "Supervisor. We need a bag. Now."

Marisol shoved the recorder under the dead comm tablet and went with her flashlight and the half roll of absorbent pads from the alcove shelf. The corridor floor bars turned every shin bone green. In Car 7 the young guard with the bitten hand stood uselessly in the aisle, thumb wrapped in gauze, a paper sick bag clenched in his good fist while a woman on the lower bunk tried to hold a little boy upright over a blanket already slick with vomit. The child had brought up everything he had and kept heaving anyway, orange water and ropey spit, each convulsion knocking his forehead against his mother's collarbone. A girl beside the ladder, maybe ten, watched the guard with her mouth set hard enough to bite again.

"Why is that still in your hand?" Marisol asked.

He lifted the bag a little, defensive even now. "She threw it."

"Then let her throw the next one full." Marisol held out her palm until he gave it over. "Get me two more and a mop head that doesn't already stink."

He muttered something about protocol and moved because route supervision still carried in her voice. Marisol passed the bag to the mother, stripped the soaked blanket down to the vinyl, and pressed absorbent pads into the seam where the sour liquid had run. The bunk had already taken the smell into its stitched middle: stomach acid, orange cleaner from some earlier wipe-down, the sweet rot of heat. The mother kept saying, "I'm sorry, I'm sorry," every time the boy gagged. Marisol told her to save her breath and hold him higher. The girl stayed planted by the ladder, watching every move like she was memorizing who got to touch what.

By the time the guard came back, too slowly, with the extra bags and a dry mop head, the blanket was in a leak sack and the boy had gone limp with the ugly quiet that came after vomiting. Marisol told the guard to fetch Paloma and to leave the bags with the family this time. She waited until he nodded before she left. The smell stayed behind her anyway. It lived in the bunk seam now.

Back in the service alcove, her hands carried sour heat even after she wiped them on sanitizer gauze. Maintenance had never only meant getting people to morning. It meant preparing damage for transport, drying it, bagging it, and naming it something smaller than what it was so the train could claim dispatch-ready status and the company could invoice another clean departure.

She started the tape again.

Voices came now in shorter cuts, maybe because Gabriel had been interrupted, maybe because he had been forced to record fast. A woman named Soraya whose whole contribution was, "If they put me under another dead man's paperwork, then write that I still have my own teeth and they're mine." A teenager laughing while he described braiding bread-bag ties together because you could not confiscate what looked like trash. Somebody in the back asking if the recorder could catch accent and Gabriel answering, "Enough for someone to know you didn't come from where the tag says." Each fragment left a body-shaped dent in the air.

Marisol wrote as much as she could catch: names when they gave them, details when the names blurred under static, own teeth, bread ties, can't swim, one sock missing on purpose.

The grease pencil softened in her fingers from the heat. Wax collected under her thumbnail. By the time the battery warning light blinked red on the recorder, the back of the incident form was full and she had started on a waste-log duplicate from the clipboard pocket. It was obscene, maybe, to use company paper for this, but then obscenity had been the house style of RailPassage for years. Everything the line did was built on the premise that the right form could launder almost any fact.

The last clear stretch of tape carried Gabriel's voice alone for a moment. He must have thought he had stopped recording or else meant to leave himself in as a marker. His breathing was close to the mic. Then he said, not to a witness exactly, maybe to the room, maybe to himself, "If they change the destination, start with the name again. Start there every time."

The recorder clicked dead.

From the cars ahead came a brief wave of sound, collective discomfort passing through bodies at once, a step short of panic. A baby. A curse. Somebody hammering twice on a locked door with the flat of a hand. Then quiet gathered over it again, thin and ugly.

Marisol looked at what she had written. The names sat on the page in the same upright script she used for bleach requisitions and injury summaries and seal-breach reports. Official handwriting. Work handwriting. She hated that. She hated that even here, even alone, the first tool available to her was the same one she used to make pain legible to payroll and invisible to conscience.

But the paper was not empty anymore.

Amparo, with the key that opened nothing here.

Lidia Mendez Ruiz, gums showing in the photo.

Leandro, kiln hands, can't swim.

Ines who chewed her shirt collars to threads.

Luz Elena, eight and a half by her own rules.

Mateo, one sock gone when angry.

Soraya, her own teeth.

More half-caught names crowded the margins, some with question marks because the static had eaten a syllable, some with only a bunk note or a detail that might have to stand in until she heard them again. It was not enough. It would never be enough. The train carried hundreds. The line had carried thousands before this week and would carry thousands after unless something happened large enough to force the truth out where it could not be cleaned up before inspection.

She had spent days telling herself the story in the smallest possible way: Gabriel on her line, Gabriel under bad paperwork, Gabriel needing time. The recorder had broken that scale. If she kept risking anything on this train, it could not be to pry one son out of hundreds. It had to be for the people the line kept trying to flatten into tags, bunk numbers, and transportable mess.

Marisol folded the pages once, careful along the crease so the grease pencil would not smear too badly, and slid them inside the plastic sleeve behind her dead comm tablet. The hiding place would not survive scrutiny. It only had to survive the next round. Her body already knew the motion from years of tucking unofficial things inside official ones. She hated that knowledge too.

Outside, the marsh breathed its rotten-sweet breath through the broken screen. The mosquitoes kept coming. The train sat in the heat with its locks alive and its people awake inside it.

Marisol picked up the recorder again though the battery was dead, just to feel its weight. Then she rose from the waste drum, knees stiff, shirt damp, the salted water gone metallic at the back of her tongue. There was another round to make. There were children in hot bunks, an old man trying to faint politely, guards getting stupid with fatigue, tanks filling under the floor. All of that remained true.

At Car 7 the girl by the ladder was still awake, face washed green by the floor bars, watching the aisle as if she had been posted there. Before Marisol checked the bunk seam or counted the remaining sick bags, she took the waste-log duplicate back out and asked for the boy's name. The girl gave it. Marisol wrote that down too, folded the damp duplicate into quarters, and slipped it inside her waistband before taking her flashlight on to the next car.

The train could make them cargo by dawn. It did not get them nameless first.


# Chapter 7

The waste-log duplicate with the boy's name on it stuck damp to Marisol's thigh as she came down the service stairs at Caldera Junction to a platform that already looked stripped for evidence.

A folding compliance table had been opened under the tin awning where crew usually smoked after shift. Two portable printers sat on it beside sealed evidence envelopes, a biometric reader, and a sweating cooler of bottled water that nobody had been invited to touch. Teresa Ordonez stood behind the table in an immaculate white shell uniform that had gone dark under the arms anyway. Her makeup held. Her cuticles did not. She kept worrying one bitten thumb with the edge of a thumbnail while a junior audit clerk beside her peeled at the blister on her heel through the slit in a brand-new shoe.

The train had rolled in forty minutes earlier with hot axles, a night of outage stink still trapped in the connector joints, and blackwater sloshing low in the tanks. Marisol had not slept. She still had mosquito bites up both forearms from Marsh Crescent. The dried salt on her collar rubbed like sandpaper each time she turned her head. She saw the compliance table, Teresa, the security pair in dark vests, and knew the deep clean had finished becoming a story.

Crew were lined up along the awning posts with their passcards already out. No one was pretending this was routine. Ivo stood halfway down the row, heavy shoulders bent, grease under his nails still black after a scrub. He had a paper cup of machine coffee in one hand and the folded court letter about his child-support review peeking from his shirt pocket because he read bad news more than once when it did not change. Paloma had come over from the trauma room in a fresh gown already damp at the spine. Her silvering braid was still pinned tight, but wisps had escaped around her ears. The room-smell of camphor and disinfectant came with her into the open air.

Teresa saw Marisol and did not raise her voice.

"Supervisor Anaya. At last."

There were people all along the platform, but Teresa made the two words sound private and disciplinary at the same time. Marisol crossed the concrete. Heat came up through the soles of her shoes. Somewhere under the cars a pump kicked, failed to catch, and kicked again.

"We had a rolling-stock outage at Marsh," Marisol said. "The connector fans died and Car 8 backed up on blackwater alarms. We came in hot."

"You came in late," Teresa said. "You came in with medical timestamps that do not match corridor footage and sanitation holds that do not match dispatch notice. Give me your card."

Marisol had imagined arguments on the ride in. She had built them out of valves, doors, temperatures, the old competence that had gotten her promoted one rung at a time over twenty years of mess nobody else wanted. None of those arguments survived the sight of Teresa's compliance table and the crew lined up to watch. Marisol slid her passcard from the lanyard and set it on the table.

The junior clerk took it with both hands, thumbs pinched at the edges, too careful for a plastic card. The blister on her heel had burst and bled through the nude stocking into the shoe lining. She fed the card into the reader. Green lit first. Amber followed. Then a hard red bar crossed the small screen.

Teresa put on her reading glasses, discovered they had been in her breast pocket all along, and looked down at the tablet.

"As of now," she said, "your credentials are reduced to sanitation-supervision access on this route only. No manifest editing. No property-room access. No red-seal restraint inventory. No unscheduled car transit beyond sanitation and service corridors. If you need to enter a custody or medical zone, you will do so under escort and with written justification attached to a time stamp. Blackwater readiness now requires secondary sign-off from compliance until further notice."

No one moved. Teresa's words landed one latch at a time, sealing doors inside Marisol's body. Manifest editing was gone. Property-room access was gone. Red-seal key authority was gone. She could not walk the length of the train because she knew where she had to be. She could not drift near Car 8 on a pretext. She could not reach Gabriel on a lie that looked enough like work to pass.

"That's not necessary," Marisol said. "You know what the Marsh segment does to the clocks when power drops. We had detainees vomiting in the heat. We had a restraint line jam at the trauma threshold. I logged what mattered."

Teresa looked up. Her face stayed composed, but the patience had thinned enough for the plain woman under it to show through.

"What mattered was chain of custody," she said. "What mattered was that a route supervisor did not create thirty-nine loose minutes around a delayed car already flagged by dispatch. You do not get to decide, after the fact, which minutes matter."

The people along the awning kept their eyes lowered in the way workers did when they wanted to see everything. Marisol could feel them taking stock of the new balance. A supervisor being corrected was one thing. A supervisor being publicly unlocked from her own authority was another. It rearranged the air.

Teresa picked up the card and laid a narrow orange strip across the printed supervisor band. The adhesive went on crooked. She peeled it back, straightened it with irritated precision, and pressed again.

"Wear this visibly," she said. "If any door logs you outside your temporary scope, security will respond, not me."

She handed the card back. Marisol clipped it to her damp shirt. The plastic hit her sternum with no weight at all, which was worse than if Teresa had hung a brick there.

"Director," Marisol said, because the crew was listening and she would not beg by first name in front of them. "Car 8 still needs an experienced hand. That crew knows my voice. We have passengers who calm faster when they know the same person is coming through the door."

"Then they will have an opportunity to demonstrate flexibility."

Teresa tapped her tablet twice. One of the security officers stepped forward. He was a broad man Marisol knew as Barrera, a custody sergeant who wore a pink elastic around his wrist for his little girl's braids and snapped it against his skin when he was bored. He handed Teresa a transfer slip without speaking.

Teresa read from it.

"Case 4471-R is being moved to restraint tier three pending reconciliation review and contact-irregularity assessment."

Marisol felt the platform tilt half an inch under her shoes. She kept her face level because Barrera was watching and Teresa would enjoy less than a flinch. She had known it might come to this the minute the audit table appeared. Knowing a blow was possible did not take the shape out of it when it landed.

"On what basis?" she asked.

"On the basis that your route has already demonstrated poor boundary discipline," Teresa said. "On the basis that a delayed case with disputed ancestry documentation now carries elevated exposure. On the basis that I do not intend to explain another preventable irregularity to investors during heat season."

The transfer team was already at the connector. Marisol heard cuffs before she saw them: metal against metal, a drag and answer drag through the tunnel between cars. Gabriel came into view between two officers in dark vests. His hands were boxed in black transport mitts attached to a waist chain. The bruise along his ribs showed through the scrub shirt where sweat had darkened the cloth. Somebody had split the scab at his left eyebrow again during the move. Blood had run a clean thread down the side of his nose and dried there. He was thinner than the first day she saw him in intake. Or maybe higher-security restraints simply drew the body tighter, pared it down to what could still be controlled.

He did not turn his head much. That was the part that told her how bad the new tier was going to be. Gabriel always looked where he wanted, even when it cost him. Now he kept himself forward because he knew exactly how far a guard's patience reached inside a transfer tunnel.

The service door nearest the connector had always opened for Marisol. She had spent years walking through it with mop handles, med coolers, meal bins, broken latch reports, things that made the route keep pretending it was just a difficult workplace instead of a machine for moving human beings out of sight. When Gabriel's chain caught briefly on the threshold rail, she stepped toward the door on reflex, card already in hand.

The reader flashed red. The lock did not click.

The sound was small, a dead electronic refusal, but it carried. Barrera's mouth moved at one corner. Teresa did not smile. She only said, "The door is functioning correctly."

Gabriel must have heard that. He lifted his face then, not fully toward her, just enough for the old scar to catch the light and for Marisol to see recognition pass over him like contempt held under discipline. Then Barrera jerked the chain loose and the team took him on into the higher-security car, where the mesh partitions were narrower and the water came through timed nozzles because even thirst had to be metered when the company had decided someone was a management problem.

Marisol stood with her useless card in her hand until the connector doors sealed.

Teresa closed the tablet cover.

"You are not suspended," she said, giving the words the careful shape of mercy. "I advise you to understand the difference."

Marisol looked at the orange strip on her badge, at Barrera's pink elastic, at the junior clerk shifting weight off the blistered heel she would have to squeeze back into her shoe for the ride home. Everybody on the platform had some petty damage strapped to them. That was part of how the line kept running. The damage did not make Teresa gentler. It only made her better at pricing what other people could afford to lose.

"What do you want me doing?" Marisol asked.

"What you should have been doing already," Teresa said. "Keep the route sanitary, keep your paperwork exact, and stop treating procedure as a place where you can improvise a personality."

She moved on to the next worker. The audit did not need Marisol's humiliation any further. It had entered the crew on its own.

Marisol stepped away from the table because standing still would have looked like pleading. Ivo did not meet her eyes as she passed. Paloma did, and what Marisol saw there was not pity. It was the flat alertness of someone in a medical room who has just heard the wrong monitor change pitch.

***

By noon the heat had turned every metal surface in the trauma room tacky under the hand.

Marisol was there because sanitation still opened for her. Paloma had called down for a mop and absorbent powder after a transfer patient vomited over the edge of the treatment cot. Under the new rules, it took a custodial escort to get Marisol through the first medical door, but once she was inside the room the officer stayed out in the corridor, grateful for any excuse not to breathe the place.

The patient was a man in his fifties with graying stubble and one church shoe still on, black leather polished by habit if not by current circumstance. The other foot wore only a thin transport sock turned inside out at the heel. His name on the chart rack was Rufino Salcedo. He kept apologizing every time pain made him louder.

"Forgive me," he said through clenched teeth as Paloma cut the shirt away from his shoulder. "Forgive me, doctora. I know you have work."

"Shut up and breathe," Paloma said, not unkindly. "Apologies do not reduce dislocations."

Rufino gave a wet laugh that broke in the middle. Somebody had dragged him hard enough during the tier transfer to pull the humeral head half out of the socket. His right shoulder stood wrong under the skin, a pointed little geography of damage. There was also a split on his lower lip and a ridge of raised flesh where the transport cuff had chewed the wrist. He smelled of old sweat, cheap soap, and the cumin that had gotten into the seam of his church shoe years ago and never left. Marisol wondered who packed formal shoes for a transfer corridor. Then she saw the careful shine on the toe and understood that Rufino had not packed them for the corridor. He had packed them because some habits were the last dry things a man could carry.

Paloma glanced at the med drawer, then at the clock over the sink, then at the chart. Her right hand trembled once in the air before she steadied it with the left.

"Hold him," she said.

Marisol set the mop aside and came to the cot. Rufino's skin was furnace-hot. When she braced his chest, she could feel the little skipped catches in his breath where pain kept trying to turn breathing into something smaller.

"This will go fast," Paloma said.

"No, no, wait," Rufino said. "Please, just tell me when."

"Now."

Paloma pulled the arm down and out. The shoulder went back with a thick, damp clunk that Marisol felt through the mattress frame. Rufino screamed once, high and young, and then bent sideways to vomit bile over the cot rail. Marisol got her hand under his forehead before he hit metal. The bile splashed her sleeve and ran warm into the seam at her wrist, right over the old chemical-burn scar. Outside in the corridor the custody officer swore and took two disgusted steps farther away.

Rufino sobbed twice without tears. Then the pain changed shape. Marisol knew that kind of change. A body never got kinder after a joint went back in. It simply stopped being one emergency and became another.

Paloma reached for the locked med drawer.

The officer at the door leaned in just far enough to make a rule.

"Director froze controlled pulls until audit clears," he said. He had a strawberry cough drop bulging one cheek and the red outline of a wedding ring on a finger that had gone bare for shift. "No ketorolac, no tramadol, no nothing without compliance sign-off."

Paloma kept her hand on the drawer.

"He needs pain control."

"Then call compliance."

"Compliance can come listen to his shoulder for the next four hours."

The officer shrugged. The gesture required no imagination at all: a tired man with a cough drop and an order that cost him nothing personally. Original cruelty had to be maintained. This kind traveled cheaper, hand to hand, as transfer of burden.

"Chart it," he said. "If they clear it, they clear it."

He stepped back out before Paloma could answer. The trauma-room door swung almost shut, leaving only the strip of corridor light and the smell of hot uniforms.

Rufino had gone gray around the mouth. He whispered, "Please," not theatrically, not even very loudly. He sounded embarrassed to still exist at the center of the room.

Paloma opened the chart and stared at the last entries. The corridor printer had already spit a transfer strip across the top margin: 11:12 - threshold handoff, unsigned under the barcode where custody had left the rest blank.

"What time did they bring him in?" Marisol asked.

"To my door? Eleven twelve." Paloma looked at the clock, then the empty signature box. "Custody didn't close the intake line until eleven thirty-eight. The drawer freeze posted at eleven twenty."

Rufino was shaking now, not from fear alone but from the body discovering fresh pain all at once now that the shoulder sat where it belonged. Paloma's braid had come loose another inch. Sweat tracked down the side of her face into the collar of the gown. Marisol knew that look on her. It was the look Paloma got when a person was being treated as paperwork first and tissue second.

"Don't," Marisol said quietly.

Paloma turned.

"Don't do what?"

"Don't do anything for me."

That made Paloma give her a very brief, exhausted smile.

"This isn't for you."

She pulled the drawer, counted by touch, drew the dose, and set the syringe on the tray. Then she took up her pen and wrote in the chart with neat, balanced letters that did not hurry even though the lie had already begun. Marisol saw the pair of lines form.

11:14 - patient received to trauma cot, under observation pending intake signature.

11:17 - ketorolac IM administered after transfer injury, observation continued.

Paloma capped the pen. Her hand trembled after the fact, not during.

"If compliance asks," she said, "he crossed my threshold before the freeze. Let custody explain why they took twenty-six minutes to catch up with his body."

Marisol looked at the chart, then at the syringe.

"You don't know that helps me."

"I know exactly what it helps."

Paloma gave Rufino the shot. He flinched, cursed softly, then apologized again because apology was apparently lodged in him deeper than pain. Paloma slapped a cotton square over the injection site and held it there longer than necessary.

"Stop saying sorry," she told him. "Save your mouth for breathing."

Rufino nodded. The tremor in his jaw eased by degrees.

Paloma stripped the gloves off and dropped them into the bio bin. She did not look at Marisol while she spoke next.

"He's your son."

Marisol did not answer. The room had the hot, chemical stillness that came after vomiting was wiped but before the air admitted it had changed. Water knocked in the pipes somewhere above the ceiling panels. Rufino had closed his eyes, either from relief or from the need to leave the room without moving.

"You don't have to tell me," Paloma said. "I saw your face when they moved him. I've known you too long to mistake that kind of stupidity."

There was no point pretending now. Not with the chart still wet, the syringe wrapper open, Rufino half sedated on a lie Paloma had chosen for reasons larger than friendship and smaller than revolution.

"Yes," Marisol said.

Paloma nodded once. The motion carried diagnosis, not surprise.

"All right."

That was all she gave the fact. She did not gasp or offer pity or make a speech about blood. She took the soiled pillowcase from under Rufino's head and rolled it inward to keep the bile contained.

"I wondered why you would risk housing debt and your own job for one detainee you barely knew," she said. "It's uglier, but it makes sense."

Marisol leaned both hands on the cot rail. It had rust pitting under the paint near one bolt head. Someone had cleaned around it for years without ever getting the rust out. Her whole life on the line seemed to sit inside that little red-brown bloom: managed, never removed.

"I didn't ask you to cross for me."

"I crossed for his shoulder." Paloma looked at her then, tired eyes hard. "And because if Teresa starts pulling every loose minute around that car, she won't stop at you. She will crawl through my drawer counts, my retainer, my visa file, every basil plant I've ever tried to keep alive in that room. So don't make the mistake of thinking care and self-interest are enemies. Most decent things on this line are both."

That sounded more like Paloma than anything noble would have.

Rufino stirred and touched the toe of his church shoe with the inside foot, checking that it was still there. Even half drugged, he did not want to lose it.

"Thank you, doctora," he murmured.

"If you thank me again, I will reduce the other shoulder out of spite," Paloma said.

Rufino almost smiled.

Marisol picked up the mop and cleaned the bile properly because work was still the shape her hands understood. Paloma filed the chart in the rack with the fresh lie buried inside it among temperatures, pulse rates, and intake times. When the floor was done and the absorbent powder had gone pale with fluid, Marisol rinsed the mop head in the slop sink. Gray water spiraled, hit the trap, and went on down the route she knew better than any prayer.

Paloma was washing her hands when she said, "Listen to me."

Marisol turned.

"If you're going to keep doing whatever this is," Paloma said, very quietly, "you don't get to do it like a mother running on instinct. Instinct is what gets people dragged between cars. You do it with numbers, timing, and an exit for the innocent, or you stop."

Marisol thought of Gabriel disappearing into tier three with the mitts on his hands. She thought of the red flash on the door reader and Teresa saying it was functioning correctly. She thought of Rufino trying to apologize his way out of pain.

"I heard you."

Paloma dried her hands. The tremor was back now that the choice had been made and could not be unmade.

"Good," she said. "Take that shirt to laundry. If audit sees bile on your sleeve, they will chart even your vomit against me."

***

The all-hands meeting happened in the crew cafeteria at the worst hour of the afternoon, when the roof trapped heat low over the tables and the vending machines glowed like exhausted altars.

Nobody had enough appetite for the steam-table food. They still took trays because shifts had taught them to eat when eating was possible, not when hunger arrived. Marisol sat with a sweating cup of station coffee and a bowl of rice that had gone stiff at the edges. The orange strip on her badge made people look twice before they looked away. Worse than formal demotion, it pinned visible uncertainty to her chest.

Teresa stood near the condiment counter with a printed memo in her hand and her glasses on top of her head where she had forgotten them again. A floor fan turned beside her, clicking on one bent blade. Workers filled the room in patchy silence. Ivo took the chair across from Marisol but angled himself half out into the aisle, knees already set to leave at the first bad sentence. Lina Paredes from laundry sat two tables over, blue fish-market gloves still stuffed in her back pocket from the station side job she worked on weekends. She had a crescent of silver hair dye at one temple where she had done her own roots in a bathroom mirror and missed a spot. She kept smoothing the same folded housing-office notice on the table, worrying the creases flat with two fingers. The amount printed on it stayed the same.

Teresa did not waste time pretending the meeting had a benevolent purpose.

"You all know we are under route-integrity review," she said. "What some of you may not know is how quickly repeated delays become payroll consequences."

The room changed at once. It did not get louder. It got tighter.

"The corridor benchmark for heat season allows a very narrow band of discretionary overtime before recovery review begins," Teresa continued. "If this route misses recovery targets, overtime will be restricted to director-approved emergency labor only. Housing subsidies tied to corridor employment can also be recalculated at recovery rate rather than concession rate. Contract renewals and visa protections attached to active line service may then be reviewed in sequence."

No one had to ask what reviewed in sequence meant. It meant first the hours, then the room, then the paperwork that let a person keep the room. It meant the line reached into your paycheck and your mattress with the same hand.

Lina spoke without raising her hand. Fear had taken care of protocol.

"Recovery rate starting when?"

Teresa looked at her.

"Next cycle, if we do not recover."

Lina pressed the folded notice flatter. "My mother sleeps in that unit. She has the fan by the window because of her lungs."

"Then I suggest all of us take recovery seriously," Teresa said.

She said it with that managerial softness that always carried the shape of threat more efficiently than shouting would have. Marisol watched the room absorb it. A dishwasher stopped scraping trays. Somebody at the back whispered a curse and then did not repeat it. Ivo sat very still. When he got frightened enough to calculate, he stopped joking entirely.

Teresa kept going.

"I know many of you support extended family. I know summer earnings matter. They matter to me as well. But I will not permit undisciplined holds, undocumented favors, or personal improvisations to put this route into a spiral that harms the very workers who keep it operational."

By then everybody in the room knew that Teresa meant Marisol's undocumented favors and personal improvisations. She never said Marisol's name. She did not have to. The orange strip hanging off Marisol's chest had already become a paragraph.

"Any worker who observes deviations in timekeeping, med pulls, sanitation notices, or custody movement is expected to report them up the correct chain," Teresa said. "That is not disloyalty. That is the difference between earning through summer and watching payroll claw back what your families already spent."

She folded the memo once and set it on the condiment counter beside the mustard pump. The meeting was over. There was no vote and no question worth asking. There was only information delivered in the language the company trusted most: rent, hours, visas, mothers on fans by windows.

People did not rise immediately. They stayed where they were a few seconds longer, the way detainees sometimes stayed seated after a door opened because movement had become too costly to waste. Then the room came apart into low talk.

Lina looked straight at Marisol across the aisle.

"Was it your car?" she asked.

There it was. The grammar carried no accusation. It carried no deference either. It was just a worker trying to locate the hand around her own throat.

Marisol set down her cup.

"My car had the hold," she said.

"And now they want two hundred more off my check if they call it recovery." Lina's voice stayed level, but she kept rubbing the housing notice with her thumb until the paper softened. "I can take a late train. I can't take my mother down three flights in August because somebody wanted to prove a point."

Marisol could have denied, soothed, gone procedural. She knew all the old supervisor lines. We're working it. Payroll always overstates first review. Let me check the numbers. None of them would have sounded like truth in that room.

"I know what they're threatening," she said.

"Do you." Lina stood up with her tray still full. "Because when they threaten you, they threaten a salary. When they threaten me, they threaten a door code."

She left before Marisol could answer. Her fish-market gloves knocked against the back of her thigh with each step like a small, practical metronome counting labor nobody saw in the official chart.

Ivo watched her go.

"She's not wrong," he said.

His voice stayed low, almost conversational, which made it land harder. Marisol looked at the court letter in his pocket.

"I didn't say she was."

"No. But you still have the habit of thinking fear makes people stupid. Usually it makes them exact."

He stirred his coffee with the handle of a plastic spoon because the spoon bowl had cracked off. Grease sat under the nails, the neck burn scar stayed half hidden by collar, and the mechanic's gait showed even while he was sitting still. All of him seemed built from things under strain.

"Do you think I caused this?" Marisol asked.

Ivo considered that longer than comfort required.

"I think somebody made Teresa look slow," he said. "I think she billed that feeling to the rest of us before the hour was over. I think the housing office has been waiting all summer for a reason to pretend their deductions are neutral math."

He gave her a machinist's answer, all force transfer and load path: the strain moved, and nobody stopped carrying it just because the blame changed hands.

"And you?" Marisol said. "What are you waiting for?"

He let the cracked spoon handle go. It spun once in the coffee and stopped.

"A number I can live with."

There was a whole sad life inside that line: pension vesting, court review, overtime, the child whose support letter he reread like prayer or punishment. Marisol had asked him for one lie already. She heard, in the space after his answer, exactly how expensive a second one might be.

At the back table two sanitation attendants stopped talking when she looked over. One of them, Tomas from pump maintenance, kept a folded city bus map in his wallet because he still did not trust the corridor app to tell him when service had been cut. The other, Celia Morn, had a lunch bag covered in cartoon strawberries because her granddaughter had declared plain black too depressing for work. Both of them glanced at the orange strip on Marisol's badge and then away, quickly, not wanting to be caught being caught.

Marisol knew that look. Detainees gave versions of it when rumor moved faster than orders. Workers gave it when payroll notices hit. Hatred would have run hotter. What she saw instead had the chill of quarantine: people measuring distance from a thing that might spread.

"They think I'm radioactive," she said.

Ivo snorted once without humor.

"No. Radioactive pays cleanup premiums."

For a second she almost smiled. Then he rubbed the bridge of his nose and added, "They think you're the sort of problem management will solve by taking it out of their rent."

That was closer.

The fan clicked. Trays knocked. Somewhere outside, a coupling slammed hard enough to rattle the condiment packets. Teresa walked past the cafeteria windows with two audit staff behind her, already moving on to the next room she intended to make orderly by threat.

Marisol looked down at her badge. The orange strip had already started lifting at one corner from sweat. Under it, half hidden, was the old supervisor band with her name and clearance level still printed like a memory of authority the machine no longer recognized.

She took her untouched bowl to the return rack and went back out into the corridor. The route smelled of fryer oil, bleach, wet uniforms, overheated plastic, and the thin sour note of fear that no cleanser ever quite got. Her downgraded card opened the sanitation cage on the first try. The light went green there. Inside were bleach tablets, mop heads, absorbent powder, all the things the company still trusted her with because they cleaned up after other people's decisions.

Beyond the cage door, down the corridor that led toward the restraint cars, a pair of attendants saw her and lowered their voices. One stepped aside to let her pass, too quickly, with the nervous speed of people who thought proximity might end up on their bill later. Marisol kept walking.

What Teresa had done at the compliance table was simple enough: take access, narrow movement, make an example. That was management work. It had rules. What settled into Marisol now was uglier because it did not belong only to Teresa. It belonged to every worker whose room key, overtime slip, visa stamp, or parent on a fan by the window could be priced against one more unexplained delay.

She had known from the start that management would come for her if she touched the schedule. She had not understood until this afternoon that the line had trained the rest of them to come away from her too.

At the far end of the corridor a red security light pulsed over the sealed door to tier three. Her card would not open it. Neither, now, would the old habit of being needed. She stood there a moment anyway, listening to the vibration under the floor, the timed hiss of water somewhere behind the door, and the clipped synthetic announcement starting up again in English and Spanish, thinking of Gabriel in the narrower mesh, of Rufino trying not to lose one polished shoe, of Lina smoothing the housing notice flat with the side of her hand.

When she turned back toward the sanitation cage, workers coming off shift made space for her without touching, leaving her alone with the downgraded card at her chest and the red light over tier three. That was the part that stayed with her. It was not Teresa's speech or the orange strip. It was the space they left.


# Chapter 8

At the sealed tier-three door, the amber light on Marisol's downgraded passcard blinked twice before the custody officer lost patience and slapped his own badge over the plate. The lock released with a flat metal cough. Heat pushed out of the seam hard enough to salt the inside of her nose. Somebody had already vomited. Bleach sat over it and did not win.

The announcement was still repeating in the car's clipped synthetic English and Spanish, the same four lines cycling over the ceiling speakers until the words stopped meaning anything except that they were happening to bodies: full biometric reconciliation, aisle compliance, prepare tags, remove nothing from your berth. The floor thrummed under her boots with the long steady vibration of southbound speed. The car swayed left, then right, and the portable scan units answered with little irritated chirps every time their handlers had to reset their stance.

This was Teresa's answer to the audit discrepancies: scan the whole car again, wash the paperwork through a second machine, and call it truth.

Marisol stepped in sideways because a line of knees already clogged the first third of the aisle. The air was body-hot, wet with old breath and overheated plastic. Children had been pulled down from upper bunks too early and were now drooping against mothers' thighs or staring at the scanners with the glassy patience of the over-tired. Men in wrist restraints sat along the right side under the windows, tags hanging from their necks. Families and overflow detainees were stacked into fold-down bunks on the left, three high, blankets kicked into ropes. White paint had split along the berth seams and rust showed through in freckles and tears.

Gabriel was four rows in, on the right, one wrist clipped to a floor ring because Teresa's audit had promoted him from inconvenient son to known problem. Fresh yellowing bruises sat under the darker ones on his ribs where the intake team had grabbed him days ago. He had lost more weight in one week than Marisol wanted to count. When he looked at her he did not look surprised to see route supervision present for the rescan, only tired and alert at the same time, already reading the order in the room before it reached her in words.

Across from him, berth 8-C, Naila Quispe was sitting cross-legged on the lower bunk with her back against the wall panel and a strip of silver wrapper flattened over her thigh. Someone had once printed tropical fruit on it in cheerful orange. She had folded the strip into narrow lengths and tied little overhand knots down the middle, one after another, enough to make a rough counting cord. Two fingernails were missing from her left hand, old damage, the nail beds grown over shiny and pale. She kept touching the knots with her thumb, not nervously, just checking where she was.

She had also counted the room faster than anyone else.

Marisol saw it happen in the first twenty seconds. Naila watched the guards instead of the scanner. She watched which one favored his right knee when the car lurched, which one had his baton already unsnapped, which handheld reader was still wrapped in dock plastic because nobody had finished the setup before they rolled. She tipped her chin once toward the backup unit, once toward the sweating hands of the first detainee in line, then toward the vent above the door that was blowing air too warm to dry anything.

"Left reader misses more," Gabriel said, his voice low enough to pass for a cough.

Naila did not turn her head. "When the car leans east."

"That's not east," said the young woman with the toddler at her knees, because fear made some people precise about stupid things. She had a cherry-red plastic comb shoved sideways through her braid and a stain of mashed banana dried on the shoulder of her shirt. Her little boy was barefoot except for one sock with the fox rubbed nearly blank at the heel. He kept trying to put his fingers into the oval holes of the berth ladder.

Naila glanced at him and hooked the ladder one rung higher with her foot so his hand could not get pinched if the car jolted. "Then when it leans window-side," she said. "Keep his hands off the metal."

Marisol knew better than to step into a car full of people and imagine silence meant passivity. Silence was work. Silence was counting. She also knew what a rescan in a moving car could do once fear got traction. The aisle was too narrow for clean force and too crowded for clean anything. If the restraint team had to come in shields first, somebody would crack a skull on the berth frame before they even reached the back rows.

"This doesn't need to turn into a scene," she said, keeping her voice pitched for the nearest bunks and the nearest guard at once. "Tags ready. Hands dry if you can. We get through it and we're done."

Naila looked up then, finally, and Marisol understood why Gabriel had been listening to her. There was nothing theatrical in the girl's face, no hunger for martyrdom, only stillness and, behind it, an arithmetic that had already run ahead.

"Hands dry?" Naila said.

She lifted her palm. Sweat shone in the center line.

"You felt the air in here."

The first scan failed with an angry beep. The guard holding the reader cursed and wiped the thumb plate on his sleeve. The detainee, an old man with one lens missing from his glasses, flinched before he realized nobody had hit him. A child laughed once, too sharp, then covered her own mouth because everyone else was quiet.

Naila bent to the woman with the fox-sock boy. Her voice dropped lower. Marisol only caught pieces.

"Not first."

"When I touch your shoe."

"Sit heavy. Not sideways."

The wrapper cord vanished into the woman's fist. A second strip of foil passed upward without hands seeming to move. Gabriel said nothing. He did not need to. He was watching the aisle with the fixed, recording attention he gave destination codes off freight.

Marisol moved closer to him under the pretext of checking restraint spacing. "Don't start this in motion," she said.

Gabriel gave her the shortest look. "They did."

"You know what I mean."

"I know exactly what you mean."

Up front the second scan failed too. The reader chirped again. The guard slapped the side of the device. His partner, a narrow man with sweat moons under the arms of his vest, barked the next berth number louder than he needed to. Volume would not dry skin or clean the audit trail Teresa was scrubbing in real time.

Naila slid off the bunk when her number came. She did it so neatly, one foot then the other, that Marisol thought for a split second she had abandoned whatever she was calculating. Then Naila put both knees down in the middle of the aisle and sat back on her heels.

She settled there with the plain economy of someone setting a load where it would hold. At the narrowest point between the first scanner and the next row of bunks, she laid her hands flat on the tread plate.

"Stand," said the guard with the reader.

Naila shook her head once. "No."

He reached for her elbow. Before his fingers closed, the young mother with the comb sat too, one arm behind her for balance, the fox-sock boy shoved up against the bunk support behind her knees. Then the old man with the broken glasses lowered himself more slowly than either of them, muttering because his left hip did not want to bend. Then two boys from the upper berth slid down and folded themselves cross-legged by the drain seam. Somebody in the back said, "Here too," not loud, just enough, and bodies started dropping in place along the aisle the way sacks settle when a load strap is cut.

The effect was worse than shouting. Shouting gave security a single source. This turned the whole car into an obstruction.

The guards stopped pretending it was one girl with bad timing. One unclipped his baton. Another hit the intercom tab at his shoulder and said, "Car C aisle refusal, multiple noncompliant, request extraction team."

Marisol's mouth went dry in the wrong place, at the back by the hinge of the jaw, the place bleach fumes always caught after too many hours in the tanks. She could feel her left knee already calculating the narrow turns she would need if the team came in fast.

"Nobody touch anyone yet," she said.

The guard nearest her gave a little incredulous laugh. "Supervisor, that decision went by you."

Maybe it had. Maybe that was the point.

The synthetic announcement cut off. For one clean second the car held only the floor-vibration and the breath of eighty people trying not to waste air. Then Teresa's real voice came over the speaker, polished even through distortion.

"This is Director Ordonez. Aisles must remain clear for verified custody movement. You are creating avoidable risk for yourselves and for minors in the car. Stand now and the review remains administrative."

Nobody answered her. Naila kept one hand on the floor and lifted the other just enough to tap the tread plate twice. Marisol heard it pass back in metal through the berth frames. Two more people sat, and then three more did.

Administrative, Marisol thought. Teresa kept choosing the noun to make a baton in a sealed car sound like paperwork.

She looked at the door, at the custody officer waiting outside it, at the red status light already live above the frame. There was still time to keep the restraint team out if she could turn delay into procedure before Teresa turned procedure into force.

Time was what she knew how to buy.

* * *

By the time Teresa reached the car door, the refusal had climbed almost halfway back through the bunks.

Marisol made herself the bottleneck before Teresa could make herself the solution. She rolled the sanitation caddy crosswise into the entry lane, yanked the lid off the top compartment, and splashed two inches of stale bleach solution across the threshold grate with enough wasteful urgency to make the threshold look contaminated. The smell hit at once, hard and false-clean. Then she crouched and started unscrewing the drain cover with her multi-tool, giving the grate the fussy attention she reserved for a failure point that could take off a foot.

It bought her twenty seconds before Teresa realized she was not hurrying.

Teresa appeared in the doorway with her tablet in one hand and her reading glasses shoved up into her hair, where she had plainly forgotten they were. Her uniform looked impossibly dry for this part of the train. Behind her, three restraint officers in white hard-shell vests waited with shields stacked against their thighs. One of them was chewing clove gum. Marisol could smell it even through the bleach.

"Supervisor Anaya," Teresa said. "Move that cart."

Marisol did not look up. "Threshold grate is loose on the right bolt. If a shield edge catches it, somebody loses a foot."

"Is that documented?"

"It is now."

Teresa's gaze flicked to the open compartment, the tool in Marisol's hand, the acid-white puddle feathering out over the metal lip. When she spoke again her voice was still level, but the consonants had hardened.

"You were instructed to witness a reconciliation event, not create a contamination theater."

"This car has two children on floor level, one toddler, three elderly bodies with mobility problems, and a live scanner error rate high enough to trigger duplicate reads. If you run a charge through here, you will be lifting people by whatever comes away first."

Teresa stepped closer. "Then clear the aisle."

"Give me five minutes."

"For what."

That was Teresa at her coldest: not a question, but a demand that the next sentence declare itself worth existing.

Marisol straightened enough to face her. "For the heat to come down, for those readers to stop throwing false misses, and for me to get the mothers standing without you cracking a child against the bunks."

Teresa's eyes moved past her into the car. She was good at that, Marisol had to give her that much. She saw the room in terms of liability spread: where the camera would catch a bruise, which injuries would look accidental if the report language stayed narrow, and which worker in the doorway could still be made to absorb the blame.

"You are visible in this obstruction," Teresa said. "Think carefully about whether that is your intention."

Behind Marisol, somebody farther back began whispering berth numbers in Spanish. The numbers moved like water under a door. Naila had her head turned now, not toward the front, but toward the families on the upper bunks. She kept touching shoulder, shoe, rail. A boy with a split lip already swelling from something earlier climbed down and sat beside the old man with the broken glasses. Gabriel shifted in his floor clip and said, not to Marisol, not to Teresa, "Close the gaps. Don't leave holes for boots."

The line tightened. People on the bunks started folding their legs in instead of leaving them dangling into the aisle, making room for the next body to sit. Someone passed back a half-empty water pouch. Someone else tucked a sleeping baby higher into the crook of a blanket so she would not roll.

There was no speech, no chant, only labor.

Marisol understood then that whatever was happening had already moved past her. She could affect timing. She could narrow the shape of harm, maybe. Leadership had already passed into the room's own hands.

That should have made the next choice easier. It did not.

If she stood aside and let Teresa send the restraint team in now, the car would break all at once. If she openly took the side of the refusal, there would be witnesses in the room but not outside it, and RailPassage would call the whole thing coordinated employee misconduct before the blood dried. If she stalled and stalled and stalled, maybe the order would climb high enough to get deferred to Caldera, where there were junction cameras, cafeteria workers, maintenance crews, commuters crossing the north platform. It would not be justice or even safety, only more eyes, more angles, and less ownership.

It was a thin plan. She took it because it was the only one she had in reach.

"You want this car readable?" she said to Teresa. "Then stop heating it up. Those face lenses can't hold a lock if the people in front of them are sweating into the scan ring and the car is pitching."

"The train is not pitching."

The car rolled through a turnout and the whole corridor gave a short ugly heave. One shield banged the door frame. The officer with the clove gum swore and reset his footing.

Marisol pointed at him. "That. Exactly that."

Teresa's mouth flattened. "Move the cart."

"Five minutes."

"You are not in a position to bargain."

"I'm not bargaining. I'm telling you what breaks first."

The custody officer by the door shifted, waiting for Teresa's nod. Marisol saw it in the corner of her eye and dropped the drain cover back into place with more force than needed. The clang rang through the car like a dinner pan in a prison tier. Heads turned, which was good. She wanted them to see her, and she wanted Teresa to see them seeing her.

Marisol lifted her voice, not toward Teresa now, but toward the seated line.

"Anybody with a child, keep the child up on the bunks if you can. Keep fingers away from the floor seam. Shoes on. No loose straps in the aisle."

It sounded like crowd control because it was crowd control. It also gave the refusal a minute more to organize itself around protecting smaller bodies. The young mother with the comb hauled her little boy backward between her calves and shoved his one good sock onto his foot with jerky angry motions. Naila nodded once at Marisol, not gratitude, not alliance, simply acknowledgment that the instruction was usable.

Teresa heard it too.

"That is enough," she said.

She put her glasses back on with one irritated motion, though they had been in her hair the whole time, and read from the tablet in the scrubbed cadence she used when she wanted language to disinfect what was about to happen.

"This is your final instruction. Clear the transport path or corrective extraction will proceed under family-preservation protocol and medical review."

"Family-preservation," Gabriel said from the restraint ring, and laughed once, short and ugly.

Teresa did not look at him. "Mr. Avelar, you will remain silent."

"Wrong name."

One of the officers took a step forward. Marisol rolled the sanitation caddy another six inches into the lane so he had to stop or knock it over.

"Don't do this in here," she said.

Now Teresa did swear, the first crack in the shell. The word came out low and private, escaping because reality had finally stopped honoring her vocabulary.

"Move it, Marisol."

For a second Marisol thought Teresa might actually say her full name the old way, the sponsor's way, the way she used to when a younger Marisol had been useful and grateful and too busy rising to ask what she was rising inside. Instead Teresa handed the tablet to the custody officer, extended one immaculate palm, and said to the restraint team, "Minimal tools. Pull from the front. Get the aisle open."

The white-vested officers raised their shields.

From the floor, Naila spoke for the first time at full hearing distance.

"Heads down," she said. "Children high. Lock your arms."

Then the team came in.

* * *

The first hard sound was a shield cracking against a berth edge, polycarbonate on steel because the entry lane was too narrow for a clean line. The second sound was the sanitation caddy tipping and spilling gray mop water over everybody's boots. Then the batons started hitting metal, bench legs, hands, whatever moved in front of them.

Marisol grabbed the fallen cart by one handle and yanked it aside before someone went over it face-first. The officer with clove gum shoved past her with his shield up at throat height. She hit the side of it with her forearm and felt the shock all the way into the old chemical burn on her wrist. Another officer came lower, driving the shield into knees. Bodies folded. Somebody screamed immediately. Somebody else started praying in a language Marisol did not know.

"Lift them," one of the guards shouted.

"You can't lift a packed aisle," Marisol shouted back. "You'll stack them."

No one listened because the point had already stopped being movement. The point was to teach a lesson.

Naila had wrapped one arm through the bunk ladder and the other around the old man's shoulders to keep him from taking the shield edge full in the chest. The missing nails on her left hand flashed wetly as she tightened her grip. She did not kick or swing. She made herself harder to separate. That alone enraged them. The officer in front drove the shield into her shoulder once, twice, trying to break the hook of her arm. She kept her head tucked and said, "Hold," so quietly Marisol almost missed it under the noise.

The young mother with the comb had turned half around, making a wall with her own body so the fox-sock boy could stay behind her legs. When the shield hit her, she slid but did not let go of the berth post. The officer with the baton struck down at her wrist, not wild, not blind, precisely where her grip was.

Bone made a small thick sound under the skin. Her hand went sideways.

She screamed from somewhere so deep in the body it sounded torn out rather than produced. The boy began shrieking too, not words, just the raw animal note children hit when they have seen a body become wrong in front of them. Her broken hand bounced once against the tread plate, fingers still trying to close on nothing. The cherry comb fell out of her braid and skittered under the bench.

Marisol dropped to her knees beside them before she had thought it through. The tread plate was hot through the fabric of her trousers. She shoved the child backward onto the lower bunk, got one hand under his ribs and one behind his head, and yelled, "Keep him up. Keep him off the floor. Don't let him touch her wrist."

The mother tried to curl around the arm and could not. Vomit came up pink and stringy and splashed onto Marisol's sleeve.

Above them the charge had already gone jagged. One officer was trying to drag two seated boys apart by the backs of their collars and succeeded only in slamming their faces into each other. The narrow guard with the sweat moons had his baton caught in a blanket knot and was cursing as an elderly woman bit his thumb through the glove. A scanner went down and skated under the bunks, still beeping under there with petty faith in administrative accuracy.

Gabriel had gotten one foot under him against the restraint ring and was hauling uselessly at the clip on his wrist. "Take me," he shouted. "If you want the organizer, take me."

A guard backhanded him across the mouth for that. Blood jumped bright onto his chin.

Marisol did the ugliest thing first because it was there. She pried the young mother's good hand away from the broken one and pinned both elbows against the berth support so the woman would not keep wringing the fracture into itself. "Look at me," she said. "Look at me. Breathe on the count. One, two, three, out. Again."

The woman's eyes were wide and furious and almost gone with pain. "My son."

"He's here."

"Don't let them."

"I know."

It was not enough to know.

A shield edge clipped Marisol's shoulder blade hard enough to drive her into the berth frame. Sparks ran up her neck. She heard Teresa at the doorway talking about proportional response and line of movement in the tidy cadence of a meeting. Then Naila cried out for the first time.

Marisol turned in time to see the officer with the clove gum wrench Naila's arm free of the ladder and fling her sideways. She hit the opposite berth with the side of her head. The metal lip opened the skin above her eyebrow in a fast clean line. Blood came at once, sheeted into her eye. She tried to push herself up. The officer struck her with the baton across the upper back and then again across the shoulder when she did not flatten fast enough.

That was when people stopped trying to remain seated and started trying not to let others be taken.

The aisle became one body with too many elbows. Hands came out from under benches. Someone grabbed a shield rim from behind. Someone else threw the half-empty water pouch and it burst against a visor, making the officer flinch and jerk his head away, blinded for one stupid second by plain water. The old man with the broken glasses went over onto his side and disappeared under knees. A teenage girl on the upper bunk started kicking the wall with both heels, not to injure anyone, just to make noise, terrible noise, enough to wreck command.

Marisol lunged for Naila because Naila was closest to the boots now. Her knee failed halfway down. Pain shot up the familiar metal-stair path from kneecap to hip and she nearly pitched onto the floor herself. She caught the berth rail with one hand, shoved with the other, and got her body over Naila's ribs just as a guard came forward to stamp the girl's wrist loose from the ladder rung she had found again by instinct.

The boot hit Marisol's thigh instead.

"Supervisor," the guard said, shocked enough to hesitate.

"That's a medic load now," Marisol barked, pure command voice, the one that had once moved crews through blackwater spills and burst valves. "Back off if you don't want brain injury on your incident sheet."

It worked for one beat because it was work language and because she was old enough in the corridor to still sound official when she chose the right verbs.

One beat was all she got.

Teresa saw her covering Naila and understood the scene for what it would look like later. "Pull Anaya clear," she said at once.

Hands grabbed Marisol under the arms. The chemical burn scar on her wrist scraped against a shield edge and lit up white-hot. She kicked back, connected with somebody's shin, and heard a grunt. Then she was on her feet, dragged two steps toward the front while the whole car rocked under the weight shift of too many struggling bodies.

In those two steps she saw enough to know what her delay had purchased: preparation.

The team had come in with more shields than the first call required. The custody officer in the doorway was already filming body positions on Teresa's tablet. The back rows, who might have stayed confused if the charge had come earlier, had used the time to join and were now taking the same blows in larger number. The children had mostly made it onto the bunks because Marisol had yelled early, and for that she would be grateful later in the cheap private accounting that let people survive work like this. But the smallest bodies were still not safe. They were simply seeing everything from a slightly higher angle.

Gabriel was shouting her name now, not Mama, not anything soft enough to let her hide. "Marisol. Marisol."

He was handing her witness, the one task she could still do right.

The officer holding her loosened his grip because the car lurched again. She twisted free and shoved back into the aisle, not toward Teresa but toward the old man, who had finally been dragged clear enough to breathe but not clear enough to sit, toward the mother, whose boy was trying to crawl off the bunk to get to her, and toward Naila, who had rolled onto one elbow and was blinking blood out of one eye while keeping the other on the people behind her, still counting even now.

"Under the seats," Naila said hoarsely to the children. "When they swing, under."

She stayed practical to the last inch.

By the time the restraint team got the front half of the aisle nominally open, the floor looked like a butcher's sink after a rushed rinse. There was water, blood, vomit, and silver wrapper strips mashed flat under boots. The portable scanner had been retrieved and wiped on a guard's pant leg. Teresa gave the order to hold position, not to stop. The distinction sat in the car like another weapon.

People were crying now, some from pain, some from the humiliation of being made audible. The refusal had not broken cleanly. It had been beaten into pieces.

Marisol stood in the middle of it with somebody else's blood drying tacky across her forearm and knew with perfect accuracy how RailPassage would code this by nightfall: transient noncompliance, minor corrective force, one family-preservation injury, maybe two if Paloma refused the downgrade hard enough.

* * *

Paloma came in six minutes later with a trauma bag, a splint roll, and her silvering braid half out of its tie from running the length of the train. Camphor and disinfectant came with her. So did her right-hand tremor, small but visible when she snapped on gloves. She took one look at the aisle, found the bodies losing blood and air first, and braced the tremor by pinning the trauma bag against her thigh.

"Who can't breathe?" she said.

No one answered first because everybody thought the answer had to be somebody else.

Paloma pointed. "You. Blue shirt. Ask him again in Spanish. You, move your boot. If she vomits on her back I will let her aspirate in your custody report and use your full name."

The guard moved his boot.

Marisol got the fox-sock boy off the berth and onto her hip because he had finally gone limp enough to cling rather than thrash. He smelled of sweat, old formula, and the sweet rot of hot upholstery. His mother, Celia, lay against the bunk support with her broken wrist cradled in the crook of her shirt. The deformity was obvious now, the hand dropped wrong at the joint, swelling fast around the intake band. Each pulse seemed to pump the skin tighter.

"Her name is Celia," Marisol said.

Paloma's eyes flicked up once, and that was good; Celia had become a name, not a category.

"Celia," Paloma repeated, crouching. "Look at me, querida. I'm going to touch the wrist. I know. I know."

Celia's lips peeled back from her teeth. "Don't let them take him."

"Nobody is taking him from my sight for the next minute," Paloma said. Then, because fear had tipped over into emergency and her stress had roughened her speech exactly the way Marisol knew it would, she added, "And if anybody tries, they can fight me for him."

The boy buried his face in Marisol's neck. His remaining fox sock dragged against her hip. She bounced him once, awkwardly, because it had been years since she had held a child that size and because everything old in the body came back stupidly fast. One of his tears ran down under her collar. She felt it cooling against the heat trapped in her uniform.

Across the aisle, Naila sat propped against the berth frame while blood streamed from above her eyebrow and down the side of her nose. It had pasted her cropped hair to her temple. Somebody had tried to hand her a wad of blanket to hold to the cut, but she had given it instead to the old man with the broken glasses, who was using it on his mouth where a tooth had sliced him from inside. Even now she was not hoarding what worked.

Marisol crossed to her when Paloma had Celia's arm stabilized between her knees.

"Hold this," Paloma said without looking up, and jammed a packet of gauze into Marisol's free hand.

Marisol crouched in front of Naila. Close, the girl looked younger and older at the same time. She was nineteen in the bones of the face, older in the way she had already learned not to waste words on pain that would not stop because it was named correctly.

"Press here," Marisol said, folding the gauze over the cut.

Naila did not take it. "The old man first."

"He's got pressure."

"He'll swallow blood."

"So will you."

Naila considered that, then let Marisol press the gauze in place. Her skin was hot as pipe metal left in sun. Under the blood and sweat Marisol caught a faint smell of sugar from the fruit wrapper the girl must have hidden earlier, saving sweetness for the right moment or the right child.

"You saw them stack outside," Naila said.

She stated the fact flatly.

"Yes."

"Next time count the door, not the scanner."

Then she closed her good eye and turned her face into Marisol's hand, not from trust, Marisol thought, but because pressure hurt less than bleeding.

Teresa was still at the front of the car, now dictating into the tablet for the incident log. Her glasses had slipped down her nose. One cuticle bled where she had worried it open without noticing.

"Corrective entry executed after repeated lawful instruction," she said. "Medical contractor on scene. Juvenile bodies preserved from trampling by immediate staff intervention. Rescan to resume in controlled order once the aisle is secured."

Gabriel laughed again, then spat blood onto the floor by his own boot.

"Controlled order," he said. "There it is."

Teresa turned her head at last. "Mr. Avelar, one more disruption and you will be hooded for the remainder of the segment."

"Then call me by the right name while you do it."

His lip was split. Blood had dried dark in the scar through his eyebrow and fresh blood lay brighter over it, a bad copy over old record. One sleeve had ridden up where he had fought the floor ring, exposing purple restraint marks around the wrist bone. Marisol saw Teresa register all of that only as future wording. Subject became verbally escalatory. Subject resisted correction. Subject required additional stabilization.

Teresa looked at Marisol instead. "You will submit your incident statement before end of shift."

Marisol wiped Celia's son's tears off her collar with the back of her wrist and left the smear there. "You already have one."

"I have the start of one."

"You have the whole thing."

Teresa's expression did not change, but she took half a step nearer, lowering her voice so the car would have to strain to hear. "Do not confuse proximity to suffering with innocence in it."

That landed because it was true enough to bruise. Marisol had proxied suffering for twenty years. She had cleaned it, coded it, vented it, bleached it, signed for it, made it move. She might have said something useful back if Gabriel had not spoken first.

"She knows," he said.

Silence opened a small hard space around the words.

Paloma was taping Celia's wrist to a molded splint now, her movements quick and brutal because gentleness would have taken longer and hurt more. The old man with the broken glasses was breathing through his mouth and holding the blanket to his lip. Naila had one hand over the gauze at her brow and the other flattened over the wrapper knots in her lap, beaten bloody and still unwilling to lose count.

Gabriel shifted against the restraint ring and looked directly at Marisol.

"You keep thinking time is mercy," he said.

His voice was not raised. That made everyone hear it.

"It isn't. Not if they still own the door."

Marisol opened her mouth, closed it, and opened it again.

"I was trying to keep them out until Caldera."

"For who."

"For witnesses. For cameras. For a chance that this would not stay in their file language."

Gabriel nodded once, blood on his teeth. "And in the meantime?"

He tipped his chin toward Celia, toward Naila, toward the old man with the blanket to his mouth, making her look where she was already looking.

"In the meantime they lined up outside, got their shields set, chose where to hit, and called it preservation. You bought them organization. You bought them a better report. If they still own the room, the delay is part of the beating."

No one in the car moved. Even Teresa did not interrupt, maybe because to stop him would make the line audible again, maybe because some part of her believed in letting people hear a truth right before she filed it under the wrong heading.

Marisol felt the words where she usually felt bad air pressure in a clogged pipe, the moment before backflow. Minutes were not neutral; they belonged to whoever held the locks, and she had let work teach her otherwise because otherwise was survivable.

"I kept the children off the floor," she said, and hated how small it sounded as soon as it existed.

Gabriel did not let her keep even that without measure. "Yes," he said. "You did. Good. They got to watch from higher up."

The boy on her hip gave a broken hiccup in his sleep and tightened his fingers in the fabric at her shoulder. Celia turned her face toward them, not fully conscious, following the sound of her son's breathing. Naila opened one eye and watched Marisol over the blood-caked edge of the gauze, not cruelly, which was worse.

Paloma rose with a grunt and stripped off one glove. "Celia needs the trauma room for films and pain control. Naila needs that eyebrow closed before it cakes dirt in. The old man needs his mouth checked for fragments. If you people are going to resume your holy scan, do it around me or do it in hell."

That, at least, stopped Teresa for another beat.

Outside the car, somewhere farther up the corridor, another announcement started for another compartment. It carried the same polished courtesy, the same two languages, the same request for orderly compliance. The sound came thin through the steel and made Marisol's skin crawl worse than shouting.

She looked down at the silver wrapper cord in Naila's lap, the little knots mashed but still countable. She looked at Celia's cherry comb lying under the bench where it had been kicked during the charge. She looked at the scanner on the seat, cleaned and ready to continue, one corner spider-cracked from the fall but still functional. That was the corridor's genius: it could leave damage everywhere and still preserve function.

Marisol knew how to scrub blood out of the tread plate before it darkened. She knew how much water to use if she wanted the pink to vanish without spreading. She knew which code on the incident form would bury a broken wrist under transport friction and which phrase would make a split brow sound like an unruly lunge into fixed infrastructure. She knew the smell of hot iron after a baton strike because the berth rails held it for minutes. She knew too much about how harm stayed operational.

In that moment the knowledge sat in her like contamination.

Teresa took back her tablet from the custody officer. "Medical transfer for three," she said. "Then we finish the reconciliation."

The pronoun landed wrong. Teresa was folding blood into process with that we.
