Marisol bent and picked up Celia's red comb from under the bench. Two teeth were broken off it. She set it on the berth beside the sleeping boy so his mother would see it when the pain medication hit and she came back through the fog looking for proof of what was still hers.

Then she stood in the ruined aisle with the blood, the mop water, the wrapper foil, and the scanner chirping ready, and felt the old reflex reach for the caddy, the rag, the nearest lie that would make the damage look procedural. She let it pass. She left the pink water in the bucket, the cracked scanner on the seat, and Celia's broken comb in plain view beside the sleeping boy. When the scanner chirped again, Teresa had to step around the blood to reach it. Blood didn't stop the line.


# Chapter 9

Every few minutes the public-address voice repeated the hold in clipped bilingual politeness, as if weather were a customer-service inconvenience instead of a wall. Somewhere south of Caldera, lightning had hit hard enough to send the dispatch board red in three blocks at once. Under the yard floods the train sat with its white paint slicked darker at the seams, rust bleeding through wherever the rain found an edge. Sometime past midnight the storm had done what Marisol had not been able to do cleanly: it had stopped the line without asking anybody's permission.

Marisol crossed the service lane with her shoulders up against the rain. Her uniform shirt stuck between her shoulder blades. The chemical burn scar inside her right wrist had gone tender again where dirty water kept needling through the cuff. She had left the pink water in the bucket and the cracked scanner on the seat, and still the line had kept moving until lightning made refusal everybody's problem. She could still feel the refusal car in her hands even though she had washed twice: the rubber drag of restraint straps, the shock of a young woman's wrist bent the wrong way, the hot slick of somebody else's blood drying in the whorls around her nails before the sink could take it. Paloma was still in the trauma room with the injured, silver braid shoved under a paper cap, right hand trembling itself empty each time she reached for tape. Teresa had gone to whatever glass office she used when she needed a storm to look temporary on paper.

The cafeteria stayed open during major holds because men who were awake and angry broke less equipment with starch in them. Caldera's night room had tired fluorescents, a steam table skinned over with grease, and windows that looked straight onto the maintenance sheds. Rain struck the glass hard enough to flatten the yard into streaks. Somebody had left the salt shakers upside down in a bowl to keep them from clumping. The fryer smell had worked itself into the walls years ago and won.

Ivo was at the far table by the vending machine glow. He had changed out of his soaked outer jacket but not his work pants. Grease still sat black in the moons under his nails no matter how many times he scrubbed. He had one boot unlaced halfway, the way he did when his arches were barking. A little paper field card lay beside his bowl, protected under an upside-down coffee saucer. Marisol recognized his handwriting before she sat down: blocky mechanic capitals, exact even when the paper was damp.

He looked up once. His face did not move much, but his eyes went over her left sleeve, her cheek, the place where a curl had come loose and dried stiff with somebody else's blood.

"You look worse than the storm," he said.

"You should've seen the other room."

"I heard it from under Car Four. Sounded like somebody dropped a crate of wrenches into a church."

She sat. The chair legs grated. Up close the paper card under the saucer read `swifts, low over shed 3, before first thunder`. Under that he had added `beaks open, eating the hatch`.

He saw her reading and slid the saucer farther onto it, not embarrassed exactly, just not interested in a joke at its expense. "My girl likes proof. Says if I don't write it down, I just made up another bird."

"Sensibly raised."

"I keep trying to ruin her."

There was a second bowl on the table, steam gone thin but still rising. It held beef stew from the steam line, potatoes collapsing at the corners, and a bay leaf glued to the surface by orange grease. Two heel ends of bread sweated on a plate between them. He nudged the bowl toward her with the back of his fingers.

"Eat before you start lying," he said.

She had not known she was hungry until the smell hit her. It was not the stew itself that caught her, but the black pepper and cheap tomato and overcooked onion, the smell of a kitchen that had kept going because stopping would have made more work. She tore bread, dipped it, put it in her mouth too fast, burned the roof, and kept chewing.

Ivo leaned back and watched the rain skitter down the window. "How bad?"

The question covered more territory than the two words could carry. Marisol let the stew settle on her tongue before she answered.

"One broken wrist that I saw. Maybe a cracked orbital on the tall kid from upper bunks. Naila took a boot and a baton before they dragged the aisle clear. Gabriel kept talking when he should've shut up for once. That probably saved three people and cost him something I couldn't count from where I was standing."

Ivo dragged the bay leaf out of his own bowl and set it on the table like a dead insect. "Security call it an event?"

"A noncompliance event."

"Sure." He rubbed a thumb along the rim of his spoon. "And if an axle shears, that's a wheel disagreement."

She almost smiled. It hurt more than she expected, not because her face was cut, though one spot on her jaw had begun to stiffen, but because the motion felt out of season. The part of the day that admitted humor should have been over before the first scream. Still, the line between his brows eased when he saw the smile happen, and that small easing traveled through her like contraband.

They ate for a minute with no obligation to fill the sound. Rain hammered the metal overhang outside the loading doors. A fork dropped somewhere near the sinks and nobody bothered to pick it up right away. The hold announcement came again, softer here, words flattened by distance and the industrial fan over the dish station.

Ivo said, "Storm pushed everything low before dark. Swifts, grackles, one lost heron over the commuter platform. My girl would've laughed herself sick. She thinks herons always look like old men in bad coats."

"She's right."

"I told her the same." He tore bread with both hands and then, as if he had not meant to start talking about home and could not quite stop now that he had, added, "She keeps feathers in old cough-syrup bottles on the windowsill. Labels still on them. Blue cap for black birds, white cap for grey. Her mother hates it because the place looks like a witch got an office job."

The picture came to Marisol complete enough to ache: bottles rinsed but never fully clear, down caught on the glass, a child sorting shades of ordinary loss into categories she trusted. She kept her eyes on the stew.

"How old now?" she asked.

"Twelve last month." He took a breath through his nose. "Wanted a cake shaped like a marsh hawk. Nora bought one from the grocery with a printout on top because nobody in this county knows what a marsh hawk is anymore."

"Did your daughter care?"

"She cared that the frosting was green enough to stain her teeth." His mouth twitched. "So no. Good priorities."

Outside, thunder rolled across the yard slowly enough to hear the separate hits on sheet metal and glass. Marisol dipped bread again and watched the grease ring widen in her bowl. Gabriel had been twelve the year he started correcting her paperwork pronunciation under his breath, not because he was rude but because he could not stand a word leaning crooked if he knew the proper weight of it. She thought of him in the refusal car, ribs bruised already from intake, mouth split again, still using the part of himself that did not know how to bow.

Ivo said, "Paloma said one of the girls bit clean through her lip."

Marisol nodded.

"And Teresa?"

"Still walking around with a dry blouse somehow." She swallowed coffee-cold stew and wiped the corner of her mouth with the heel of her hand. "She'll call this weather mitigation and successful restoration by breakfast if the rails drain fast enough."

"That's optimistic."

"It's RailPassage. Optimism is cheaper than repair."

He laughed once, short and ugly. Then he leaned forward, elbows on the table. "You get hit?"

"Not in a way she'd count."

"I'm not Teresa."

She looked up. His burn scar, a pale knot high on the neck where his collar never quite covered it, shone damp from the walk over. She remembered kissing that scar once in the old dorm stairwell because he had flinched when her hand found it and she had wanted to turn the flinch into something else. The memory came with the taste of cigarette ash and rust from the railing. She put her spoon down.

"One elbow," she said. "A restraint door kicked back when they overrode it."

"Show me."

"Eat your dinner."

"Sol."

She rolled her sleeve enough to show the swelling. Skin already darkening, a clean-edged bruise where metal had found the exact same place twice. Ivo did not touch it right away. His hand hovered, rough and broad and careful in a way she had not trusted from many men. When he did put two fingers against the skin below the bruise, he used almost no pressure, only enough to feel heat.

"You'll lose some range tomorrow," he said.

"I know what a hinge does when you hit it."

"I wasn't explaining hinges. I was telling you to keep the arm moving before it turns stupid."

She let the sleeve fall. The place his fingers had been felt cooler than the rest of her. He looked back to his bowl first, giving her the decent thing of a way out, and that decency was one of the reasons she had let him into her bed years ago and one of the reasons she had stopped. A man who knew how to be careful could still be dangerous when life got hungry.

He said, "You haven't gone home in three nights."

"Two."

"You counted fast."

"Because you're wrong."

"Fine. Two. Long enough."

Marisol rubbed her thumb along the spoon handle until the cheap metal bent a little. "I was there this morning. Fifteen minutes. Shower. Changed socks. Looked at the sink."

"The one with your mother's glass."

She glanced up again, surprised by the exactness of it, though she should not have been. Ivo had stood in that bathroom half-naked once, cursing at a faucet handle that came off in his hand, while her mother yelled from the bedroom that she had known from the start he was not a plumber no matter how his shoulders advertised. That had been a good year for nothing except appetite.

"Still there," Marisol said.

"Dentures?"

"In the blue tumbler. Cleaner tablets turned to chalk at the bottom. Tube of adhesive gone hard." She stared past him at the rain-black window. "I keep thinking I'll clear the room on my next full day. Then there isn't a full day. Then there is one and I spend it asleep with my shoes on."

Ivo wiped stew from the table with a thumb and then cleaned the thumb on his napkin. "How long?"

"Since January."

He let that sit.

"You can say that's filthy," she said.

"No."

"It's filthy."

"It's crowded," he said. "That's different."

The line landed harder than sympathy would have. The room belonged to the blunt economy of labor and debt and grief sharing one narrow apartment and taking turns with the sink. Her mother reduced to bite marks in acrylic. Gabriel carried south in a train Marisol helped keep odor-controlled. A housing advance still coming out of her pay two hundred at a time because there had been one emergency, then another, then a body that needed medication more than principle needed purity.

She said, "Sometimes I think the room's waiting for me to admit she's dead in a way the paperwork can use."

Ivo worried the damp napkin into a tight cord between his fingers before he answered. "That's your mother talking. Not the room."

The thunder came again, closer now. The vending machine lights flickered and came back. In the brief drop of power his face flattened into younger angles in her memory, the years before his neck scar and her knee and Gabriel's silence between them. She could still remember the feel of his mechanic's shirt half-unbuttoned under her palms, the trapped heat of him after a double shift, the way he used to take off his boots at her door and line them straight because her mother could forgive a lover faster than she could forgive mud. The wanting that moved through her now was not clean and not romantic. It was worse than that and smaller. It was the body's old habit of reaching toward the place it had once rested without lying to itself about the rest.

He broke the moment by standing up for coffee.

"You want more?" he asked.

"No."

"Liar."

"Then bring the bad kind."

He came back with two thick mugs. The coffee had been on the burner too long and tasted faintly scorched, but it was hot and there was sugar. He dropped three packets beside her bowl. One stuck to the damp table. On the back of his hand, near the thumb joint, a fresh slice had been sealed with clear glue and shop tape.

"What happened there?" she asked.

"Fan shroud didn't like my opinion."

"You should let Paloma look at it."

"I let Paloma look at it. She called me a dumb bastard and put the tape on crooked so I'd remember."

Marisol finally smiled without pain. "That sounds like her."

"She also said if this summer gets any hotter she'll start charging by the square inch of skin stupidity."

The smile went again. In summer heat, time was measured not by calendar pages but by how long the company could keep extracting labor from bodies before the bodies answered back. Ivo drank and watched her over the rim.

"I was at the pension office this afternoon," he said.

The change in subject was too deliberate to be casual. Marisol set her mug down carefully.

"Why?"

"Because payroll shaved six overtime hours off last week's sheet and I wanted to know if they were shaving them off the vesting count too."

"Are they?"

He laughed without humor. "Depends which snake you ask." He pulled a folded paper from his pocket, softened with sweat and rain and being handled too much. He did not open it. He held it between two fingers like something that smelled bad. "One woman said no because hours are hours. Man behind the glass said heat-season supplemental time only counts if the route closes in good standing. Which means if Teresa decides the yard lost discipline, all those extra nights turn into charity."

Marisol felt the bottom of the mug warm against her palm. "How far are you from vesting?"

"Nine weeks if the sheets stop getting cute. Maybe ten if they keep editing my life in quarter-hour blocks."

"That's all."

"That's all," he said. "Twenty-two years, and nine weeks is the part they can still use to make me beg."

The storm hit the window in a fresh hard wave. Beyond the glass the sheds disappeared entirely for a moment. Marisol pressed the folded paper flat under her thumb until the damp fibers started to buckle.

"And the court?" she asked.

He had not said he wanted to talk about that. She knew from the slight set of his jaw. But he had brought the paper. He had started down the line himself.

"Six thousand eight hundred," he said. "County review at summer's end. I get it down enough, maybe the judge stays bored and leaves me alone. I don't, they start talking like they invented fatherhood and I'm renting it from them."

"Ivo."

"Don't give me the voice. I know the number." He looked down into his coffee. "I know it every time I pick up overtime my back doesn't want. I know it when Nora texts a photo instead of an argument because she's too tired for the argument. I know it when my girl asks if I'm coming Saturday and I say if the line clears, if dispatch doesn't change, if Teresa doesn't get religion about punctuality."

He rubbed his face hard enough to drag the skin. When his hand fell, he was smiling again, but it was the kind of smile he used on stripped bolts, all method and no hope.

"Anyway," he said. "That's the romance portion of the evening."

Marisol reached across before she had decided to and pinned the folded paper to the table with her palm, bypassing his hand entirely. The ink had bled at the corners. The page was all numbers, vesting language, and a box around projected benefit release that might as well have been a threat. She slid it back.

"It's not romance," she said. "It's extortion with stationery."

Ivo barked a laugh, real this time. "There she is."

The relief that went through her at making him laugh was so immediate it shamed her. That, too, was old. The feeling belonged to muscle memory rather than love: the urge to keep one person in a room from looking broken, if you could manage it.

He finished his coffee. "You going to tell me why you've been looking at the train like you want to take a crowbar to its teeth?"

She wiped her mouth and stood before she answered.

"Not in here," she said.

* * *

They went out through the side door by the dry-goods cage because the loading dock roof reached farther there. Rain still drove sideways hard enough to wet their shins. The service lane shone black. Beyond it the commuter platform lights burned in a row, halos swollen by water. Somewhere under the train a drain coughed and then found its pull again. The whole yard smelled of hot metal cooling too fast, wet creosote, fryer grease escaping the vent stack, and the deep latrine animal underneath everything the bleach never killed.

Ivo lit a cigarette with both hands around the flame. Then he held the lighter out to her. The shape of it, his knuckles cupped against the wind, brought back another stairwell, another night when he had shielded a match the same way before pushing her back against concrete and kissing her like there was still enough time in the world to waste some. The memory hit low in her body and annoyed her for being alive. She took the cigarette instead of the rest of it.

The first drag scraped her throat clean of cafeteria grease. Rain hammered the roof over them and overflowed the gutter in a steady rope six feet away. Water struck the pavement hard enough to bounce.

"All right," Ivo said. "Not in there. Here, then."

Marisol watched the far track fill and drain, fill and drain. "Gabriel was right."

"About what? He says a lot of things right at people."

"About delay." She kept her eyes on the gutter. "A private delay is still the line doing what it was built to do. We buy six hours, twelve, then security reorganizes and somebody weaker pays for the time. That's what today was. I kept thinking I was making room. All I did was make them hit harder when they came through."

Ivo said nothing. Under stress he usually talked through options until the real fear arrived, and then the silence showed up like a locked tool chest. She heard the silence now and kept going.

"I don't have a clerical path. I don't have Teresa. I don't have the old authority. What I've got is knowing what the company can swallow and what it can't."

"That sounds bad already."

"It already is."

He took the cigarette from her, smoked, passed it back. Rainwater ran off the roof behind him and darkened the shoulders of his shirt. In the yard a forklift beeped twice and then gave up, stranded by lightning protocol.

"Say the next part straight," he said.

Marisol pressed her thumb against the burn scar on her wrist until the sting sharpened her. "I need to know what failure makes the line belong to other people. Not just RailPassage. People outside the line. Inspectors. Civilians. Cameras if it comes to that. Something they can't reclassify into a memo and force us to mop behind."

Ivo drew on the cigarette until the tip flashed bright, then held the smoke without letting it out. He skipped surprise and went straight to calculation, like a man measuring load against steel he knew had already fatigued.

"You want a stoppage with witnesses," he said.

"Eventually."

"Eventually is a coward's adverb."

"Fine. Yes."

He laughed once under his breath, but there was no pleasure in it. "Jesus."

"Don't make me prettier than I am. I'm not asking for absolution. I'm asking what the line can't bury."

"And when it can't bury it?" he said. "What then? Teresa takes the hit? You think that's how this works?"

"No."

"Good." He flicked ash into the wet. "Because it isn't. First thing that happens is they come for payroll. Overtime freezes. Housing office gets a hard-on for immediate collection. Anybody on a visa tied to the route starts waking up to very educational email. Then they walk the yard looking for the easiest bodies to punish and call it accountability."

Each sentence hit with the practical rhythm she trusted in him and feared. He was not dressing the risk up. He was laying it on the dock between them piece by piece like damaged parts.

"Paloma loses more than a job," Marisol said.

"Paloma loses her papers before Teresa loses a night's sleep."

Marisol nodded.

"And me," he said. "Let's not get noble and skip me. I lose the vesting if they can make cause stick. Nine weeks short means zero company match and years tied up in review while the lawyers eat them. I lose the overtime that is cutting the court number down. Maybe I lose the truck if county gets impatient. Maybe I tell my girl Saturday again, not this one. Maybe next one. Maybe after the hearing. She stops believing weekends are real."

The cigarette had burned down to the filter. Marisol ground it under her boot. The wet concrete made a soft grinding sound like bone wrapped in cloth.

"I know," she said.

"No, you know facts." His voice did not rise. That made it worse. "You don't know the shape of it yet. There's a difference. Facts are numbers on paper. Shape is when your daughter starts packing her backpack before she asks if you're coming because she's learned how disappointment wastes less time that way."

She could not answer quickly because the image arrived whole: a twelve-year-old girl with cough-syrup bottles full of feathers on the sill, zipping a bag before hope embarrassed her. The storm pressed them closer under the roof. A warm gust threw mist across her face.

"My mother's dentures are still in the bathroom," Marisol said, not because it answered him, because it was the truest available thing. "I keep leaving them there because every full day gets chewed up by the next mess, and I tell myself that means grief is crowded, like you said, not filthy. The room doesn't care. It just keeps whatever I leave sitting."

Ivo waited.

"That's this line," she said. "We keep calling it crowded, backed up, weather, whatever. It still keeps what we leave in it."

He turned toward the train and scrubbed rain off his mouth with the heel of his hand. The cars sat under the floodlights patient as appliances. Somewhere beyond them a detainee shouted once, too far to make words, and the sound disappeared into rain.

"You think I don't know that?" he asked.

"I think you know it every day."

"Then what are we doing out here?"

Her knee had started to complain from the cold. She shifted her weight and felt the old stiffness click. "Because knowing it isn't the same as putting your hand on it."

That finally made him turn back. Water ran from his hairline down beside the neck scar. She had the stupid urge to wipe it away with her thumb. Instead she tucked both hands into her pockets.

"I can't ask you for this clean," she said. "There isn't a clean version. I needed to hear your numbers out loud. The pension. The arrears. I needed to hear what gets torn loose if I pull harder than I already have."

"And?"

Behind him, up the rise past the service lane, the crew dorm windows shone in wet yellow rows. They were worker housing, one rectangular room after another bought with deductions, advances, overtime, and the promise that if people kept the line running the line would keep them fed. Marisol thought of Paloma sleeping in two-hour scraps so her contract would stay valid, of the men in the sanitation crew who mailed cash home before they bought themselves new socks, and of Ivo's daughter sorting feathers into medicine bottles while her father counted court debt against brake inspections.

"And I know it now," she said. "What it costs. Visas. Rent. Saturdays. Pension years."

His shoulders dropped a fraction. She read recognition in it, maybe, or plain exhaustion finally finding somewhere to stand. Relief would have been too generous a word.

"What exactly are you asking from me tonight?" he said.

The wind shifted. Rain sprayed across the dock, cold on her jaw. Marisol took a breath that smelled of creosote and clogged drains and fried oil and all the other things the company spent money trying to rename.

"A hearing," she said. "That's all tonight. I need the bigger map. The real one. Which failures stay ours. Which ones drag the world in before Teresa can scrub around them. I need you to hear the larger plan before I decide whether I can live with asking anybody to help me do it."

Ivo rubbed the back of his neck. For a second he looked older than forty-six, every shift and crawlspace and overtime summer sitting plain on him. Then the deadpan came back around the edges, not enough to hide him, just enough to let him keep standing.

"Jesus Christ, Sol."

"Yes."

"You really picked a romantic night."

"The coffee was bad enough to count as ambiance."

That got the smallest snort out of him. He rubbed at the lifting edge of the tape on his hand, sealed it back down with his thumb, and stood listening while the train hummed below and the dorm windows burned up the rise.

"Tomorrow after first maintenance whistle," he said. "Machine shop annex. Teresa won't come in there unless the place is actively on fire."

Marisol kept still.

"That isn't me saying I'm in," he added.

"I know."

"It's me saying I'll hear it. The whole ugly thing. You get one pass at telling it straight."

The words should have felt like relief. They did not. They landed with the weight of a deduction already taken. Across the yard the dorm windows burned on in the rain, company rooms paid one withheld check at a time, and Marisol understood with a new exactness that every hand she asked for would come to her already pledged to rent, medicine, visitation days, pension years, or some other ordinary piece of life RailPassage had priced to the cent.


# Chapter 10

Marisol stood in the trauma-room sluice with a deck brush in one hand and a quarter-close notice in the other while dirty water lapped at the drain lip. First maintenance whistle had come and gone while storm cleanup sprawled and Teresa staged her quarter-close theater, and she still had not made it to the machine-shop annex. The south half of the train smelled as if the marsh had crawled up under the floor to die; by noon even the storm water in the blackwater traps had gone warm. Teresa's neat signature sat above the words SUPPORTING LOG COMPRESSION - LOCAL RETENTION WINDOW CLOSING 18:00. Anything older than ten days would be normalized into summary tables for concession renewal review. Handwritten addenda would not be retained unless tied to active litigation. Supplemental sanitation variance notes were to be discarded after supervisory reconciliation.

Teresa had a way of making erasure look like housekeeping.

Paloma came through the inner curtain backward, one hand holding a tray of used hemostats, the other steadying the door with her hip. Her braid had come half loose, and the camphor on her clothes was losing its fight with disinfectant and stale blood. "If you're going to stare at that memo," she said, "do it while you scrub. I need this room clear in six minutes."

"Who's coming in?"

"No one new. Follow-up dressing change." Paloma set the tray down and flexed her right hand once, trying to shake the tremor out of it. "And before you ask, yes, it's her."

Marisol folded the notice into her back pocket. The paper kept its crease like thin tin. "You gave me six minutes. Don't waste two of them."

Naila sat on the exam cot with her shirt lifted to the bottom of her ribs while Paloma unwound yesterday's bandage. The bruising from the refusal crackdown had turned from black-violet to a yellowed green that reminded Marisol of oil on old dishwater. Naila's cropped hair had grown into a rough shadow around her head. She had twisted the silver lining from an oral-rehydration packet into a narrow cord and was knotting it around her left forefinger with the two fingers that still had nails. It wasn't nerves. She was counting, making something hold.

When she looked up, her face stayed still except for the eyes. "You brought paper."

Marisol took Gabriel's scrap notebook out of the inner pocket of her coveralls. It was not a notebook in any proper sense. It was wrapper paper flattened and layered, a ration label peeled clean and used twice, part of a laundry ticket, two bunk tags gone soft from sweat. Gabriel's handwriting crossed over other people's penciled marks. Some names had only initials, some had a bunk and a guessed country, some had nothing but a date and a corner notch.

Paloma peeled tape off Naila's side with slow fingers. "Talk while I work. If she starts bleeding again, both of you shut up."

Marisol spread the scraps on the instrument trolley. "These four. Here, here, here. They show up in Gabriel's list, then nothing. No transfer confirmation, no reassignment on the manifest. You told him some people disappeared before the port."

Naila leaned in. The room's fluorescent light made the sun marks across her cheeks look like bruises laid down years ago and kept. She touched one tag with the pad of her missing-nailed finger. "Not before the port. Before the country. Different thing."

"Then say it clean."

"They went out through clinic or wash." Naila tapped the tag again. "This woman was Celia. Bunk 6-F on the intake bus, then 3-C on the train. She kept the salt packets from the rehydration mix and ate them dry because the sweet drink made her vomit. After the latrine backed up into the aisle, they took her with the ones who had rash and fever."

Marisol heard herself slip into command voice. "Who took her, what color band, which station."

"A custody woman with the throat tattoo. A medic with green tape on her shears." Naila spoke as if laying knots one after another, no hurry, no wasted lift. "White temporary band first, then blue over it after clinic. At Caldera, not on the public side. By the service fence near the linen yard."

Paloma kept her eyes on the bruising, but Marisol saw the pause in her hands. It lasted only half a second, but it was enough.

"You saw her there?" Marisol asked.

"I saw her two days later through the water-fill window." Naila nodded toward the tracks beyond the steel wall, as if the sight line were still open. "She wore a yellow municipal apron. Her wrist was wrapped so thick she used the other hand to shake sheets. She had tucked two salt packets into the seam at her chest. She hid them the same way."

Marisol wanted to say it could have been another woman, another yellow apron, another chest seam hiding contraband. The objection came all the way to her teeth and stopped. Naila did not look like a girl guessing. She looked like a worker whose whole life had depended on noticing which thing in the pile was still usable.

Paloma pressed fresh gauze over the worst of the bruising. "Breathe shallow."

Naila obeyed without complaint. "This one too," she said, pointing at a tag with only M. REYES and a bunk number. "He had one sandal split at the toe. He tied it closed with red bread wire. Coughed all night, then bled from the nose when they hit the brakes too hard outside Marsh Crescent. They called him nontransportable because he would not stop staining the blanket. After that I saw him on the drainage crew by the commuter tracks. It was the same sandal, the same red wire. He was shoveling mud that smelled worse than this room."

Marisol put two fingers on the trolley to steady it. The metal was tacky from the steam Paloma had been running through the wound-cleaning tray. "You told Gabriel all this?"

"Some." Naila rolled the silver cord tighter around her finger. "I didn't have enough words. We had wrappers. We had numbers. We had the way people stopped answering when the lights came on."

Paloma tied off the bandage and sat back. Her eyes looked older in the fluorescent wash than they did over cafeteria coffee. "I can give you three more minutes. After that I have to make it true that she needed the room."

Marisol lifted one of the bunk tags. The corner had been clipped square. She had assumed damage, or boredom, or some detainee habit of marking what belonged to them with the only tool they had. Now the square corner felt intentional in her fingers. "What does this cut mean?"

Naila looked at it once. "Clinic hold."

"You know that how?"

"Because they changed the tags after rescan. Whole corners for families, triangle for transfer group, square for clinic. We learned fast." She glanced toward Paloma, then back to Marisol. "You don't survive rooms like this by waiting for people in uniform to explain the symbols."

Paloma gave a dry short laugh with no humor in it. "No, you don't."

Marisol gathered the scraps into order again: Celia, Reyes, two others with clipped corners and blank transfer fields. The paper smelled of hand oil, old sugar, and the mildew that lived in the train's seams no matter how often she threw caustic at it.

"Why municipal?" she asked. "If they defer transfer for medical, they should go to hospital intake or court hold."

Naila's expression barely changed. That was what made the answer land. "Hospitals let people go. Work yards don't."

The words sat in the room like something dragged in on a boot.

Paloma stripped her gloves off and dropped them in the red bin. "Your six minutes are dead. Take the scraps and get out before someone decides to audit my tenderness."

Marisol tucked the wrapper papers away. "I need Gabriel."

"You always need Gabriel." Paloma reached for the used instruments. "What you need right now is a reason to be at his car."

"Sanitation pull."

"Then pull something." Paloma bent to open the pathology cupboard and, without turning around, said, "The square-corner tags started showing up after Teresa's audit week. I noticed because they made my counts wrong."

Marisol looked at her.

Paloma kept arranging sealed sample jars inside the cooler, her silvering braid slipping against the nape of her neck. "I said I noticed. I didn't say I understood." She shut the cupboard with her hip. "Go."

The higher-security car had been resealed after the storm with fresh strips of tamper tape over every service hatch. Marisol broke the tape on the meal-waste port because sanitation outranked optics when the bins were full enough, and because nobody argued with a smell that had already started to turn. Inside, the air came out hot and wet, thick with old sweat, bleach from the rushed respray, and the sweet sick rot of fruit cups opened too early and eaten too slowly.

Gabriel was three bunks in, one wrist cuffed to the side rail. The bruise along his upper arm had gone yellow at the edges. His eyebrow scar cut through the damp dark of his face like a mark made years before this and kept. He did not stand when he saw her. He smiled once, without warmth.

"Supervisor Anaya," he said. "You look radiant."

"You look like hell."

"That too." His eyes dropped to the port in her hand. "You didn't come for the bins."

Marisol kept her voice low. The custody guard at the far end of the car was flirting with the water-service contractor through the outer door. For the moment, their laughter covered her. "Square notch on the tags. Naila says clinic hold."

Gabriel's expression changed before he answered. It wasn't surprise. It was recognition, and then anger that she had taken this long. "You finally asked the right question."

"I asked what I could prove."

"No. You asked what fit in the box they gave you." He shifted closer, chain dragging against the rail. "Clinic hold, med deferral, civic recovery, municipal stabilization. They keep changing the label because the label isn't the point."

Marisol slid the clipped tag and one page of the scrap notebook through the port. Gabriel glanced once toward the guard, then laid the paper flat over his knee.

"Paper House heard the rumor on three corridors," he said. His voice had none of the performative heat he used when he wanted to hurt her. That made it worse. "People who got too sick, too injured, too contaminated for clean transfer stopped moving south but didn't come home either. They turned up cleaning flood drains, sorting city linens, hauling shelter cots, always under some local contract with a different badge and the same case number buried under it."

"Rumor is not proof."

"No shit."

He tapped the clipped corner. "This isn't a detainee mark. Intake staff use it when they don't want the change obvious from a distance. You can see it in the track labels too. Listen to the recorder. Track seventeen, the woman from North Basin? She says they moved her after a restraint sore on her ankle got infected. She thought deferment meant a ward bed. What she got was a city laundry with a locked dorm and bleach burns up both arms because they had her stripping contaminated sheets bare-handed."

Marisol had heard the recording in the dark at Marsh Crescent, but she had been listening then for the wound in the voice, for the fact of a person naming what the train had done. She had not known what piece of machinery hid inside the nouns.

"Track twenty-two," Gabriel went on, already there, already sorting sound into evidence. "Man from the estuary camps. Broken finger, fever, transport postponed. Four days later he was patching storm screens for Caldera municipal works. He laughed when he said it because he thought he had cheated the ship. Then he said the guards on the work van still carried rail restraints."

Marisol looked past him at the car's white paint failing along the window seam. Rust had come through again, thin as dried blood under skin. "Why didn't you say it plain?"

He gave her a look that would have gutted a weaker person. "I did. You wanted a family exception, not a business model."

The laughter at the outer door stopped. The contractor wheeled a water cart away. Marisol crouched to yank a waste bin free so if anyone looked in she would be doing the thing she was supposed to be doing.

"They purge local supports at eighteen hundred," she said. "Quarter-close compression. Teresa signed it herself."

Gabriel's silence lasted long enough to become its own shape in the air between them. Then he said, "Of course she did. The summaries don't bleed. The raw logs do."

"If this is real, I need event codes, not rumor."

"Then stop thinking like compliance." His hand came through the slot just far enough for her to see the ink still ground into the sides of his fingers from some pre-arrest life that had not finished leaving him. He pointed to the page she had given him. "The names matter, but the categories matter more right now. Match clinic holds to sanitation incidents. Not just beatings. Look at vomit events, lice wash, blood on restraint foam. Anybody who makes the car look unfit to show. Dirt is what moves them sideways."

The phrase hit her in the place where professional knowledge lived: not just beatings, but vomit events and blood on restraint foam, all the things she had spent twenty years learning to clear fast, clear quiet, clear before a camera or inspector or civilian nose could catch them. The train had not merely reacted to those thresholds. It had learned to earn from them.

She stood with the sloshing bin in both hands. The lid rattled from the train's low floor vibration. "If I find it?"

Gabriel leaned back from the hatch. His voice went flat and almost gentle, which was the register he used when he was naming the worst thing in the room and refusing to blink. "Then you get it out of company hands before they turn it into a better spreadsheet."

* * *

The clinic log cage sat behind the oxygen closet and smelled of paper mold, iodine, and the mouse poison somebody had tucked under the lowest shelf in the dumb hope of discipline. Marisol had supervised the cleaning of that room for years before she had ever been allowed to touch the folders inside it. She knew which floor tile rocked, which lock stuck in humid weather, which shelf bowed under the older binders. Losing passcard reach had not scrubbed that map out of her.

Paloma opened the cage with her own credential and left the door on the latch. "You have eleven minutes before the courier starts screaming about signatures," she said. "If you crumple anything, I'll hit you with a tendon hammer."

Marisol already had gloves on. "I'm not crumpling."

"Everybody crumples when they're afraid."

Paloma set a stack of blank prescription pads on the desk and a clinic pencil sharpened down to the metal ferrule. The tremor had come back into her right hand now that she had stopped moving fast enough to hide it. She pressed the heel of that hand against the desk until it quieted. "You can't take originals. If anything goes missing, Teresa will have the whole wing strip-searched on principle."

"I know."

"Do you." Paloma looked at her at last. "Marisol, I'm already hanging by thread and paperwork. So if what you find in there is enough to make you stupid, decide not to be."

Then she was gone, curtains whispering shut behind her.

Marisol pulled the first storm-week binder and laid it flat. Trauma-room entries ran in two columns: intake case number, presenting condition; disposition, signatures, cross-reference. Her own whole career was buried under forms like this, under the faith that if the language stayed exact enough the damage might count as managed instead of chosen.

Case 5128-C: scalp laceration, restraint impact, cleaned and closed.
Case 4410-A: diarrheal contamination, dehydration, observed.
Case 4471-R: rib bruising, intake restraint, no transfer delay.

Gabriel's number caught in her throat before the rest of the line did. The chart listed no transfer delay, as if his bruised ribs were a minor intake inconvenience and not her son cuffed three bunks into a sealed car. She forced herself past his entry and kept hunting the clipped-corner cases Naila had named.

Celia was not listed as Celia. She appeared as 3182-M, dermatologic spread with GI contamination, offloaded under MED DEFERRAL - TEMP CIV STAB, seventy-two-hour review pending, cross-referenced to Sanitation Exposure Event MW-CAL-77, with a destination field that read municipal receiving clinic. The phrase sat there with the clean dead confidence of a lie that had passed through legal twice.

Marisol copied the case number onto the prescription pad, then the incident code. Beneath it she wrote the bunk Naila had given her, 3-C, and the note about the salt packets because if this ever had to become more than paper she wanted the person back inside the code.

She pulled the next binder. Reyes was 4417-L, persistent epistaxis post restraint, linen contamination, MED DEFERRAL - LABOR SAFE PENDING RESPIRATORY CLEARANCE, cross-referenced to MW-MAR-12, destination municipal recovery berth.

Marisol stopped writing. The phrase LABOR SAFE was not medical language. It was warehouse language. Her chemical-burn scar dragged against the page as she steadied her hand. It was how management talked about a pallet with one broken corner that could still carry load if you did not stack it too high.

She copied the line anyway.

The sanitation office kept its own record books in the next room over because blackwater incidents, foam replacements, and contamination holds all touched dispatch-safe status. Those were her books, or had been, before Teresa decided she could no longer be trusted with the full anatomy of the route. Marisol crossed the hall with the prescription pad tucked inside her sleeve.

The office was empty except for the fan in the corner pushing hot air from one side of the room to the other. Someone had left a cup of instant noodles half eaten on the desk, the surface filmed pale with congealed broth. The smell of ammonia from the floor drums cut through it.

Her old key still worked on the lower file cabinet. Teresa had been thorough about electronic permissions and sloppy about metal.

Inside were the NCR log books in triplicate stacks: white copy to compliance, yellow to route office, pink retained until quarter close. Marisol pulled the week of the storm hold and flattened the pages one by one, fingers moving with the memory of shifts when she could do this half asleep. MW-CAL-77 recorded a Car 3 latrine reflux into the central aisle, contaminated linen removal, and four passengers designated nontransportable following exposure review. MW-MAR-12 logged a restraint-soil incident and nosebleed contamination, bedding discard, and one adult removed for municipal respiratory clearance.

The words on the sanitation side were worse, in their way, than the trauma-room lies because they were plainer: reflux, bedding discard, nontransportable. They named the body only when the body had become a problem for surfaces.

Marisol wrote the matching codes down, then opened the top drawer of the desk where route-cost folders sat waiting for signoff. Those should have held contractor receipts for bleach drums, pump seals, biofoam replacements. They did hold those. They also held a thinner folder she had never opened because it had always been Teresa's hand initialing the cover: MUNICIPAL OFFSETS / CONTAMINATION RESPONSE.

She knew before she touched it that this was where the rot would show its bones.

The first invoice came from Caldera Civic Linen Recovery, incident reference MW-CAL-77: four adult temporary placements, two days laundering contaminated public-stock bedding, custodial supervision surcharge payable to RailPassage Logistics, net municipal reimbursement $672.

The second came from Caldera Drainage Authority, incident reference MW-MAR-12: one adult temporary placement, debris-screen clearing and pump-house wipe-down for forty-eight hours, medical observation externalized, net payable $186 plus transport hold credit.

There were more behind them. Enough more that her stomach gave a small practical warning the way it did before she climbed bad stairs. Marsh Crescent heat-fever cases turned into shelter laundry support. A family-unit scabies hold became municipal mattress reclamation. Pregnant detainees coded unfit for immediate ship transfer were routed into climate-center kitchen prep under custody waiver until the next review window.

Marisol sat down hard in her old chair. Its vinyl had split years ago; the foam underneath scratched the back of her thighs through the coveralls. A mouthful of blood on a blanket, vomit in summer heat, lice eggs in the seam of a family bunk, a wrist restraint rubbed raw to wet meat because nobody loosened it fast enough: she had spent two decades scrubbing those thresholds into variance language. Teresa's folder showed what happened next. Once a passenger made the car look unfit to show, RailPassage sold the delay and billed the city for the body.

"Fuck."

The word came out soft and total.

Over the speaker in the corridor, Teresa's voice clicked on in both languages, clipped and polished even through cheap metal. All supplemental route logs were to be stacked for courier pickup by seventeen-thirty. No local copies were to remain outside approved archival channels. Staff were reminded that quarter-close review was an audit-sensitive period and unauthorized reproduction of chain-of-custody documents would be treated as evidence tampering.

Marisol stood before the announcement finished in English. Outrage could wait. Paper could not.

The NCR books solved part of the problem. She tore the pink retained copies for MW-CAL-77 and MW-MAR-12 from the binding with a practiced slow pull that left the stubs looking old rather than freshly robbed. From the trauma-room files she could not tear anything without exposing the theft. She went back to the clinic cage, found the copier no one used because its toner left soot on fingers, and laid the relevant pages one at a time on the glass.

The machine coughed like a smoker and spat out grey crooked ghosts, but the codes were legible: 3182-M, 4417-L, MED DEFERRAL, LABOR SAFE, MUNICIPAL RECOVERY BERTH.

It still wasn't enough. She copied two more, then three more, enough to show pattern rather than anomaly: a scabies wash detail, a heat-collapse kitchen assignment, and one case that made her stop again because the patient age line read sixteen and the invoice buried the fact under juvenile family support. She copied that one too and hated the paper in her hand for staying dry.

Paloma slipped back in with a box of suture packs and shut the cage door with her heel. "Courier is here," she said. "He smells like hair oil and grievance."

Marisol held the copies up.

Paloma's tired eyes took in the words and sharpened in a way Marisol had not seen since the worst nights in the trauma room, when the doors opened too fast and bodies came in stacked by urgency. "Oh," she said.

"It's worse than rumor."

"I can read." Paloma set the box down. Her right hand trembled once, then flattened against the metal shelf until it obeyed. "How many do you have?"

"Seven case pages, two sanitation copies, four invoices."

"That's not enough for court."

"It's enough for a leak."

Paloma looked toward the ceiling as if she could see Teresa's office through it. "If you send anything through a terminal, she'll know before the file finishes leaving the outbox."

"I know."

"If you take originals, she'll lock the whole wing down."

"I know that too."

Paloma picked up one of the photocopies and ran her thumb over the black streak the copier had left in the margin. "There's a parish archive by the commuter viaduct. Old baptisms, dockworker marriages, death books, all the paperwork people suddenly need once a treaty turns grandparents into border hardware. Gabriel talked about it once when he was twelve and bored in my waiting room. He said the church kept records better than the state because the church still believed paper had a soul."

A laugh rose in Marisol's throat and died there. "Paper House."

"Probably." Paloma handed the copy back. "If you go, go with paper. Don't use a drive or a photograph. Paper can rot, but it doesn't notify compliance on the way out."

Marisol gathered the pages into order and slid them between two ordinary sanitation variance sheets so anyone glancing down would see only pump failures and bleach inventory. "If they ask, you never saw this."

Paloma's mouth tightened. "If they ask, I'll tell them I was busy stopping a girl from coughing blood onto my floor. Which, for the record, is true often enough that nobody should doubt it."

She reached into the cabinet, pulled out an empty sleeve from a pack of adult incontinence briefs, and shoved the copied pages inside it. "Use this. Men in uniforms don't like digging through anything that suggests age or shit."

Marisol took it.

Paloma added, "When this comes back on us, and it will, don't insult me by apologizing like a priest. Just make sure it lands somewhere that can hurt them."

* * *

Santa Marta parish crouched under the commuter viaduct three blocks from Caldera Junction, its stone front permanently filmed with rail dust and candle soot. People came there for mass, for cheap soup on Tuesdays, and for records that had become more dangerous than relics. The side door to the archive opened off an alley that smelled of wet cardboard and frying oil from the stand across the street. Gabriel had written the directions on the back of a meal ticket in pencil so faint Marisol had nearly missed them: ask for the archive, not the office. Say Avelar if somebody else is listening.

She crossed from the employee side of the station with the packet inside the incontinence-brief sleeve, the sleeve inside her cleaning tote, the tote under a folded yellow caution vest. Every set of footsteps behind her sounded, for three strides, like security. Her left knee had stiffened in the evening damp and made the stairs a negotiation. Heat still rose off the pavement though the sky had gone the color of a bruise turned silver at the edges.

The archive room was cooler than the street by only the width of a lie. Two fans turned overhead, pushing paper smell, wax, and old mildew in patient circles. Metal shelves ran to the ceiling. Baptism ledgers sat beside freight-union death books, shipping parish registers, marriage packets, and acid-free boxes labeled in a hand more careful than any company clerk's. At the central table, a woman in a sleeveless black dress was dusting the edges of a ledger with a makeup brush. Her glasses were held together at one hinge with beige athletic tape. A battery radio on the windowsill muttered a baseball score to itself.

She kept dusting the ledger edge when Marisol came in. "Office is closed."

"I need the archive."

"That's where you're standing."

Marisol shut the door behind her. "I'm looking for an Avelar certificate."

The brush stopped moving. The woman raised her head then and looked Marisol over without hurry: coveralls, route badge, cleaning tote, the posture of someone who knew where the exits were. One of her hands wore a rubber finger cot on the thumb to keep oil off the pages. "For court," she said, "or for memory."

Marisol thought of Gabriel saying labels were never the point. "For survival."

The woman's mouth twitched once, not into a smile. She switched off the radio. "You shouldn't bring rail uniforms in here."

"I came from work."

"That's not a defense." She set the brush down and got up, moving with the careful economy of somebody who had spent years carrying brittle things. "Did Gabriel send you?"

Marisol nodded.

"He usually sends students and granddaughters." The woman came around the table. Up close she smelled faintly of clove cigarettes and book glue. "I'm Lidia."

Marisol kept her hands at her sides. "Marisol."

"Do you have originals?"

"No."

"Good. Originals get noticed fast." Lidia pointed to the table. "Put down whatever is making your shoulders do that."

Marisol set the tote down, pulled out the sleeve, and slid the papers free. The absurdity of the wrapper made Lidia glance at it once and then away with professional respect.

She spread the packet in silence: one photocopied case page, then another, then a pink sanitation copy, two invoices, one more photocopy, and the handwritten cross-reference sheet from Marisol's prescription pad.

Lidia's eyes sharpened on the codes almost immediately. "These are local."

"All from the same corridor."

"And partial."

"Because I had twenty minutes and a copier that wants to die."

Lidia picked up the page with LABOR SAFE on it and read to the bottom. Her taped glasses slid down her nose. She pushed them back with the finger-cot thumb. "This isn't enough for publication by itself."

"I know."

"It's enough for corroboration if Paper House has testimony."

"They do."

"Do they have dates?"

"Some of them do. They have names, bunks, voices." Marisol heard the strain enter her own voice and cut it off. "Listen to me clearly. They're purging local supports tonight. The summaries will stay, but the summaries hide the mechanism. I need this somewhere Teresa Ordonez can't recall with one call."

Lidia gathered the pages into three piles by document type. Her face had gone very still. "Sit."

"I don't have time to sit."

"Then stand still and listen." She laid one finger on the invoice. "Paper House doesn't move copied rail records blind. RailPassage would love nothing better than to seed the city desk with a clean fake and watch who comes running. Anonymous packets get quarantined until the source can be tied to a living mouth."

Marisol heard the fan above them chopping the warm air into dull slabs. "How long?"

"Morning if they're kind. Longer if they're scared." Lidia tapped the handwritten cross-reference sheet. "Anonymous codes and copier soot look scared."

"Morning's useless."

"I know." Lidia opened a drawer and took out a narrow cream form headed ARCHIVAL CUSTODY NOTE. The paper smelled faintly of incense and dust. "So you can keep your deniability, and this sleeps in the sacramental overflow until somebody verifies it, or you can give me your real name, your badge number, and a chain statement in your own hand. Not the medic who let you in. Not whoever forgot to search your tote. You. If the pouch gets opened, Teresa Ordonez won't have to guess whose throat to put her hand on."

The form looked smaller than the risk it held. Marisol had spent twenty years learning how to keep her name one line away from the filth that paid her. Put it here and the line back to her would be straight enough for any auditor with teeth.

"Leave Paloma out of it," she said.

Lidia's expression did not shift. "I asked for your hand, not hers."

Marisol took the clinic pencil from the table. The ferrule bit cold against her fingers. She wrote in block letters to keep the tremor out: MARISOL ANAYA. Route supervisor, Meridian West. Copied from trauma-room and sanitation holdings at Caldera Junction before quarter-close compression. Supporting codes cross-referenced from internal case and incident logs. Her passcard tapped once against her hip when she bent to copy the badge number from its back.

Lidia read the note once. "That will move it tonight."

"Not without cost."

Lidia carried the note to the old overhead scanner rigged beside the shelves. It was the size of a sewing machine and looked nearly as old. No rail-company terminal would have recognized it as worth monitoring. She snapped on the lamp, and the bulb threw a white square over the cradle. "Digital copy on isolated storage, paper duplicate in the sacramental overflow, courier packet on the nineteen-ten to the city node. Different routes, different hands. If one chain gets leaned on, the other two still move."

The phrasing was not churchy. It was archival, which Marisol trusted more.

"Will Paper House get it tonight?"

"If the local runs on time for once in its miserable life." Lidia fed the first invoice under the glass, squared it with two nicotine-yellow nails, and brought the platen down. "Don't ask for more than that."

The scanner clicked and whined. On the small attached screen the invoice bloomed up in hard black letters, uglier and more convincing than it had looked in Marisol's hand.

Net municipal reimbursement.
Labor safe pending respiratory clearance.
Custodial supervision surcharge payable to RailPassage Logistics.

Lidia scanned the rest with the concentration of a surgeon or a forger. When she reached the end of the packet, she laid Marisol's custody note on the cradle and sent that through too. Marisol watched her hands. That was how stress worked in her now. It wasn't pulse or dizziness. It was hands, the useful ones in the room: her own, cracked and bleach-rough; Lidia's, steady over the platen; Paloma's, roughened by tremor and still precise enough to close flesh; Naila's, knotting silver wrapper into memory because paper could be taken but counting had to live somewhere.

"Why a church archive?" Marisol said before she meant to speak.

Lidia kept feeding pages under the glass. "Because when governments started sorting people by grandparents and ports and dead fathers, everybody came running to churches for proof. Baptisms, marriages, burial books. Old dock unions did the same. Paper House figured out fast that the safest place to hide testimony was among all the other documents power pretends are sacred." Another click, another page swallowed into duplicate. "Also because no compliance officer under fifty can tell one ledger room from another. They think mildew means irrelevance."

That tracked so perfectly with Teresa's kind of intelligence that Marisol almost respected the elegance of it.

When the last page had gone through, Lidia printed two thin thermal receipts with checksum strings Marisol couldn't parse and tore them off. One she tucked into an envelope with the paper originals. The other she held out.

"What is that?"

"Proof I took custody, if I die inconveniently before the packet moves."

Marisol took it. The strip curled warm in her palm like something alive and temporary.

Lidia slid the scanned pages into one acid-free folder marked MUSIC MINISTRY DONATIONS and the originals into a second marked DAMAGED REGISTERS - HOLD. She clipped Marisol's custody note to the donation folder before pulling a canvas diocesan mail pouch from under the table and putting it inside with three other packets. "City node gets the pouch on the nineteen-ten local. Paper copy stays here tonight. If the train is watched, the archive still has a set. If the archive burns, the city has a set."

"And if both burn?"

Lidia finally looked straight at her. The tape on her glasses had started to peel with sweat. "Then somebody skipped recall and went straight to fire. At that point the city desk matters more than this room."

From outside came the rattle and brake-whine of an arriving commuter train. Lidia cinched the pouch and called through the open inner door, "Mateo."

A boy of maybe sixteen appeared from the back stacks carrying a crate of hymnals. He had a smear of blue paint on one wrist and the solemn put-upon face of someone who had expected a quiet evening with damaged paper and had instead been given live trouble. Lidia handed him the pouch.

"Platform three. Direct to the city desk. Not the station office. If anyone asks, parish records."

Mateo rolled his eyes with the reflex of long practice. "They always ask."

"Then answer better than they do."

He slung the pouch over his shoulder and went out at a trot, hymnals forgotten on the floor. The side door banged once behind him.

Marisol listened to his steps hit the alley, then the station stairs, then vanish into the larger iron sound of Caldera Junction swallowing and releasing bodies as if that were all a station ever did.

Lidia was already restacking the shelves, putting the room back into its ordinary disguise. "Don't leave on Mateo's heels," she said. "Station police notice when two frightened people use the same door and the same platform stairs. Count to sixty, then go with the next commuter spill."

Marisol stayed still for a second. In the station beyond the church wall, the nineteen-ten gave its departure chime, that clipped synthetic politeness the corridor used to make orders sound like service. She pictured the canvas pouch knocking against Mateo's leg as he boarded, the folder inside it rubbing against parish donation sheets and wedding banns and the paper proof of dead people once married under kinder bureaucracies. It wasn't enough for court, and it wasn't yet enough to tear the line open. It was enough to leave her empty-handed in the only way that helped.

When Marisol finally reached for her tote, it felt wrong, almost toy-light. She tucked the warm thermal receipt inside her bra, where the edge scratched at her damp skin with every breath. If Teresa stopped her now, she'd find bleach variances, a folded caution vest, and nothing she could claw back into company custody.

By the time Marisol stepped back into the alley, the nineteen-ten was already moving south to north, away from the concession office, carrying the pouch toward the city with the evening crowd and their groceries and their sweat and the ordinary loose change of after-work life. She waited until the last car cleared the viaduct, then turned back toward the yard. Quarter-close pickup would already be moving floor by floor. She needed to be in Teresa's line of sight again before absence became its own document. The white cars waited under their lights with rust coming through at the seams.


# Chapter 11

Quarter-close pickup put her back under Teresa's sightline, but it did not stop Marisol from working the problem. There were fewer things that could stop a Meridian West train than people said, and after watching one version of its dirt cross into other hands, she kept circling the others. She knew most of them by smell before she knew them by code. Hot brake lining gave off a scorched-penny stink when a shoe dragged too long. A blackwater tank near overflow sweetened the air in a way bleach never quite reached, toilet chemical floating on top of old shit and heat. Preservative from the trauma room lived at the back of the tongue like bad fruit and a coin held too long in the mouth. New hires thought a line could be stopped by a man collapsing in restraints, by screaming, by a child with a fever, by the sight of blood on the floor. Meridian West rolled through all of that. What stopped it, reliably, was liability that belonged to somebody else.

The clinic exam bay at Caldera Junction had been built to suggest care and had settled, over the years, into storage. A cracked blood-pressure cuff hung from a hook. The examination light had lost one of its handles and stayed aimed at the corner as if punishing the mop bucket. Paloma's pathology cooler sat on the tile between their feet, white plastic gone yellow at the edges, the latch wrapped in a strip of green dish towel because it chewed the palm when she carried it. Every time the compressor kicked, the lid rattled softly. Marisol heard the glass inside tap each other and thought of all the ways a body could be turned into paperwork.

She had spread three documents across the stainless prep table: the dispatch-safe checklist, a waste-management escalation sheet, and a restraint inventory form with the old company logo ghosting through the new one. On the back of an expired meal count she had written three headings in block capitals with a grease pencil.

CAN SPIN

CAN BUY TIME

CANNOT CONTAIN

Ivo leaned one hip against the sink and read over her shoulder without pretending he had not. Grease darkened the seams of his fingers no matter how hard he scrubbed, and he had one of those small hotel sewing kits open beside him because a shirt button had come loose at the collar. He had threaded the needle badly, using his big mechanic hands for a job that asked for patience, and every so often he squinted at it with the concentration he usually gave bearings. His burn scar rode pink above the collarbone when he bent his head.

Paloma sat on the rolling stool with both shoes planted wide to keep her balance while her right hand trembled itself out. She had taken the braid down after shift and was redoing it by touch, face shiny from the heat, camphor and basil following her each time she moved. A tea bag label stuck to the cuff of her scrub top. She had not noticed.

Marisol tapped the first heading. “Heat faint. Vomit. Bruised wrists. Smell. Dead camera if the outage stays in-house.”

“Most medical things,” Paloma said. “Most bodies, if we're being honest and ugly.”

Ivo threaded the needle at last, bit the end clean, and said, “Put civilian complaints on there too. Journalists gagging into linen napkins too, probably.”

“That part hasn't happened yet,” Paloma said dryly.

He glanced at her. “Feels inevitable.”

Marisol ignored that. She marked another line under CAN SPIN. “Sink backup in a sealed corridor. Missing soap stock. Anything ugly that stays ours.”

The fluorescent ballast buzzed overhead. Outside the clinic wall, a cart clanged over the expansion joint in the corridor and then rolled away. Marisol could hear the train itself through concrete and rail, the long sleeping weight of it on the shared yard track, vibration reaching up her shins by habit more than force. She had spent so many years reading danger through metal that stillness had its own noise.

She drew a line under CAN BUY TIME. “Blackwater high alarm.”

Ivo shook his head immediately. “Depends where.”

“It always depends where.”

“Then write it that way.” He nodded at the paper. “Blackwater high alarm in a yard buys you men with a pump truck and two lies. Blackwater high alarm out on the line buys you a dirty hold. Blackwater dumping where civilians can see it, that's different. That's city people, cameras, health codes, commuters stepping around shit in office shoes. That's not our paperwork anymore.”

Marisol changed the words. Her grease pencil made a gritty squeak across the form. BLACKWATER FAIL IN PUBLIC SPACE.

Paloma leaned forward, stool wheels ticking. “If it's only sewage, Teresa can call it maintenance and ask for patience. Ugly, but containable. If there's medical contamination in it, or anything that reads as medical contamination, then the jurisdiction changes. The instant it leaves our contained area and hits a civilian platform or a municipal drain, it stops being a concession problem and starts being a public health problem.”

“You sound like her when you say it that way,” Ivo said.

“I bill by the same vocabulary,” Paloma said. “That doesn't mean I love it.”

Marisol made another note. She could feel sweat collecting under the passcard lanyard at the back of her neck. Ever since Teresa had downgraded her access, the card sat against her chest like a joke: enough authority to sign off on dirty work, not enough to move cleanly through the cars she was supposed to supervise. The plastic edge had rubbed a raw line into the top button seam of her shirt. She kept touching it as if the card might change shape in her hand.

“Brake-test anomalies,” she said.

This time Ivo did not contradict her. He pushed off the sink, came to the table, and put the half-sewn shirt button down beside the dispatch sheet as if it were part of the meeting. Up close she could smell machine oil and the orange peel he kept in his pocket to fight the grease smell. His daughter had told him about that two years ago, laughing over a video call from somebody else's kitchen, and he had been doing it ever since. Small things took root in him harder than people expected.

“Not any brake anomaly,” he said. “If it's on private spur, company track, service siding, they rerun it. They blame heat, dirty sensor, warped shoe, whatever sounds technical enough to bore the listener. If it's on shared civilian track, or a commuter platform where dispatch hears the same pressure loss we do, then it gets a different number in the log. Once somebody outside RailPassage hears the bad cylinder breathing or sees the coupler strain not settle, they don't care what story Teresa prefers. Nobody wants a passenger train clipped by a deportation set because some bastard was saving schedule minutes.”

Marisol wrote SHARED TRACK BRAKE ANOMALY under CANNOT CONTAIN.

“How long does that hold?” she asked.

“If it's clean, if they're lucky, twenty minutes for inspection and rerun.” He scratched the side of his jaw with a thumbnail gone blunt from years of metal. “If it's not clean, if you stack it with something else, longer. A lot longer.”

“Stack it,” Paloma said. “That's the point. One failure is narrative. Two is exposure.”

Marisol looked at the second sheet, the restraint inventory form. Red-seal restraint key set, twelve primary cuffs, four juvenile flex sets, emergency bolt cutter logged under supervisor authority. She had signed these forms so many times her hand could have done it in sleep, but on the table they showed their other shape: the skeleton of a lock.

“A missing restraint inventory,” she said, “has to stop custody movement.”

“It should,” Paloma said.

“It doesn't,” Ivo said at the same time.

Marisol rubbed the grease pencil between finger and thumb until the paper wrapper softened. “Say it clean.”

He obeyed. “If they lose a cuff inside the car, they search, they yell, they beat hell out of the nearest person, and then somebody 'finds' it wedged under a bunk whether or not that's where it was. If a count fails on a sealed line and only company hands are there, they can manufacture a count. If a count fails on an open platform with outside police, rail staff, or cameras, then security gets stupid in a way that takes time. Time is yours if somebody else is watching.”

Paloma nodded. “And if the red-seal key log is wrong, then no one can say with confidence who opened what. Chain of custody starts bleeding out all over the floor.”

The word bleeding made Marisol think of the restraint foam after the refusal crackdown, the way blood had dried in the pores and stayed there through the first scrub, then the second. She had signed the replacement request herself. Everything on this line lived two lives: the first one in the body, the second one in the record pretending to explain it.

“Put it in the middle,” Ivo said, tapping the CAN BUY TIME column. “Missing inventory buys you time unless the room is public.”

Marisol moved it over. MISSING RESTRAINT COUNT, PUBLIC WITNESS = CANNOT CONTAIN. She underlined PUBLIC WITNESS hard enough to tear the damp paper.

No one spoke for a moment. The cooler compressor kicked on again. Somewhere out in the yard a synthetic announcement repeated a boarding restriction in two languages, clipped and polite, as if politeness were a form of force multiplier. Marisol looked at the list and saw not strategy yet but thresholds: the points where the company lost the right to keep its own mess indoors.

Paloma reached down and rested her steady hand on the cooler lid. “You're still avoiding the ugly one.”

Marisol knew which ugly one she meant. “Biohazard.”

“Yes.”

Ivo exhaled through his nose. “I hate that this sounds tidier than sewage.”

“It's not tidier,” Paloma said. “Sewage hits people as dirt. This hits them as fear.”

She stood and unlatched the cooler. Cold preservative breath rose up into the room. Marisol saw foam racks, sample tubes banded in red and yellow, a sealed bag of swabs, a small hard case with temperature tags stuck along the side. Paloma kept her medical things the way church women kept holiday ornaments: wrapped, labeled, exact. A dried basil leaf had been trapped by the gasket and gone dark there, flattened into the plastic.

“These are routine holds,” Paloma said. “Stool, sputum, blood, lesion scrapings when the rash is bad enough to satisfy protocol. Most of it's nothing dramatic. Half of medicine is waiting for a result that says the body is ordinary after all. But if a stool cup or sputum tube breaks where it shouldn't, the first thing people get is preservative in the back of the throat. Then the shit under it, or spit gone sweet from sitting warm in plastic. Boots track it. Gloves come out. The first guard through starts yelling exposure before he finishes cursing.”

“Supposed,” Ivo said.

She looked at him levelly. “Yes. Supposed. I'm not stupid about who employs me.”

“And the people in the car?” he asked.

Paloma kept her eyes on the cooler. “They get the worst of the first minute. Eyes watering. Dry heaves. Security dumping bleach too hard in the vestibule because panic always comes with a spray bottle. If they shut the vents to contain it in this heat, the old ones and the asthmatics fold first.”

Marisol heard her own voice go flatter. “Can you do that and still call it help?”

Paloma shut the lid halfway, not enough to latch. “No. I can call it smaller than letting the whole train slide south in clean paperwork. That's all. It is uglier than sewage because it uses sick bodies as leverage. If we do it, it has to be one threshold spill, tight and public. Not a whole cooler dumped like theater. I'm not poisoning a car to save it.”

Ivo rubbed his mouth. “If the old man in eleven-C chokes on bleach because we started a scare, that's on us too.”

“Yes,” Paloma said. “That's why it has to be small enough to force outside hands in and not wide enough to soak the bunks. One cracked container. One contaminated step. Enough to stop the train and make somebody with a badge and a payroll we don't answer to start writing.”

Marisol could hear the tremor back in Paloma's right hand now that it rested on the open lid. It made the sample tubes click softly against each other. It was not fear alone. It was overwork, heat, years. Paloma never mentioned the tremor unless she had to fill something delicate, and even then she made a joke first so no one else could.

“If I build from this cooler,” Paloma said, “I lose the contract. I lose the apartment. The work visa follows it down. I'm not saying that to be dramatic. I'm saying it because I'd like us all to have the same arithmetic before anybody nods.”

Ivo gave a short laugh that was not really a laugh. “That's kind of you. My arithmetic is already shit. End of summer review, pension vesting, six thousand eight hundred in arrears looking at me like a loaded socket. You want the romantic version of this, ask a priest.”

“I'm not asking for romance,” Marisol said.

“Good.”

She had known his numbers since the cafeteria night, but hearing them out loud in the clinic turned them into an invoice laid across stainless steel. The whole corridor worked that way. The company translated hunger into deduction, translated fear into retention, translated every private trouble into a lever that could be pulled from an office. The loose shirt button sat beside the dispatch sheet, small and white as a decimal point.

Paloma shut the cooler and latched it again. “There's another problem.”

“There's always another problem,” Ivo said.

“This one has teeth. A stop isn't enough. A quarantine isn't enough. I can force review language into the room, maybe. I can even force a delay if the discrepancy looks real enough. But if the doors stay closed and the evidence stays in our chain, Teresa can bury the story under a better one. She'll call it a false alarm, a sample mismatch, contamination by a tired contractor. Then the first rows get hauled to the vestibule, swabbed, bleached, photographed, and shoved back into the same heat while compliance writes corrective action over the top of it.” Paloma touched her own sternum with two fingers, a brief, contemptuous tap. “Me. I become the explanation and the explanation becomes the cleanup.”

Marisol looked back down at the headings. Her own block letters had started to sweat grease onto the page. CAN SPIN. CAN BUY TIME. CANNOT CONTAIN. Even the last column could still be hosed down if it happened behind the wrong door or without the right witness. A brake fault got renumbered. Sewage got mopped. A medic got blamed. She dragged the side of her hand across the page and left a gray smear through CANNOT CONTAIN.

“What about a death?” Ivo asked quietly.

Paloma closed her eyes for one beat. “A death inside custody is reportable. It's not automatically public.”

Marisol did not write that one down. She did not need the paper to remember it. She set the grease pencil beside PUBLIC WITNESS, and the wax left a dark half-moon on the restraint form.

Ivo took up the needle again but did not sew. He rolled the thread between his fingers and watched Marisol instead. “Say the part you're circling.”

She knew which part he meant too. “We need a stop that doesn't belong to them.”

“No.”

She looked up.

“We need a stop,” he said, “and then we need the thing under the stop to be visible. Otherwise you're making work for mechanics and cleaners and nobody else. I can give you bad numbers on a brake test at the right platform. She can give you medical language. You can give them a mess they can't hose away in thirty seconds. If the only story anyone sees is that something broke, Teresa still wins.”

Paloma's mouth tightened. “He's right.”
